HEADLINE: Computer Games Venture Into the World of Gun, Bow and Big Game

BYLINE: By STEVE LOHR 

 BODY:
The business mastermind behind one of the hot trends in computing today is not Bill Gates or Michael Dell, and not some Internet whiz kid from Silicon Valley or Seattle, but an anonymous middle manager from Bentonville, Ark.
Yet Robert Westmoreland of Wal-Mart Stores Inc., has been the driving force behind the phenomenal success of computer hunting games, led by Deer Hunter and Cabela's Big Game Hunter. It was Mr. Westmoreland who, by all accounts, prodded the software industry to make hunting games and then promoted them heavily at Wal-Mart, the nation's largest retailer. 
 "He's the man," observed Robert Kotick, chairman of Activision Inc., whose Head Games unit created two best-selling Cabela games.
The hunting game phenomenon is a sure sign that personal computing is moving to the masses. These digital diversions are priced below $20, less than half the cost of classic personal computer games like Doom, Quake or Starcraft.
The hunting games are also an early wave of what industry analysts call casual games, easier to play and more mundane in appearance than the special-effects-on-a-screen adventures preferred by what the industry considers hard-core gamers -- male techies in the 16-to-35 age range. Computer Gaming World, the bible of computer game purists, once criticized Deer Hunter as "boring" and "repetitive" with "lame" graphics.
The outdoors games -- whose audience, though still mainly male, is often older and less technophile than gamers have been in the past -- took off last year, as computers priced below $1,000 became commonplace and the proportion of American households with computers rose to 50 percent.
Five of the 20 top-selling computer games last year were low-cost hunting titles, according to PC Data, a research firm. And so far this year, the latest versions of Deer Hunter and Cabela's Big Game Hunter are high on the best-seller lists -- and more sequels are in the works. Even a spoof of hunting games, Deer Avenger, in which deer stalk people, has recently entered the top 10 list.
The hunting games have prompted an elitist backlash, derided as "red neck," "Bubba" or "trailer park" software. Yet others see the hunting-game phenomenon as simply a sign of the times.
"The computer is a personal medium, and as it is more widely available and affordable its use will reflect the diversity of society," said Paul Saffo, a director of the Institute for the Future, a research group in Menlo Park, Calif. "Bubba software is one aspect of that diversity."
And, of course, commercial success tends to breed respect, even if grudgingly. At the start of this year, Jeff Green, a columnist for Computer Gaming World, conceded that the industry first made fun of Deer Hunter, then scrambled to copy it. "You don't have to admire the game itself," he wrote, "but you've got to love anything that makes the entire gaming industry, press included, look stupid."
Certainly, Mr. Westmoreland, Wal-Mart's computer software buyer, faced considerable skepticism a few years ago when he first suggested the idea of inexpensive games for a mass market of newcomers to personal computers. Mr. Kotick of Activision recalled a meeting with Mr. Westmoreland in a Las Vegas hotel room during the Comdex computer trade show in the fall of 1996.
At the time, Mr. Westmoreland's concept was sharply at odds with the prevailing thinking and culture of the PC game business. The major game companies regarded video games, running on the console technology of Sony, Sega and Nintendo, as the mass-market channel.
Personal computers, by contrast, were viewed as the preferred technology for hard-core gamers. PC games, with multimillion-dollar production budgets and priced in the $40 to $50 range, strove for Hollywood-style special effects best displayed on turbocharged, cutting-edge PC's.
So at that 1996 meeting, Mr. Kotick listened in disbelief to Mr. Westmoreland's suggestion. "He described his grand vision of under-$20 software, and I looked at him as if he had three heads," Mr. Kotick recalled. "I almost walked out of the room on him. But he was absolutely right."
By last June, Mr. Kotick was ready to reverse field and plunge into the market for low-cost PC games, as Activision purchased Head Games Publishing of Minneapolis, producer of Cabela's Big Game Hunter.
But Mr. Westmoreland had found a more receptive audience when he met in Minneapolis with executives of the Wizardworks unit of GT Interactive Software in April 1997. Seeing the large sporting goods sections in Wal-Mart and the falling price of computers, Mr. Westmoreland began to think that an inexpensive hunting game for PC's could be a winner.
"He said, 'You make me a deer-hunting title, and I'll get behind it,' " recalled Paul Rinde, a senior vice president of Wizardworks.
Wizardworks, based in the Midwest, was perhaps less influenced than game developers elsewhere by the industry penchant for Hollywood-style productions and cutting-edge computing. Like most software publishers, Wizardworks taps a network of contractors.
In May 1997, Mr. Rinde visited Anthony Campiti, president of Sunstorm Interactive Inc. in Indianapolis. Sunstorm was a six-person software developer working on PC versions of simple games like pool and darts, and when Mr. Rinde suggested a deer hunting game, Mr. Campiti had his doubts.
"I asked," Mr. Campiti recalled, " 'How in the world can you make a game about sitting in the woods for three hours, doing nothing?' "
But Wizardworks was offering Sunstorm a job it needed. So Mr. Campiti, not a hunter, started researching the subject. He talked to hunters, read hunting magazines and gained respect for the sport. "I learned there is a lot of skill involved in hunting," he said.
Mr. Campiti ended up designing a simple hunting-simulation game in which the virtual hunters can use decoy calls, cover their scent and choose their weapons (rifle, shotgun or bow). It was called Deer Hunter, and it proved a stunning success.
"In hindsight, everyone is a genius," Mr. Westmoreland said. "Did the sales of Deer Hunter surprise me? Of course. But what did not surprise me was that there was a big market for casual gaming software, priced below $20, waiting to be tapped."
Mr. Rinde agrees that the timing is right.
"The growing installed base of PC's and expanding demographics have created the casual gaming market -- the hunting games are just the beginning," Mr. Rinde observed. "This is PC game software for the rest of us. People who don't care about being transported to the year 3000 and battling aliens on the planet Zorak."
"The casual game market," Mr. Rinde added, "is for people who want to do much the same things on the computer -- hunt, fish, play cards, whatever -- that they do in real life."
The other lesson of the hunting game phenomenon is that as software becomes more a mass-market product, the mass-market retailers -- more than specialized computer stores -- may become the vital channel of distribution. In short, Mr. Rinde said, "Wal-Mart can make a title, if they get behind it."
And the Bubba market is not without its ironists. Wal-Mart shoppers have also helped make the satirical "Deer Avenger" game a best seller. "There's a good-sized crossover market of Wal-Mart customers who do have a sense of humor," said Brian McCann, the game's creator and a writer on the television show "Late Night with Conan O'Brien."
But beyond mockery, Mr. McCann contends that his game is simply more compelling to play, with its "kill scenes" in which a deer can use everything from slingshots to bazookas to zap the virtual humans. "It's far more rewarding than watching a deer slump over sadly," he said.
The business model in the casual game market is a world apart from the glitzy, fantasy war-and-strategy games. Production schedules tend to be four months instead of two years, and production budgets generally range from $150,000 to $200,000 instead of $2 million to $4 million.
The low-cost economics are opening the door in the PC game market for the first time in years to tiny start-ups with bootstrap financing. One such fledgling venture is Rise Software in Rogers, Ark. Its assets so far are story boards for a few games and hopes of success.
Its founder and chief executive is a 32-year-old entrepreneur who started puttering with PC's as a 12-year-old and who may have some useful experience and acquaintances in the industry. His name: Robert Westmoreland, whose last day at Wal-Mart will be this Friday.
"I'm going down the road myself, trying to capitalize on this emerging market," he said.
He would not discuss his product ideas, except to say they will not be hunting games.

HEADLINE: CONFLICT IN THE BALKANS: THE RUSSIANS;
A Team of Moscow Mediators Arrives in Yugoslavia With a Plan for Peace

BYLINE: By BLAINE HARDEN 

DATELINE: BUDAPEST, March 28

 BODY:
A Russian delegation met here today with the United States special envoy, Richard C. Holbrooke, before attempting to travel by road to neighboring Yugoslavia to present a peace plan to President Slobodan Milosevic.
The three prominent liberal politicans from Moscow warned that NATO's bombing of Yugoslavia had severely damaged Russia's relationship with the United States. 
 The group said its mission had been approved by the Russian President, Boris N. Yeltsin, and that its primary goal was to stop NATO air attacks against Yugoslavia. They also said the Clinton Administration made a serious miscalculation by expecting that bombs would bring Mr. Milosevic to the negotiating table and by failing to understand how the bombing would inflame anti-American feelings in Russia.
"We think the continuation of the fighting and bombing in Yugoslavia is extremely dangerous for the world," said Yegor Gaidar, a member of the delegation and the Acting Prime Minister in 1992 after he helped set in motion the shock therapy reforms of the Russian economy. "If the situation continues in the direction it is going now, we are confronted with a serious danger of the cold war re-emerging."
Mr. Gaidar and the other members of the delegation, Boris Fyodorov, a former Finance Minister, and Boris Nemtsov, a former Deputy Prime Minister, said that they had several specific peace proposals to present to Mr. Milosevic, but that they would not disclose them until after they met with the Yugoslav leader. The Russian group was reported to have reached the Yugoslav capital this evening.
Mr. Milosevic's response to previous mediation efforts suggested that the Russians' chances for affecting the course of the war were slim.
On Saturday, Mr. Milosevic met with a Ukrainian delegation led by Foreign Minister Boris Barasyuk which, like the Russians, had traveled to Belgrade in an effort to mediate an end to the conflict. There was no indication that they had met with any success.
In Belgrade, an official at the Yugoslav Foreign Ministry said that efforts were being made to set up a meeting for the Russians, but that he had no other details.
After meeting here with the Russians, Mr. Holbrooke, who is in Budapest on private business, said that while he wished the peace delegation well, he wanted to make clear that the Moscow group would not be carrying a message from the United States to Yugoslavia.
"We reviewed the American position," said Mr. Holbrooke, who failed last week in talks with Mr. Milosevic aimed at persuading him to accept a plan for peace in Kosovo. "There is no change in that position in the sense there is a clear disagreement between the Russian Government and NATO, but that does not mean we cannot still continue close contacts."
A senior Western diplomat said after the meeting that the Russian delegation was extremely unlikely to make any headway with Mr. Milosevic and that its presence in Belgrade would have no effect on NATO's bombing campaign. But the diplomat said the mission represented "a genuine desire" on the part of Western-minded reformers in Russia to limit, and, if possible, reverse a marked deterioration in American-Russian relations since the bombing began last Wednesday.
Members of some NATO Governments suggested today that the Russian Government itself could be useful in negotiating with Mr. Milosevic to stop armed attacks by Serbian forces in Kosovo against ethnic Albanians and in bringing an end to the bombings.
President Jacques Chirac of France has urged the Russian Prime Minister, Yevgeny Primakov, to go to Belgrade to ask Mr. Milosevic to end the crisis, French officials said today.
Prime Minister Bulent Ecevit of Turkey called on President Yeltsin to help find a political solution to the conflict. "I think an intervention by Mr. Yeltsin to persuade Serbia to a more reasonable position would be most useful," he said.
The Dutch Foreign Minister, Jozias van Aartsen, also said the Russians had a key role to play in resolving the conflict over Kosovo.
In an interview here, Mr. Nemtsov said the bombing of Serbia was an "enormous miscalculation" that had infuriated an overwhelming majority of Russians.
Mr. Nemtsov said the bombing, if it continues, could provoke a powerful anti-American political movement that would severely weaken the influence of pro-Western reformers in Russia. He made his remarks before hearing word of shots being fired today in Moscow at the American Embassy.
"I know Milosevic," Mr. Nemtsov said. "I know that bombing is not going to make him change his mind. It has made him a hero to the Serbian people. The United States made a big mistake."

HEADLINE: PUBLIC LIVES;
Balkan Policy, as Seen From a Former Insider-Dissenter

BYLINE: By FRANCIS X. CLINES 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON

 BODY:
THE first NATO missiles and bombs were only a few hours from striking Serbian targets last week when James R. Hooper, once the State Department's senior dissenter on Balkan policy, sipped his coffee a few blocks from the White House and talked of ground troops likely to follow.
"We now are going to have to consider a NATO protection force on the ground to go in on very short notice if the bloodletting escalates suddenly," Mr. Hooper said, characteristically speaking the politically unspeakable on the Balkans without apology. 
 It has been two years since Mr. Hooper, the very model of an earnest Foreign Service careerist, quit the Government in frustration after 26 years of toiling at diplomacy. He joined a handful of pioneer State Department dissenters who left across two Administrations protesting Balkan policy. It was not the easiest of choices.
"I had wanted to be in the Foreign Service since I was a teen-ager and read 'The Ugly American,' " Mr. Hooper said of the novel about intrigues and frustrations of laboring abroad. "It got me mad and interested in foreign affairs."
Now he plays the role of articulate, impassioned gadfly as executive director of the Balkan Action Council, one of this city's countless independent pressure groups that nip at the heels of Government.
"It's as if there's a destiny," he said of his sudden decision to retire from the Foreign Service to promote his views from the outside. "I wanted to follow my dream, and now here's the Kosovo issue front and center."
Mr. Hooper, who was once the State Department deputy director of Eastern Europe in charge of the Balkans, can sympathize with the American public in its bewilderment over the region.
"To be honest, I didn't know a damn thing about it," he said, referring to his taking on the Balkans portfolio a decade ago after years of specializing in the Middle East.
He soon came to conclude that Serbian ultranationalism was a threat to European order and to American leadership rooted dearly in the Allies' anti-Fascist victory in World War II.
In the midst of the modern Balkan violence, Mr. Hooper's father, a veteran of World War II, told his son he had finished that war in Czechoslovakia yet only lately understood what was at stake more than 50 years ago. The son tries to stay mindful of his father's lesson.
"The United States has to be prepared to use force abroad to protect democratic values and our interests," Mr. Hooper said.
"I think we're still living off the patrimony of the policies of the Truman Administration," he said of such bulwark measures as the Berlin airlift and NATO itself.
"Truman's the politician I admire most," he said, finding President Clinton lacking the statesman's instinct of Harry S. Truman. "You get that for being willing to step out into the dark to take risks, to take responsibility for policies when you don't know the outcome."
Studying the confrontation as an expert in the American home front's considerable confusion, Mr. Hooper discusses the complex problem in terms of two dominant personalities: the Yugoslav President, Slobodan Milosevic, and Mr. Clinton.
Mr. Hooper finds the authoritarian Serbian leader cunningly using force to test the American leader, a poll-driven consensus politician who turned late -- not too late, Mr. Hooper hopes -- to forceful intervention and to an overdue lesson for the public about global leadership.
"Now the President has to restrain himself from stopping the NATO attacks too early," said Mr. Hooper, who was dubious about Mr. Clinton's latest burst of tough talk about Mr. Milosevic until the first NATO missiles hit Serbian targets.
"To pause would be projected as weakness toward Milosevic," he warned, estimating that if Mr. Clinton remained resolved and committed ground troops, if necessary, the outcome in Kosovo could prove as successful as intervention in Bosnia.
Mr. Hooper graduated from the School of International Service at American University and received a master's degree in Middle Eastern studies from Columbia University. His reading ranges from George F. Kennan, the former Ambassador to Moscow and Sovietologist, to escapist books on mountain climbing.
"You've got to get away from all this in order to survive," he said of his preoccupation with the Balkans.
At the age of 52, Mr. Hooper, a native of Grand Rapids, Mich., misses the friends and economic security of the Foreign Service, but not at all the careerist pressures that can make change from within a difficult task at any of this city's institutions of power.
ASKED about role models, he talks simply of his mother's lesson in persistence in gaining a college degree late in life when she was going blind. He talks of steady guidance from his wife and two grown children.
"It seems to me the fun in life is to try and get as far as you can by being yourself and sticking to your principles," he said.
"I was always conscious that we were bureaucrats back in Washington trying to change the policy," he said of his State Department days in wrestling with the Balkans.
"I would drive home every night, and the greatest threat I faced was the traffic, while there were people out there who were doing the real fighting and dying," he said as he tracked the NATO bombs and missiles falling on the forces of Mr. Milosevic.

HEADLINE: Questioning Three Gorges Dam

 BODY:
In China, where even a slight relaxation in suppression of debate can indicate a softening of official policy, it is encouraging that some publications have cast an increasingly critical eye on the Three Gorges Dam being built on the Yangtze River. The official line is that there is no turning back on a project that will destroy one of the world's great scenic areas, inundate hundreds of archeological sites and force 1.3 million people to resettle elsewhere when the dam's 400-mile reservoir floods towns and farmlands. But the insoluble social, environmental and technical problems that have plagued the project cannot be wished away, and they may now be getting some consideration.
The dam has been a matter of internal disagreement within the Communist Party for some time. As far back as 1956, a vice minister of electric power, Li Rui, produced a report arguing for smaller dams on tributaries of the Yangtze rather than a 600-foot behemoth at the Three Gorges. In 1992, when the final vote to approve the Three Gorges project was taken in the National People's Congress, a third of the delegates abstained or voted against it, even though the dam was championed by Li Peng, who was then Prime Minister. But all public debate on the project has been banned since the Tiananmen demonstrations in 1989. 
 So it is noteworthy that the Chinese journal Strategy and Management, a publication with some links to the Government, printed an article by a scholar under a pseudonym detailing the failure of resettlement efforts so far, and the extreme problems of relocating hundreds of thousands of people into steep hillsides that are barely habitable. In February, People's Daily, the party-controlled newspaper, ran articles on engineering issues and problems with excavating cultural relics that would be destroyed in the flood zone. Other newspapers have reported on official corruption connected to the project. Chinese media reports have also noted that existing flood-control systems and older dams are neglected and in danger of collapse as attention is diverted to new projects like Three Gorges.
Last December, Prime Minister Zhu Rongji, who is considered neutral on the project, raised concerns about the project's safety and suggested it may be necessary to bring in international experts to monitor the engineering. Li Peng, who now heads the National People's Congress, is expected to fight any retreat on the project. But the thaw in repression of criticism suggests that altering and even halting the project may yet be possible.
That is why it is crucial for American financial institutions to refrain from underwriting bonds for Chinese entities, like the State Development Bank, that finance construction of the dam. China cannot finance the dam, which is expected to cost well over $25 billion, without foreign capital. The United States Export-Import Bank and the World Bank have refused to support the project because of its disastrous environmental and social consequences.
The world is beginning to change its views on large dams. The World Bank has sponsored creation of the independent World Commission on Dams to assess the effectiveness of such projects and alternatives, with a report due next year. Major dams have been stopped in mid-construction in Malaysia, India and Eastern Europe when the governments found that the benefits were uncertain and the costs enormous.
The Three Gorges Dam is a throwback to failed development strategies of decades past. This is an important moment to show China's leaders that the international community wants no part of this destructive, gargantuan project.

HEADLINE: Super-Fast Computer Virus Heads Into the Workweek

BYLINE: By MATT RICHTEL 

DATELINE: PALO ALTO, Calif., March 28

 BODY:
With tens of thousands of computers thought to be affected already, corporations and network security professionals said today that they were bracing for the continued spread of the "Melissa" computer virus with the beginning of the workweek on Monday.
Over the weekend, computer security firms said they had received an unusually large number of calls for assistance from companies affected on Friday and Saturday. Melissa, which is carried by E-mail, appears to be one of the fastest replicating viruses ever detected. It automatically tries to mail itself from each recipient's machine to 50 other E-mail addresses, computer security experts said. 
 "This message may be sitting in a lot of mailboxes, dormant," said Jeff Carpenter, the incident-response team leader from the Computer Emergency Response Team, an emergency security service at Carnegie Mellon University financed by the Department of Defense and now known simply as CERT. "When people go to work and open it up, a large number of organizations could be negatively affected."
Mr. Carpenter said the response team had received 50 to 60 telephone calls on Friday and Saturday from organizations. Most of their mail servers were incapacitated by an overwhelming volume of mail.
So far, it appears that the biggest threat from the virus is that it overwhelms networks because it reproduces so rapidly but it is not expected to otherwise attack the machines it infects. Also, the infection appears to become dormant after once using a recipient's computer as a platform to re-send itself.
As the virus continued to spread over the weekend, a debate emerged over whether it was intended as a malicious attack or was actually an Internet marketing ploy that spun out of control. The E-mail message that carries the virus includes an attachment that lists several dozen Web sites with pornographic content.
Computer security experts speculated that the virus program might have been written as an effort to promote the pornographic sites by automatically spreading them across the Internet in a way that made each message appear to have come from an acquaintance of its recipient.
Eugene H. Spafford, director of the Center of Education and Research in Information Insurance and Security at Purdue University, said Melissa could be a malignant form of "spam," as unsolicited advertising by E-mail is known.
When a user opens the file labeled list.doc attached to the E-mail, it executes a Microsoft Word macro, a sort of rudimentary program that searches the recipient's hard drive for an address list produced by one of two Microsoft E-mail programs, Outlook or Outlook Express. It then copies the message to the first 50 names in the address book, giving it the appearance of having been sent by someone known to the recipient.
"This person may have an economic incentive," Mr. Spafford said of the virus's creator. "He may be trying to propagate his list far and wide."
If, on the other hand, the program is actually intended to create havoc in Internet mail servers, Mr. Spafford and other security experts said it could have been much worse. The virus could have been written -- and could yet be modified -- to carry a far more dangerous payload, like a program that deletes data.
"It's entirely possible that within hours to months, we will see something worse" Mr. Spafford said.
Network security experts continued over the weekend to search for the origin of Melissa. One lead that several said they were exploring involved a posting made to a sex-related Internet discussion group early on Friday morning, just before the virus started spreading.
The posting came from an address on America Online that included the same list of pornography sites carried by the virus. In at least one other instance, in December 1997, a person using the same E-mail address posted lists of pornographic Web sites and passwords that also contained viruses.
"The virus hit Friday afternoon, and this message was posted at 7 A.M.," said Richard M. Smith, president of Phar Lap Software, in Cambridge, Mass. "One could assume that this message was the first time the virus existed."
Eric Allman, co-founder of Sendmail, the software company in Emeryville, Calif., which makes the Internet's most popular post-office program for directing E-mail, said, "Word macros are probably more powerful than they ought to be, but you do get something for that power."
Since the virus appears to affect only computers that are loaded with both Word and either Outlook or Outlook Express, it seems designed to take advantage of three of the most popular programs on personal computers.
Security experts said that computers loaded with Word and one of the two Microsoft E-mail programs are vulnerable even if the default E-mail program on the computer is a competing product like the Netscape Communications Corporation's Navigator mail or Qualcomm's Eudora.
Generally, Mr. Allman said, consumers need to be more aware of what information they are accepting on their hard drives and other storage devices when they download an E-mail attachment. A setting within Microsoft Word cautions users when they are about to open an unknown document that contains a macro and asks if they would like to continue.
One lasting impact from the attack of the virus is that it appears to alter the user's settings within Word so that the program no longer warns users if they are about to open a file with an unknown macro, Mr. Spafford said. The virus "turns the protections down on the machine to the lowest setting," he said.
Users can tell if they have been infected by the virus by checking the Windows registry, which is a database that contains a list of programs downloaded onto the computer.
Among the corporations infected by the virus on Friday were Charles Schwab & Company, Lucent and the Intel Corporation. An Internet posting from the Department of Energy's Computer Incident Advisory Capability noted that the virus is "spreading widely within and without the D.O.E. complex."

HEADLINE: CONFLICT IN THE BALKANS: IN BELGRADE -- NATO Planes Step Up Attacks on Serb Troops;
Support for Homeland Up as Sirens Wail and News Is Censored

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Serbia, March 28

 BODY:
Slobodan Milosevic, defiant as ever and proud of his nation's resistance to NATO's air attacks, staged an eerie celebration today -- a rock concert in the center of Belgrade to show solidarity with the soldiers facing down the allied assaults.
Few of the thousands of Serbs who attended were unaware that this was also the 10th anniversary of the day when Mr. Milosevic stripped broad autonomy from Kosovo, just after his security forces had killed some 30 ethnic Albanian protesters in Pristina, Kosovo's capital. 
 Today, the Serbian security forces are sweeping through Kosovo, driving tens of thousands of ethnic Albanians into panicked flight and provoking what Western officials described as the worst refugee crisis that Europe has seen in 50 years.
In Belgrade, given strict censorship rules and the patriotic fever of wartime, few Serbs seemed to know much about events in Kosovo, or the Western reaction. They poured their scorn on the Americans, rejoicing in what Serbian television persistently called the shooting down of an F-117A stealth jet, and rallied around their nation -- and, by extension, its leader, Mr. Milosevic -- in a way not seen here in years.
The rock concert organized to celebrate the holiday known as "the day of Serbia's statehood" bore an ironic resemblance to similar concerts held by anti-Milosevic demonstrators during the mass pro-democracy demonstrations of 1996-97 in Belgrade.
The same bands played today to some of the same people, by their own account, symbolizing the unifying effect that air strikes have had on the general population. "Forget who is supporting whom, this is no time for divisions," said one of the leading singers at the rally, Dejan Cukic.
Many of the thousands at the afternoon rally -- called "Songs Keep Us Alive and United" -- wore small placards on their chests displaying a black target. One placard referred to the downing of the stealth aircraft, and read: "F-117A. Sorry, we didn't know that you are invisible." Another redefined NATO as "New American Terrorist Organization," while others compared President Clinton to Hitler, whose air force bombed Belgrade in 1941.
During much of the rally, Belgrade was under an air raid alert. The first bombs fell today at about 4 A.M., and then again at 8 A.M., with their detonations preceding any air raid sirens. Air raid warnings continued to howl after dark. Tonight, however, in contrast to Friday and Saturday night, when the city was plunged into darkness, most of Belgrade's street lights stayed on.
Mr. Milosevic held a meeting of his Cabinet and military leaders and issued a statement praising the army and the citizenry. The two were linked "by strong unity, a high patriotic conscience and determination to endure in the just struggle against the criminal aggressors," the statement said. "Our citizens are demonstrating high personal responsibility and a high degree of mutual solidarity."
There was no reference to increasingly blunt Western charges that Mr. Milosevic's armed forces and police units are engaged in widespread atrocities in Kosovo.
Western reporters with no access to Kosovo were largely reduced to interviewing the ethnic Albanian refugees pouring across its borders, a situation reminiscent of Cambodia in the mid-1970s, when the victorious Khmer Rouge emptied the cities and reporters had to piece together the scale of atrocities from the refugees who straggled into Thailand.
On Serbian state television's evening news, the Serbian Minister for Refugees, Bratislava Morina, whose late husband was a Kosovo Albanian Communist, suggested that ethnic Albanians were fleeing NATO bombs, not the Serbian military. "If NATO wants these people to return to their homes, it should stop this aggression immediately," she said.
Still, the official Tanjug news agency did announce that NATO planes attacked before and after sunrise, targeting the air force base in the Belgrade suburb of Batajnica for the fourth consecutive day. Two other explosions cracked windows at Belgrade's main civilian airport, Surcin, 15 miles from the city center. Other missiles fell near Cacak, the site of a huge military factory in central Serbia, Tanjug said. In Kosovo, four bombs hit an army barracks outside Prizren, the agency said. There was no report of any casualties. To date, the Yugoslav media has talked of dozens killed in the air raids.
At the rally, words of general patriotism came from noted performers like Mr. Cukic and Momcilo Bagajic, known as Bajaga, who said, "We should defend ourselves." Mr. Bagajic is known not to be a rabid nationalist, and he has played in other parts of the former Yugoslavia, like Slovenia.
He wrote a hugely popular and moving song about the Yugoslav civil wars, largely initiated by Mr. Milosevic, that says, "My friends are pearls scattered all over the world." Ironically, the Government is now playing the song often on state television as a backdrop to patriotic pictures of the Yugoslav armed forces.
One member of the Children of Bad Musicians, a popular group, told the crowd, "I used to play sax, but since that sex maniac plays it, I don't touch it anymore."
Olga, a 43-year-old economist wearing a fur vest and Italian shoes, said she never went to the air raid shelters because she could not bring her English setter, Loli. She is no fan of Mr. Milosevic, she said, but war is no time for divisions. "I was in all the demonstrations for democracy," she said, "but now I have to be in favor of defending my homeland."
In an interview, Zoran Djindjic, the leader of the Democratic Party and an organizer of the pro-democracy demonstrations, noted the irony. "The bombs have marginalized any dissenters here, and there's little we democrats can do to affect the situation," he said. "The same is also true of those democrats and moderates among the ethnic Albanians," he noted.
Washington has spent more on one day's bombs than it ever spent helping the democracy movement in Yugoslavia, Mr. Djindjic said bitterly. "Milosevic could be far more easily eliminated through the development of democracy here than through air strikes," he said. "Every crisis strengthens him. The only players that matter now are Milosevic, the Kosovo Liberation Army and the Americans."
The consequences of the air strikes are "this radicalized and pauperized Serbia and a destabilized Montenegro," whose independence Washington wants to encourage under President Milo Djukanovic, Mr. Djindjic said. Yugoslavia consists of Serbia and Montenegro, and Mr. Djukanovic is a former protege of Mr. Milosevic who in the last year has turned against the Yugoslav President. "Now Djukanovic is compromised by the bombs, and there is more anti-American feeling in the entire region," Mr. Djindjic said.
There are Yugoslav military and police depots and headquarters in Montenegro, and NATO has bombed them freely, despite a statement by Mr. Djukanovic that Montenegro would stay neutral in any fight between NATO and Serbia.
Mr. Djindjic and Mr. Djukanovic are close, and Mr. Djukanovic told him the day the bombs started dropping that no one in the American Government ever telephoned to explain the situation or to warn him, Mr. Djindjic said. "Ordinary people in Montenegro will now say that Djukanovic based his entire policy on America, but we're getting bombed just as badly as Milosevic," Mr. Djindjic said. "This will make it much more difficult for Montenegro to secede."
The evening news gave a hint of a Russian initiative to solve this crisis, announcing that President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia and Mr. Milosevic had exchanged letters. But the Yugoslav Ambassador to Moscow, Mr. Milosevic's brother, Borislav, said he could not confirm that the two Presidents would meet, and there was no indication that a Russian delegation, which arrived tonight and includes former Prime Minister Yegor Gaidar, would get anywhere with its own peace initiative. France said it was pressing the Russian Prime Minister, Yevgeny M. Primakov, to seek a diplomatic solution. Russia is a traditional backer of Serbia.
For the first time today, the Yugoslav Government organized a junket for some 70 foreign journalists here -- dominated by non-NATO countries like Japan -- to the site where the F-117A crashed, in Budjanovci, 30 miles northwest of Belgrade. Reporters, in two buses, were only allowed to see the remains of a wing while local villagers danced with evident joy at this small triumph of their hugely outgunned military.
A young boy wearing a knitted cap with the logo of the Chicago Bulls stood on the American Air Force star painted on the wing, while the assembled cameramen scrambled for angles. The boy had a toy pistol in one hand while he tried to make the Serbian victory sign with the other.
The evening news said that, in addition to the wartime censorship that now applies to media, new wartime banking rules were established to fund the military, and that profiteering would be punished severely. In the newspaper Glas Javnosti today, there was a story about the arrest of the first "war profiteers" in the city of Nis, a man and his son who owned a gas station and were hoarding 3,171 liters of gasoline, 5,300 liters of diesel fuel and 1,000 liters of oil. Gasoline is nearly unavailable now except on the black market, where the price has now reached almost 10 German marks a liter, many times its price a week ago.
On the back page, however, there was a story about the opening of the yearly automobile show at the Belgrade Fair, featuring a photograph of the new Volkswagen Beetle.
Also today, Dragoslav Rancic, a senior contributing editor of the weekly magazine Nin, and his editor, Stevan Niksic, were released from police custody. They were arrested early on Saturday for offending censorship regulations with an article about Mr. Milosevic's negotiating style. Friends said they had not been beaten by the police.
No Belgrade newspaper published a word about their arrest, a sharp contrast to the solidarity local journalists displayed when Veran Matic, the chief editor of the banned radio station B92, was detained for eight hours last Wednesday. But on Wednesday night, the first NATO bombs fell on Belgrade, and, as one local journalist said bitterly, "media solidarity is evidently another casualty."

HEADLINE: Council Offers Budget Plan, With Tax Cuts

BYLINE: By DAVID M. HERSZENHORN 

 BODY:
Asserting that the Giuliani administration has underestimated city revenues for the next 15 months by more than $1 billion, the Speaker of the City Council, Peter F. Vallone, proposed a broad package of tax cuts yesterday worth about $700 million. The contrasting revenue projection and the proposed tax cuts represent the Council's starting point in negotiating the next budget.
The unveiling of the City Council's revenue projections and Mr. Vallone's tax plan, which aides said was intended primarily to aid the working poor, first-time home buyers and some businesses, was offered as a prelude to the Council's full response, expected today, to the $35.4 billion spending plan for next year that Mayor Rudolph W. Giuliani presented in January. 
 In the full response, which includes specific spending proposals for individual agencies, the Council apparently has rejected a proposal by Mr. Giuliani to accelerate the start of a Police Academy class that would have increased the uniformed police force by more than 1,500 officers on Jan. 1, 2000, six months earlier than originally planned. The plan would cost $32 million.
Mr. Vallone raised concerns about the Mayor's proposal last month when he released a Council report showing that while the total number of uniformed police officers increased over the last two years, the number of officers assigned to neighborhood precincts declined as more officers were assigned to narcotics task forces and other special details.
With three months of budget negotiations still ahead, the plan could very well be restored in the final budget, but the Council's initial rejection of it is certain to raise the ire of Mr. Giuliani, who has made crime fighting the hallmark of his tenure.
Deputy Mayor Joseph J. Lhota criticized the Council's position. "It would be unfortunate if Speaker Vallone wanted to decelerate the class at a time when shooting incidences are going up," he said. "It would seem like a complete reversal of the role he played in Safe Streets, Safe Cities, when police head count was increased to deal with public safety."
In releasing its revenue projections, the Council suggested that Mr. Giuliani and his budget team had underestimated city revenues by nearly $1.01 billion. Under the City Charter, only the Mayor has the authority to determine the city's revenue stream, and Mr. Giuliani has prided himself in making conservative estimates that in recent years have led to generous surpluses.
The early disagreement over revenue projections, with the Legislature criticizing the Mayor for underestimates, is a typical opening of the budget negotiations. Last year, the Council said the Mayor had underestimated by about $917 million, and the year before that by about $516 million. Such underestimates often allow for spending to be increased later, or for a surplus to be saved for balancing future budgets.
One major difference between the administration and Council projections is estimates of city revenue from Wall Street. While the Mayor has estimated about $5 billion in Wall Street revenues, the Council has suggested that a more realistic figure is between $10 million and $12 million. There were also differences in projections of personal income tax and other business taxes.
"You have to understand, that the executive has a problem," Mr. Vallone said before marching in the Greek Independence Day Parade yesterday. "They can't really put all of their revenue in because of labor negotiations. That's like the great dark, deep secret, but it exists. On the other hand the Legislature has an obligation to be accurate. And we are being accurate."
After marching in the same parade, Mr. Giuliani offered a passionate defense of his conservative approach to budget-making, saying that his caution offered the city protection against a downturn in the stock market or some other unexpected souring of the economy. In his spending plan, the Mayor identified more than $370 million in tax cuts, including some mirrored by the Council.
The Mayor has also formed a task force to consider an additional $100 million in as yet unidentified cuts. "I think our budget practices are sounder, much more solid, much more fiscally prudent than the city has had in the last 30 years," the Mayor said, pointing to increases in the city's bond ratings as evidence of fiscal success.
"I don't know, Peter Vallone doesn't know and none of us know when Wall Street will not be performing the way it is performing now," Mr. Giuliani said. "So, I estimate in out years, modest Wall Street performance. The good news is, if Wall Street does better, then we can retire more of our debt, we can reduce more of the gap and we can reduce taxes even more."
With the tax package proposed yesterday, Mr. Vallone and his aides said they believed that the city would be better served by taking more direct advantage of the nation's strong economy in recent years and passing lasting tax cuts. The proposed cuts include creating a city earned income tax credit, similar to those available to the poorest taxpayers at state and Federal levels.
According to Council estimates, a married couple earning $20,000 would save $844 a year in taxes, including a $600 tax credit for child care and an earned income tax credit of $181. A couple with two children earning $50,000 who buy a single-family home for the first time would save $4,382 including a $2,370 exemption from the mortgage recording tax and $1,421 from the real property transfer tax.
Other proposals include the phasing out of the commercial rent tax, which has already been curtailed and is now paid only by businesses south of 96th Street in Manhattan that pay more than $100,000 a year in rent. The Council has also proposed eliminating sales tax on clothing costing less than $110, a move supported by Mr. Giuliani and already approved by the State Legislature. The package also includes incentives for first-time home buyers, including a new tax-free savings plan.
At least nine of the proposals would also require the approval of the State Legislature. Mr. Vallone said the tax cuts, which would increase to $1.2 billion by fiscal year 2002, would have broad appeal. "We have a menu of tax reductions which affect everyone, which is really, I think, something that I have always wanted to do," he said.
Mr. Guiliani said he would consider all of the Council's proposed tax cuts, but said he might argue taking a different approach. "There is never a tax cut proposal that I am not willing to look at," he said. "It doesn't mean that I'll agree with it completely, and I might want to substitute one of my own." But he applauded the Council's efforts and said he expected smooth budget negotiations this year.

HEADLINE: Market Place: Technology;
For Silicon Alley, a Time to Rise and Shine

BYLINE: By LISA NAPOLI and SAUL HANSELL 

 BODY:
Out of symbolic modesty -- or maybe impatience -- Candice Carpenter took the subway rather than a limousine back to her office from the Wall Street headquarters of Goldman, Sachs earlier this month.
She returned after watching the stock of iVillage, the Internet company she co-founded, open for trading the day after its initial public offering and quadruple its initial price of $24 before closing at $80.125. 
 "The thing that got me excited," Ms. Carpenter said of coming back to iVillage's offices at 170 Fifth Avenue, near the Flatiron Building, "was walking the halls and seeing the kids so delirious that their hard work had paid off."
During the next few months there are likely to be a lot of delirious executives and employees as more than a dozen of New York City's Internet companies try to go public. The stock of Miningco.com, for example, another prominent member of the group, more than doubled after shares began trading Wednesday.
The offerings, of course, will create a windfall payoff for the work put into these companies, with their young programmers and producers holding options worth -- on paper -- tens or even hundreds of thousands of dollars.
But it is the company founders who really have the right to be delirious. Ms. Carpenter and Scott Kurnit, chief executive of Miningco.com, each now have paper wealth of more than $50 million.
Along with financial rewards, the successful public offerings come as more than a little vindication for Silicon Alley, the collection of entrepreneurs trying to make a go of Internet businesses in New York. They have had to fight harder for respect and for financing than their Silicon Valley rivals on the other side of the continent. The technology mainstream considered the New York companies too lavish in their spending, too focused on content by writers and designers -- and simply too far from Palo Alto, Calif.
Never mind all that. The success of iVillage's offering indicates that there are no hard feelings between Silicon Alley and the great wired masses of Internet stock buyers.
Not surprisingly, most New York Internet companies going public are on-line extensions of the city's traditional industries. There are media companies, like iVillage, which provides women's services; Miningco.com, an Internet directory that relies on human guides, not machines; and Star Media, an on-line service for Latin America. There are Wall Street offshoots, including Wit Capital, an on-line investment bank, and Multex, which distributes brokerage firm research. The advertising business is represented by Razorfish, an interactive agency, and Media Metrix, which measures the audience size of World Wide Web sites.
Companies in other New York industries, like publishing and fashion, are spinning out on-line divisions. These include Barnesandnoble.com from Barnes & Noble, the bookseller, and Iturf, an offshoot of Delia's, a maker of clothing for teen-age girls.
"New York has become the center of the Internet content business," said Fred Wilson, a principal with Flatiron Partners, a venture capital unit of Chase Manhattan. "People said that all the technology plays and all the infrastructure plays have been done. Now it's time for what you can build on top of the technology."
To be sure, some companies going public here would be just as natural in Silicon Valley, including Juno, an E-mail service financed in part by the News Corporation, and Mapquest, which makes electronic maps.
Choosing to base in New York was "a real gamble," said Kevin O'Connor, chief executive of Doubleclick, an on-line advertising network that went public in February 1998 and has a value of $3.2 billion, the most of any Silicon Alley company. After starting his business in Atlanta in 1996, he considered moving to California, but chose New York because "we're a tech company and a media company, but mostly a media company."
Six months ago, as technology stocks plummeted and investors shunned new offerings, many Silicon Alley companies feared they would never be able to go public. But the dark days lasted only a few weeks, and two small New York Internet companies, Earthweb and Theglobe.com, went public in the fall.
Rather than validation for Silicon Alley, the herd of public offerings could simply mean that investors are so addicted to Internet stocks they will buy anything, even something as improbable as a technology company based in New York.
Indeed, the iVillage offering went forward, at twice the projected price, mere days after the company's former chief financial officer accused Ms. Carpenter of overly aggressive accounting. iVillage says it stands by its financial reports, which were audited by PricewaterhouseCoopers.
"iVillage is a lesson that the companies with the highest profile in the media earn the highest valuations, even in the face of accounting questions," said Alec L. Ellison, a managing director of Broadview, a New Jersey investment bank. "In the absence of any rational metric with which to value a company, investors are betting on the future, and that means image and brand."
Analysts are quick to caution that for any investor with a time horizon longer than the life of a fruit fly, nearly all of these companies are highly speculative. Even the biggest and most established, like iVillage, are swimming in red ink with no clear way to profits in sight. Some companies are so small and unproven that in earlier days they would have hardly qualified for a bank loan, never mind a stock offering.
Whether good for investors or not, the flow of money benefits the New York economy as some entrepreneurs apply profits from these offerings to start new companies.
"This wealth is the next-generation seed capital that will be reinvested in start-ups and will help strengthen the whole ecosystem of new media in New York," said Brian Horey, partner at Lawrence, Smith & Horey, a venture capital firm, and a founder of the New York New Media Association. "Over the last 30 years, this has turned Silicon Valley into an enormous economic flywheel."
For many of those at iVillage, though, the talk is less of pushing that flywheel than of pumping money into the local economy in different ways: buying an apartment, taking a vacation, paying for a nice wedding.
"I remember the first meeting where we talked about the options, and they told us they might be worth a thousand dollars some day if the company grew," said Kate Hanley, a producer at iVillage since 1996.
"We were like, 'yeah, but we want a raise.' A lot of us had no idea what an I.P.O. was. Now, I've learned what a limit order is."

CORRECTION-DATE: March 31, 1999, Wednesday

 CORRECTION:
A chart in Business Day on Monday about companies in Silicon Alley that have issued stock or plan to issue stock misstated details about several of them.
For Multex, the underwriter was BancBoston Robertson Stephens, not Bear Stearns. For Razorfish, the estimated value of shares being offered to the public is $33 million, not $3 million; the company's cumulative loss since it was founded was $264,000, not $930,000. For iVillage, the cumulative loss was $76.3 million, not $24.2 million. Wit Capital's chief executive is Ronald Readmond, not Robert Readmond. Internet Financial Services' 1998 loss was $632,410, not $9.7 million; its cumulative loss was $1,903,761, not $24.2 million.
In addition, an incorrect logo was used for Internet Financial Services. The logo used was for one of the company's suppliers, Stockpoint, which is privately held and has not announced plans to go public.

HEADLINE: Computer Associates to Purchase a Rival

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: ISLANDIA, N.Y., March 29

 BODY:
Computer Associates International Inc., the fourth-largest maker of software in the United States, agreed today to buy a rival, Platinum Technology International Inc., for about $3.5 billion in cash, bolstering its fledgling service business to help counter slower corporate sales.
Computer Associates said it would pay $29.25 for each share of Platinum, which makes software and helps companies run their information systems. That is about triple Friday's closing price of $9.875. Shares of Platinum, based in Oakbrook Terrace, Ill., soared $14.1875 today, to $24.0625 on the Nasdaq. Computer Associates rose $3.0625, to $37 on the New York Stock Exchange. 
 Computer Associates, based in Islandia, has been buying companies that set up and run computer systems as demand ebbs for its software used to manage computer networks at big companies. It is also facing increased competition from rivals such as BMC Software Inc. This is the fifth and largest acquisition since the Computer Sciences Corporation, one of the top computer-services companies, rebuffed Computer Associates' $9.8 billion unsolicited offer a year ago.
"It gives them critical mass" needed to remain competitive with rivals, said Paul Rodriguez, an analyst with CE Unterberg Towbin.
Computer Associates plans to link its products with Platinum Technology's consulting services, especially in Europe. Computer Associates also wants Platinum's software for managing data and technology.
Computer Associates, which initiated talks about a week ago, will pay for the acquisition through a $4.5 billion credit line with Credit Suisse First Boston Group. It expects to pay the loan back in about four years.
The acquisition of Platinum is expected to add 25 cents a share to Computer Associates' earnings in the first 12 months, excluding charges. It should also increase cash from operations by about $450 million, the company said.
"There's a lot of room for profit improvement, and I think C.A. sees that," said Jean Orr, an analyst with Nutmeg Securities in Westport, Conn.
Computer Associates plans to complete the purchase "within a couple of months," officials said on a conference call.
Platinum Technology had $986.2 million in 1998 revenue, with services accounting for about 30 percent of that. Computer Associates had sales of $3.62 billion for the nine months ended Dec. 31.
"The services dimension is one of the reasons they are buying them," said Gary Abbott, an analyst with Punk, Ziegel & Company.
The large premium offered to Platinum's shareholders suggests that Computer Associates was competing with other suitors, possibly I.B.M. and the Compuware Corporation, said Jim Mendelson, an analyst with Soundview Technology Group.
Shares of both companies plunged over the last year amid concern about slowing sales as the economic slowdown in Asia crimped business and competition increased. Since they reached highs in July, Platinum Technology's shares are down about 71 percent and Computer Associates slumped about 50 percent.
Platinum Technology said in February that it would dismiss 1,000 workers, or about 15 percent of its work force. The chief executive, Andrew Filipowski, vowed then to lift profits or sell the company by 2000.
The companies said some workers would be laid off, and Computer Associates instituted a hiring freeze.

HEADLINE: Sales of New Homes Declined in February

BYLINE: Bloomberg News 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
New-home sales unexpectedly fell in February for a third consecutive month, suggesting that higher mortgage rates might prevent the housing market this year from matching the record pace of 1998, the Commerce Department reported today.
Sales of new single-family homes declined 2 percent in February, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 881,000, the lowest in five months, the department said. That followed declines of 6.7 percent in January and 2.1 percent in December. 
 Even with the three-month drop, February's sales rate was close to last year's. The glass "is more than half full; it's 93 percent full" for housing, said Paul Taylor, senior economist at America's Community Bankers, an industry group. This year is likely to rank second in the records for new-home sales, he said.
For all of last year, new-home sales rose to a record 886,000 from 804,000 in 1997. Last year's sales beat the record of 819,000 set in 1977.
Higher mortgage rates may be one reason sales have tapered off since November. The average rate on a 30-year fixed mortgage increased to 7.04 percent this month from 6.81 percent in February and 6.79 percent in January, according to figures from Freddie Mac, a big buyer of home mortgages.
Industry indexes that measure volume of prospective buyers at model homes and mortgage applications have also declined. Analysts had expected February new-home sales to rise seven-tenths of a percent, to an annual pace of 924,000 units.
The stock prices of home builders are lower this year because of expectations that sales have peaked. The Standard & Poor's Homebuilding Index -- a barometer for the industry consisting of Kaufman & Broad, the Pulte Corporation and the Centex Corporation -- rose 1.7 percent today. For the year, however, the index is down 23.5 percent. That compares with a 9 percent gain for the Dow Jones industrial average this year.

HEADLINE: I.M.F. Relents on Aid to Russia, but U.S. Talks Tougher

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The International Monetary Fund said today that it had agreed on "a broad framework" to resume financial aid to Russia, but American officials immediately warned that they would permit only enough support to keep Moscow from defaulting on international loans.
The vague accord, the first since Russia's economic meltdown last summer, followed a hastily arranged meeting in Moscow between Michel Camdessus, managing director of the fund, and Prime Minister Yevgeny Primakov of Russia. But only hours after Mr. Primakov's aides suggested that the I.M.F. would lend Russia more than $8 billion this year, the fund denied that Mr. Camdessus had discussed specific amounts. 
 Other officials suggested that Russia would receive only a few billion dollars this year. That would barely cover what Russia owes the I.M.F. this year, meaning that the aid may amount to a paper transfer that allows Moscow a grace period on repaying its loans.
American opposition to new loans to Russia reflects how deeply diplomatic and economic relations between Moscow and Washington have deteriorated in the last nine months.
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, who helped engineer an I.M.F.-led, $17 billion emergency bailout for Russia last year, is now reluctant to provide more than token aid to the country. The July bailout failed in August, when Russia devalued its currency and then fired the team of economic reformers whom the United States was betting on to remake the Russian economy.
Since then, the I.M.F. and Mr. Primakov have been involved in an elaborate political and economic dance. Russia has refused to undertake many politically painful economic reforms, but still insists on a resumption of aid. An accord was expected last week, when Mr. Primakov was scheduled to arrive in Washington. But he ordered his plane to return home when he learned that NATO was planning to begin bombing Serbia.
Now, Russia's opposition to the NATO bombing and suggestions that Moscow might quietly resume military aid to President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia have greatly complicated the I.M.F. negotiations with Russia. That became clearer than ever today, when, only hours after Mr. Primakov declared that an I.M.F. deal was imminent, the Russian Defense Minister claimed that a Russian-made air defense system sold to the Yugoslav military was responsible for shooting down a United States F-117A stealth fighter on Saturday.
White House officials said today that they would open themselves up to Congressional attack if they approved a resumption of financial aid to Moscow at a moment when Russia was resuming aid to Mr. Milosevic. While the United States holds only 18 percent of the voting power on the board of the fund, as a practical matter it could veto an aid package. Today, White House officials went out of their way to make sure that Mr. Primakov understood that the two issues were closely linked.
"A U.S. judgment about an I.M.F. program," a senior Administration official said, "would depend on Russian economic policy actions and could also be influenced by broader political developments," a clear reference to tangible Russian support for Mr. Milosevic.
Even before the situation in Kosovo strained relations with Moscow, the United States had been trying to limit the I.M.F.'s re-engagement with Moscow. American officials have told the fund that they would oppose offering what the Treasury refers to as "new resources" to Russia, meaning that any loans could be used only for repaying the fund or the World Bank. Under one possible arrangement under discussion, Russia would repay the fund and receive a check for roughly the same amount the next day -- a move that, in other contexts, is often referred to as "check kiting."
American officials have gone further, saying that by the end of the year they want to make sure that Russia pays more to the I.M.F. in 1999 than the I.M.F. pays Russia. But officials of the World Bank and the fund say they oppose that approach.
Mr. Camdessus was clearly seeking to mend fences in Moscow over the weekend. He made a whirlwind tour of the Russian political elite, meeting with parliamentary leaders and with directors of Gazprom, the giant Russian natural gas company, and even dining with the Patriarch of Moscow and All Russia, Alexei II. He also talked on the telephone with President Boris N. Yeltsin.
Russia agreed to raise 60 billion rubles -- about $2.4 billion at current exchange rates -- in new revenues, partly by postponing a cut in its value-added tax. The fund protested when the Russian Duma approved the tax cut, noting that the value-added tax was one of the few taxes that Russia successfully collects.
The fund said Russia also agreed that its budget would run what is called a "primary surplus" of 2 percent of the country's gross domestic product, or roughly $3 billion. The term refers to a surplus before payments toward foreign debts. But how the Russians will achieve that surplus, one senior I.M.F. official said, "is still a considerable mystery."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
BP and ARCO Said to Edge Toward Deal

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS and LAURA M. HOLSON 

 BODY:
BP Amoco P.L.C. is close to an agreement to acquire the Atlantic Richfield Company in a stock swap worth about $28 billion, executives close to the talks said yesterday.
The executives also said that some of the top executive officers at ARCO including its chief executive, Michael R. Bowlin, were not likely to remain in their jobs if an agreement was reached. 
 The boards of both companies are expected to meet on Wednesday to consider the deal.
BP Amoco, which was formed last December in a merger of British Petroleum and the Amoco Corporation, forming the word's third-largest oil company, and ARCO confirmed the negotiations in a joint statement yesterday.
They cautioned, however, that an agreement had not been reached and that the discussions could still be called off.
Nevertheless, shares of ARCO surged yesterday, rising $8.6875, or 13.3 percent, to $74.0625, in heavy trading on the New York Stock Exchange. The American depository shares of BP Amoco, each of which represents six ordinary shares, were also up sharply, climbing $4.5625, or 4.5 percent, to $105. In London, BP Amoco shares rose 40 pence, or 3.9 percent, to 1,077.
ARCO, based in Los Angeles, has most of its operations on the West Coast, where it is a large refiner and seller of gasoline. It has about 26 percent of the gasoline retail market in California.
Analysts said that ARCO would flesh out BP Amoco's national refining and marketing business, which is strong in the East and Midwest but not on the West Coast.
It also has major oil and natural gas fields and production in Alaska, gas fields in Indonesia and is making an effort to get a foothold in the Caspian Sea region.
"Buying ARCO would transform BP Amoco from a very powerful regional company to a truly global oil company," said J. Robinson West, the chairman of the Petroleum Finance Company, an industry consulting group based in Washington. "In the past, they have cut costs very well, but this gives them growth as well."
The main force in building up BP Amoco has been Sir John Browne, its chief executive, who took over a slow-moving and troubled company in 1991 and helped to transform it into an industry pacesetter by sharply cutting costs and forming alliances with other oil companies.
His takeover of Amoco last August helped lead to the purchase of the Mobil Corporation by the Exxon Corporation.
In the current talks, ARCO is being advised by Goldman, Sachs & Company and Salomon Smith Barney, a unit of Citigroup. BP Amoco is being advised by Morgan Stanley Dean Witter & Company.
If Sir John were successful in buying ARCO, analysts said it would put great pressure on other large oil companies such as Texaco Inc. and the Chevron Corporation to also find merger partners. The stocks of most major oil companies rose yesterday, with shares of Texaco up $2.1875, to $57.75, and Chevron up $2.8125, to $89.625.
Executives close to the talks said yesterday that BP Amoco would offer about $28 billion in stock for ARCO.
"That's a good deal for ARCO shareholders," said Adam Sieminski, an analyst with BT Alex. Brown, because they would gain shares in a growing company.
ARCO has been severely hurt by the big fall in crude oil prices last year, which sharply lowered its earnings and led to loss in the fourth quarter. Analysts began to wonder whether it could keep paying its substantial yearly dividend of $2.85 a share.
Analysts said that one of the main reasons BP Amoco was interested in ARCO was that both companies have big holdings and production in Prudhoe Bay, Alaska, where they made major finds in the late 1960's.
ARCO has largely lived off its production there, generating money to build its refining and chemical business.
For a time the company was an industry leader, being one of the first oil companies to add convenience stores to its gas stations. It has 1,700 in seven Western states, with 900 in California. It also built two large refineries, one in Cherry Point, Wash., and the other in Carson, Calif. Both can process more than 200,000 barrels of crude oil a day.
Rather than concentrating on exploration to find another Prudhoe Bay, however, the company went into the petrochemical business and bought tankers to transport crude oil.
As the production began to run down in Alaska, the company began an aggressive program to find more oil but had disappointing results. In the 1990's, it sold its chemical business to finance more exploration and bought an independent oil company, Union Texas Holdings Inc. of Houston, last year to strengthen its overseas exploration operations.
Its refining and gas station business has also been under pressure as new low-cost independent refiners have entered its markets, and other refiners have merged their operations and cut costs.
ARCO has not joined the industry in building stations where customers can use a wide variety of credit cards to fill up at the pumps. It still relies on cash and debit cards.
Some analysts think that employees of the former Amoco, who were considered savvy marketers of gasoline, will be of great help in revamping ARCO's gasoline business.
While ARCO has been more of a no-frills seller of gasoline, it nevertheless has been making good money at it, with profits from its stations surpassing many of its larger rivals.
"We're not talking Bloomingdale's, we're talking Sam's," Mr. Sieminski said, referring to the discount retail chain. "They're price conscious and they're doing very well."

PUB-DATE: March 30, 1999

SECTION: Section A; Page 22; Column 1; Editorial Desk 

LENGTH: 296 words

HEADLINE: 10,000

 BODY:
It was only last fall that stock markets in the United States and most other countries were reeling in fear of a possible worldwide recession. That it failed to appear is readily apparent from the fact that the Dow Jones industrial average yesterday closed above 10,000 for the first time -- an increase of more than 30 percent in less than seven months.
The Asian crisis that sparked the worries is far from over, and the best thing that can be said about Russia, whose default on its debt precipitated the market plunge, is that the country has not descended into anarchy. Latin American economies remain troubled, with Brazil in a severe recession. 
 Nonetheless, the developed world is optimistic. The British stock market also hit a record high this month, and most European markets have moved up as well, although not to new highs. Even long-suffering Japan seems to be reviving. One reason is that central banks in all major economies lowered interest rates, spurring economic growth and providing liquidity for stock market speculation.
Contrary to the expectations of most economists -- and of this page -- the American economy did not even pause while much of the world was suffering. Instead, consumers benefited from lower prices for commodities and imported goods, and their willingness to spend rather than save more than offset weakness in the manufacturing sector.
There are ample signs of speculative excess in the stock market, most notably in Internet stocks. Corporate profits have not risen nearly as rapidly as stock prices, a fact that is likely to be important when economic growth starts to slow. But for now, America is in love with the market and happy to see rising stock prices as an endorsement of its supremacy in the world economy.

HEADLINE: U.S. Eases Stance on the Eviction of Landowners in Oneidas' Suit

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN 

 BODY:
The Federal Government moved yesterday to soften its support of the Oneida Indians' claim to 270,000 acres of land in central New York, saying it did not want to evict any private landowners from their homes.
A Department of Justice official said that the Government still supported the Oneidas' original claim that state and local governments had acquired Oneida lands in the late 1700's and early 1800's through illegal and coercive treaties. The official, speaking on the condition of anonymity, reiterated that the 20,000 or so landowners who lived within the land-claim area were still defendants in the lawsuit, and therefore liable for any future damages. 
 But in recent months, the specter of eviction -- or ejectment, in legal terms -- had so overshadowed the lawsuit, and distracted from the parallel effort to hammer out a compromise, that the Justice Department felt that it was imperative to assure landowners that eviction was not a viable part of the solution, the official said. So yesterday, the Department of Justice formally asked for permission to amend its complaint during opening arguments in Federal District Court in Syracuse.
It was not immediately clear when Judge Neal McCurn would rule on the Government's request. But if the request is granted -- which seems all but certain -- then it could help assuage the anxiety that has been percolating in recent months in Madison and Oneida Counties, where there have been boycotts of Oneida businesses, protest meetings and personal threats and uncivil language.
"They are listening. They are paying attention, and I think that's very significant," said Representative Sherwood Boehlert, a Republican from Utica, summing up his discussions with Government officials. "I think this takes away the cloud of uncertainty that's hovering over all the private property owners in the region that someone is going to knock on their door and say, 'Get out!' "
Others, including Gov. George E. Pataki, want the Government to go even further.
"The Federal Government's decision to back down from its outrageous attempt to frighten and intimidate the people of central New York by seeking to eject them from their homes is a small first step, but not nearly enough," Mr. Pataki said in a statement. He added that the Government "must end its aggressive campaign against its own citizens by completely removing all homeowners from the land claim lawsuit."
Leon R. Koziol, a lawyer for Upstate Citizens for Equality, a landowners group that says it has 3,500 members, was even more blunt. While he said he believed that the Government's request took a little bit of the steam out of the Oneidas' claim, he also blamed the Government for stirring up fear and resentment in the first place.
"They used the people as pawns," Mr. Koziol said. "It was an unconscionable act, to use their own citizens as pawns in a scheme to raise the level of political discussion and legal argument in a lawsuit."
A spokeswoman for the Oneida Indian Nation, Carole Trimble, characterized the Government's request as inconsequential to the merits of the case. She restated the Oneidas' hope that they can reach a resolution that is fair to all sides.
The dispute between the Oneidas and the state officially dates from 1970, when the Oneidas filed a lawsuit in Federal District Court saying that their land had been seized illegally. In 1985, the Supreme Court upheld the Oneidas' claim. But talks between the Oneidas and the state stalled for years.
So in December, to prod the negotiations along, the Federal Government sided with the Oneidas, filing an amended complaint that named 20,000 landowners as defendants. And that, in turn, unleashed a latent sense of resentment over the Oneidas' recent good fortunes.
Like all federally recognized tribes, the Oneida Indian Nation does not pay taxes. And thanks in part to their new Turning Stone Casino, the Oneidas have seemingly risen from abject poverty to unbridled affluence in a very short time, while their neighbors have been grappling with a tough local economy.
To vent their frustration, more than 2,000 motorists participated in a rally in January in front of the casino. An even larger one is scheduled for May 1, when Mr. Koziol said he expected that 5,000 cars would travel from Buffalo to Albany to protest the Oneidas.
The next court hearing on the land-claim issue is scheduled for May 26. The court-appointed mediator, Ronald J. Riccio, who was brought in last month to try and broker a compromise, has arranged two mediation sessions and has visited the land-claim area, and said he planned more visits and more meetings. Mr. Riccio, the mediator who is also dean of Seton Hall University School of Law, described the progress as encouraging, even suggesting that there might be some kind of agreement in mid-May.
"It's possible; I'm not ruling it out," Mr. Riccio said. "At least what's possible is you can come up with the broad framework of a settlement. I think the actual implementation would take some time, but you want everyone to say, 'Yes, these are the components of a settlement,' and I think we're at the stage where I think everybody has a good idea of what the components are."
