HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place -- Does the Dow Measure Up?;
Despite Growth of Rivals the Old Gauge Still Matters

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT 

 BODY:
It is a staple of the nightly news, of daily discussions at dry cleaners and doughnut shops, overheard in cell-phone conversations and in cocktail party chatter.
When Americans talk about the stock market, they talk about the Dow Jones industrial average. For roughly a century "How'd the market do today?" has inevitably been answered with a reference to the Dow. 
 Yet for all its longevity and accuracy as a barometer of the progress of the stock market, the 30 stocks of the Dow industrial average have, in recent years, failed to keep up with the gains of smaller, more sought after companies that are part of other indexes that track the overall market. Since the beginning of 1995, the Dow has lagged behind by a few percentage points each year the returns of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, the broader index that most professional investors on Wall Street use as a benchmark for their own portfolios.
The Dow has fallen even further behind the Nasdaq composite index, rich with the technology stocks that lately have been the market's biggest gainers. Even the unwieldy Wilshire 5000, the universe of stocks which, despite its name, encompasses more than 7,000 companies based in the United States, has climbed at a rate more than 1 percent a year faster than has the Dow.
All of which raises the question: Does the Dow matter anymore?
The answer is still yes.
"The Dow has come in for a lot of criticism for a long time," said Steven Leuthold, a market analyst whose firm, Leuthold Group, is based in Minneapolis. "People complain that it has a limited number of stocks; they complain about the way the index is calculated," based on the numerical price of stocks rather than the total market value of the companies it tracks.
"But I think the rather amazing thing is how accurately, over time, it has tracked a broader index like the S.& P. 500," Mr. Leuthold added. "There are times when the two get out of sync. But we use it because it is still the first thing people refer to when they talk about how the market did today."
So far this year, the Dow, with a gain of nearly 9 percent -- and closing above 10,000 for the first time yesterday -- is more than a percentage point ahead of the S.& P. 500. But those gains mask a growing disparity in the overall market. While the major stock indexes are trading at or near record highs, three-quarters of the stocks in the S.& P. index, and 88 percent of the stocks traded on the New York Stock Exchange, are more than 10 percent below their all-time highs.
The differences between the gauges can be traced to how they are calculated. The Dow industrial average is price-weighted, meaning that high-priced stocks, like I.B.M. at over $177 a share, have more of an effect on the Dow than do low-priced stocks like Disney, at about $33. That is true even though a company with a low share price but substantially more shares in circulation could be worth more.
In contrast, the S.& P. indexes are weighted by market capitalization, meaning that the indexes are most influenced by companies whose total stock market value -- derived by multiplying their share price by the number of shares in circulation -- is greatest.
A system like the Dow's price-based system was necessary in the late 19th century, when the Dow was first compiled and the calculations had to be done with pencil and paper. The first calculations, in 1896 with a group of 12 stocks, were very simple: add the stocks' prices and divide by 12.
Today, the average is calculated by dividing the sum of the prices by a fractional divisor, currently about 0.225. That means that each $1 change in the price of a Dow stock moves the average by about 4.44 points.
With the advent of superfast computers and instant reporting of changes in stock prices, any index can be calculated in a microsecond, adjusted for stock splits and dividends. More importantly for the companies that devise and market indexes for use with options, futures and other derivatives contracts, an index can be frequently tinkered with through the removal and replacement of companies without difficulty.
That has caused new indexes to sprout like mushrooms, with each seeming to slice the stock market into ever-smaller pieces. Bloomberg lists 417 indexes tracking United States equity markets alone.
From a statistical perspective, however, the Dow is among the most efficient gauges around. It uses only 30 stocks, which together account for close to a quarter of the stock market's total value. The S.& P. 500's components, which together account for about four-fifths of the market's total value, are, by comparison, much closer to a full census than to a sampling.
Recently, critics of the Dow have argued that the older gauge, having missed the upswing caused by the Internet investment mania, lags behind too far. From the beginning of 1995, when the latest leg of the stock market's upward march began, through the end of 1998, the Dow climbed 24.3 percent annually, compared with 27.8 percent for the S.& P. 500.
The difference stems primarily from the absence of the technology giants Intel and Microsoft from the Dow industrials. While the Dow has contained I.B.M. since 1981 and Hewlett-Packard since 1997, those stocks have not performed as well as Intel and Microsoft, the stars of the new economy built around personal computers and the Internet.
Over longer periods, however, the gauges track quite closely. Since the beginning of 1980 through the end of last year, the Dow gained 13.4 percent a year, compared with 13.6 percent for the S.& P. 500. Including reinvested dividends, a measure known as total return, an investment in the Dow and the S.& P. 500 would have come even closer -- an average annual gain of 17.72 percent for the Dow industrials versus 17.65 percent for the S.& P.
Even when measured since the beginning of 1990, the two benchmarks differ by an average of less than a half of 1 percent a year: 17.8 percent for the S.& P. 500 versus 17.4 percent for the Dow.
The proliferation of indexes in part comes from the desire to more closely track certain sectors of the market.
"It depends what you want a benchmark to be," said Leah Modigliani, an equity strategist at Morgan Stanley. "It serves to represent a universe of investments, to act as a benchmark" for investment managers to measure their own performance.
Increasingly, however, stock market gauges have also provided an alternative investment, one that frequently can be had at a lower cost than that charged by traditional portfolio managers. Today, index mutual funds and unit investment trusts track virtually every stock market gauge imaginable, from subsets of Internet stocks to the entire universe of publicly traded companies.
"Without a doubt, the Dow has a lot of problems that are well known," said Theodore R. Aronson, a money manager and market analyst at Aronson + Partners in Philadelphia. "I nonetheless marvel at how experts in the field allude to it. And it's still hands-down the most popular shorthand for how the market's done. I wake up each morning as National Public Radio is reporting the Dow, which they still do to two decimal places. Think about how meaningless that is."
Indeed, said Mr. Leuthold, people seem to be increasingly frightened by the Dow's amplitude. Hundred-point swings in the Dow often result in bigger headlines in the next day's paper, even though 100 points recently represented little more than a move of 1 percent in the average.
With the Dow crossing 10,000, perhaps the excitement that has come with each 1,000-point mark will die down. But the 10,000 level might also represent a level that has greater historic resonance to investors. For once it reaches the Promised Land, will the Dow be able to move on?
Consider that for six periods from 1963 to 1982, the Dow Jones industrial average approached or crossed the 1,000 level, only to retreat back into triple digits. The first of those concluded a fantastic run of three and a half years, during which the Dow gained more than 20 percent a year.
The Dow first finished a full day of trading above 1,000 in November 1972. But it was not until a full decade later, at the beginning of the bull market in late 1982, that the Dow average firmly climbed above the 1,000 mountain. Today, of course, it looks more like a molehill.

HEADLINE: Company's Plan to Privatize Angers Shareholders

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN 

 BODY:
Even in a raging bull market, some New York Stock Exchange companies wallow in neglect. A few, like dusty antiques in the attic, turn out to be gems.
The PEC Israel Economic Corporation, a holding company that has stakes in Israeli ventures, is one such company. Management takes a low profile. Analysts ignore the company. The stock price has gone mostly downhill since the early 1990's. So forgotten is PEC that it is slated for extinction, with its largest shareholder preparing to buy up the remaining shares and delist it as of today. 
 The delisting, however, comes as several PEC investments, including a stake in Cellcom, Israel's largest mobile telephone operator, are scheduled to go public and capture a market value that analysts believe will greatly exceed the amount that PEC says they are worth.
The Discount Investments Corporation, a company controlled by the Recanati family of Israel, is the majority PEC shareholder that wants to privatize PEC. By doing so, it would not have to share any future gains in the market value of PEC assets with other stockholders, disgruntled shareholders contend.
The offer to privatize PEC was made several months after Goldman, Sachs & Company, the investment bank, paid $78 million for a 9.5 percent stake in the Israeli holding company that controls Discount Investments. Goldman, jointly with Lehman Brothers, also happens to be a lead underwriter of Cellcom, which plans to list shares in the United States shortly.
Minority PEC shareholders have filed lawsuits to block the deal, contending that the offer substantially underestimates the value of the investments PEC has made over the years. Unless the suits prevail, the delisting is a done deal because Discount Investments already holds an 80 percent stake in PEC. Shareholders will formally vote on the proposal at a shareholder meeting today in New York.
The conflict involves a tangled story of American-Israeli economic connections that turned sour. Some shareholders see the demise of PEC as an ignominious end to a company founded in 1926 by Justice Louis Brandeis of the Supreme Court. He and other Zionists established the company to support Palestine at a time when Jews there had difficulty raising money for even the most basic necessities, like housing and utilities.
In fact, until the Israeli stock market boom of the 1990's made it easier for Israeli companies to raise capital, PEC was run as much as a charity as a corporation. Its stock is still widely held by Jews around the world who once regarded it as much as a donation as an investment.
"This company was never supposed to be about squeezing every last dime out of shareholders," said Murray S. Greenfield, a retired 72-year-old Israeli broker who sold PEC stock to Jewish visitors to Israel in the 1960's. "It was about building Israel. If Brandeis woke up today he would not be the happiest man in the world, I can tell you that."
For more recent shareholders, PEC's privatization is a fresh lesson about the risks of investing in undervalued companies. Some investors said they snapped up PEC stock in recent years because the price fell far below the level at which analysts estimated the value of its underlying investments. These investors were hoping PEC would eventually enjoy a run-up in the value of Israeli companies that went public after the 1993 peace accord with the Palestinians.
Like PEC, some investment holding companies trade at a discount to the book value of their underlying assets, especially when management is indifferent. But PEC's discount -- more than 46 percent, according to an estimate made by an analyst at Lehman Brothers late last year -- was large enough to make PEC a prime target for investors seeking the most undervalued companies on the United States stock market.
"The pity is that this is happening just as some of PEC's investments are just about to take off," said one large shareholder who declined to be identified because his company has legal action pending. Among the largest minority shareholders are mutual funds run by Credit Suisse Asset Management and the Vanguard Group.
PEC's management team, which includes two members of the Recanati family, declined to comment through a spokesman on the lawsuits or the buyout offer, citing Securities and Exchange Commission regulations that they say prohibit them from speaking publicly while the offer is pending. Goldman, which stands to benefit if the Recanatis have their way at PEC, also declined to comment.
But in documents, the company says that the $30-a-share purchase price for the shares of PEC that Discount Investments does not already own is fair, pointing out that the price is 32 percent higher than what the shares were fetching before Discount Investments made a bid.
Late last year, PEC management hired Merrill Lynch & Company to appraise its value. After Merrill's review, Discount Investment raised its bid to $30 a share from $25.50. Merrill called the $30 price fair, a fact that PEC officials said validates the latest offer.
But some shareholders fault Merrill for collecting as much as $625,000 from PEC in fees if the deal flies, but failing to extract from PEC management the sort of information Merrill normally seeks to judge the fairness of a deal. Merrill acknowledged in its fairness opinion, for example, that it did not have access to the management of Cellcom, the mobile phone operator and what many consider the portfolio's hidden jewel.
"We have not been afforded the opportunity to meet with the management of Cellcom Israel, the company's largest single holding, and thus have not been able to discuss with such management the business prospects of Cellcom," the Merrill review states.
Israeli newspapers have reported that Cellcom plans to go public in the United States this year. Both Merrill and Goldman competed for the right to underwrite that company's shares, which are expected to generate considerable investor interest because of Cellcom's rapid growth and its status as the No. 1 provider of mobile phone services in Israel, itself a leader in per-capita use of cellular telephones.
In its fairness opinion, dated Dec. 11, Merrill explored various ways to value Cellcom and settled on a range of $2 billion to $2.5 billion. More recently, Israeli news reports and Wall Street analysts close to the company have placed a value on Cellcom closer to $3.5 billion.
Merrill officials declined to comment in detail on the fairness opinion. But a spokesman said that the opinion explored the reasons that PEC shares traded at such a heavy discount to the value of its individual investments. It also weighs the possibility that Cellcom's plan to go public could be delayed by a downdraft in the American stock market, as it was late last year.
Shareholders opposed to Discount Investments' offer contend that the discrepancy between Merrill's valuation of Cellcom and the price underwriters now hope to achieve in the initial public offering amounts to $11 a share for PEC's current holders. Discount Investments, they say, should pay at least $41 a share instead of the $30 being proposed.
The dissenting shareholders also say that the understatement of PEC holdings goes beyond Cellcom and extends to PEC's stakes in several private companies and real estate holdings in Israel.
The offer to take PEC public, these shareholders say, does not account for a recent spike in the value of its stake in Gilat Satellite Networks, which just went public on the Nasdaq market last month. Another example: PEC has the right to convert a loan it made to United Pan-Europe Communications, a European cable television company, into shares in that prosperous concern. Shareholders say they consider that conversion right a lucrative opportunity that they will miss if PEC is privatized anytime soon.
Evidence that some long-dormant PEC investments are starting to ascend can be seen in the value of Discount Investment stock listed on the Tel Aviv stock exchange, the shareholders said. Discount Investments' assets mirror many of those held by PEC, as the two invest side by side in many ventures. The two, for example, each hold 12.5 percent stakes in Cellcom, and have identical positions in many other Israeli technology and industrial companies.
Discount Investment stock has been on a tear, with shares rising from less than 80 shekels, or about $19, at the time it offered to buy the shares of PEC it did not already control last fall to more than 140, or about $34.68, in recent days, an all-time high. PEC shares on the New York Stock Exchange, which closed yesterday at $29.6875, rose in December when Discount Investment made its $30-a-share offer, but are still well below 1993 highs.
For now, the issue seems destined to end up in court. But for some shareholders, it's an unfortunate paradox that American Jews who once bought PEC shares to support cash-short Jews in Palestine would wind up in court contending that Israeli financiers are profiting at their expense.
"It's a shame," said Mr. Greenfield, the former broker and PEC shareholder. "But if they are hellbent on profits, then at least we should have our share."

SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 1; Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 629 words

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Dow Closes Above 10,000, Reflecting Strong Economy
After nearly two weeks of thrust and parry, stock traders pushed the Dow Jones industrial average above 10,000 and kept it there until the end of the day's trading. The Dow closed at 10,006.78, up 184.54 points, or 1.9 percent.
Although 10,000 on the Dow was little more than a psychological hurdle for investors, the market's move is significant in what it reflects: unparalleled strength of the economy and American corporations' dominance of the world stage. Page A1. 

Almost lost in the shuffle of the Dow's record-setting day was a solid performance by broader gauges. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 27.37 points, or 2.1 percent, to 1,310.17, while the Nasdaq composite index jumped 73.67 points, or 3 percent, to 2,492.84. C11.
 
Microsoft Reorganizes Into 5 Units
Microsoft's president, Steven Ballmer, announced the reorganization of the company into five divisions that he suggested would act with some independence. Brad Chase, left, and Jon DeVaan will run a newly created consumer and commerce group that includes the Microsoft Network. The other new units are the Business and Enterprise Division, the Consumer Windows Division, the Business Productivity Group for "knowledge workers" like telecommuters, and the Developer Group. C2.
 
A Virus Writer Strikes Again
A software detective who has assembled a dossier of the author of a rapidly spreading computer virus, called Melissa, thinks the virus writer has struck previously and that his activities are already well known to computer security experts. A14.
 
Kodak Issues First-Quarter Alert
In a radical departure for the traditionally secretive company, Eastman Kodak warned that its first-quarter earnings would come in below expectations but gave detailed, and much rosier, forecasts for the rest of the year. C6.
 
Computer Associates to Buy Rival
Computer Associates International, the fourth-largest maker of software in the United States, agreed to buy a rival, Platinum Technology International, for about $3.5 billion. C8.
 
MCI Worldcom Seeks Internet Links
MCI Worldcom, seeking advanced wireless Internet links, is said to be investing about $200 million in what is known as the wireless cable business. It is buying bonds of three small wireless cable operators, CS Wireless, People's Choice and Wireless One, and shares of another, CAI Wireless Systems. C9.
 
Amazon.com Set to Offer Auctions
Furthering its ambition to be involved virtually any time anyone sells anything on the Internet, Amazon.com plans to let individuals and small businesses conduct auctions on its popular Web site, beginning today. Shares of Amazon.com rose nearly 8 percent, closing at $149.625, up $10.5625. C10.
 
KPMG to Consolidate Operations
KPMG, the fifth-largest accounting firm, said that it would consolidate most of its operations into two regional groupings as it seeks to transform a loose association of firms into a unified global business. C9.
 
OPEC Cuts May Hinder U.S. Access
Greater American access to Saudi Arabian petroleum has been put on indefinite hold, senior Saudi officials said after a new OPEC agreement was reached. That agreement, aimed at raising prices by reducing production, was essentially negotiated by Saudi Arabia. C4.
 
Olivetti Raises Telecom Italia Bid
Olivetti raised its hostile bid for a bigger rival, Telecom Italia, by 15 percent, to 60.4 billion euros, or about $64.7 billion. C4.
 
Viag and Algroup Abandon Merger
Viag, Germany's third-largest utility, and Algroup, the world's biggest maker of packaging for the pharmaceutical industry, abandoned their $8.7 billion merger plan because they could not agree on terms. C4.

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: IN BELGRADE;
Belgrade 'Targets' Find Unity 'From Heaven'

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER 

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Serbia, March 29

 BODY:
Along Belgrade's loveliest shopping street, Knez Mihajlova, groups of young men filled with anger and beer today completed the trashing of the American Center, the British Council, the Goethe Institute and the French Cultural Center, as scattered policemen watched and chatted among themselves.
The impact of the stones shattering the windows seemed in rhythm with the sounds of another patriotic concert in the city center, starring Ceca Raznjatovic, wife of the notorious Serbian paramilitary leader Zeljko Raznjatovic, known as Arkan. 
 "Ceca" was famous, a Serbian Cher, before she married Arkan, a proud pioneer of ethnic cleansing in Bosnia. But she has taken to doing music videos with a live tiger cub, a reference to the name of Arkan's brigades, the Tigers.
As ordinary Belgrade residents walked by, or stood to watch the destruction, the young stone throwers also spray-painted obscene graffiti on the walls, comparing NATO to the Nazis and making snide references to President Clinton's sexual life.
Like many of the passers-by, they wore a "target" logo that has swept Belgrade -- a gesture of contempt toward NATO's bombs that is made more palatable by the fact that no NATO bombs have struck the populated center of the Yugoslav capital.
"I'm ashamed to see this," said a medical doctor dressed in a fashionable pale-beige suit who identified herself only as Zaklina. "But you know the bombs have made people crazy. They crack the veneer of civilization, as if war excuses anything."
She laughed then, saying, "Anyway, they are bored with being down in the shelters, with the grannies."
Belgrade is a city where people say "ciao" and drink espresso, judge the latest Hollywood movies with asperity and surf the Net. But the war is dragging it farther from Europe, into isolation, uniting the Serbs behind their soldiers, their Kosovo and even President Slobodan Milosevic. American movies have been withdrawn from the theaters, though the Original Levi's Store across from the American Center was left untrashed by the mobs.
"People used to say that only God could unify the Serbs," said Aleksa Djilas, the historian son of the late Milovan Djilas, the most famous dissident of the Tito period. "But now they've been unified from heaven -- but by the bombs, not by God."
No one really feels they deserve to be bombed, Mr. Djilas said, "even if they know that Milosevic's policies have played a big role."
At the same time, the Government has urged citizens to run to the air raid shelters for 12 or 15 hours a day, understanding that collective panic provides a sense of solidarity with the nation and its military.
"But if you refuse to go down, or say that there is no point in it, people get very angry," said Ilija, a former journalist in his 50's. "People think you're belittling their plight or somehow saying that it's all right that they're sitting in a shelter."
There is much more suspicion around, with rumors of Western spies and local dissidents planting radar guidance devices to insure that NATO's bombs find their targets. Some Serbs insist, conversely, that some devices have been planted in the hotels where journalists are staying, intended not to guide the bombs, but to deflect them.
And the city authorities, while deciding now that it is safer to keep Belgrade's street lights on, still insist that curtains be drawn after 6 P.M., as if NATO's modern weapons still depended on illuminated living rooms to find their targets.
Still, as Yugoslavia faces its sixth day of bombing without major civilian casualties, people are becoming inured to the sharp wail of the sirens. Fewer spend all night in the shelters, but there is little else to do, with nearly all the restaurants, clubs, cafes and movie theaters shutting down at dusk.
"There is a deepening sense of boredom -- the war is really very boring, especially with this monotonous war propaganda on the news," said a cosmopolitan Serb, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "My son is bored; he can't go out, except to visit friends in their shelters, where it's boring. My little one is bored, because all her favorite cartoons like Power Rangers have been replaced by these interminable news programs."
So people are renting armfuls of videos, especially comedies, like "You've Got Mail."
Yet the Serbs remain resourceful and ironic. A basement cafe downtown has renamed itself "The Shelter Cafe," offering satellite television along with coffee and drinks. A placard put up in a window along Knez Mihajlova read: "F-117A. Crashed. Low mileage. Foreign license plates. Cheap. Call after 4 P.M."
Instead of "Dobra den," or good day, people wish each other "Bombar den."
The target placards worn by so many here were printed various ways, but some come straight off the Internet (http://www.beograd.com/ nato/target/target72.gif).
And with cigarettes so scarce, people joke, the war is a superb opportunity to quit smoking.
Some of the mockery is cruder and angrier.
A funeral notice featured Mr. Clinton and said he would be buried alongside others "in charge of destroying civilization," like "Hitler, Napoleon, Mussolini and Ante Pavelic," the Croatian fascist leader during World War II. Mr. Clinton is survived, the notice said, "by wife Hillary, daughter Chelsea, mom, dad, dog, necktie, friend Monica and lots of Tomahawks and radar locators and a big family of NATO rats."
The Milosevic Government, through its censorship of the news media, has isolated Belgrade and the rest of Serbia proper from the real war going on to the south, in Kosovo.
While state television shows careful film of NATO damage, there is little effort to discuss the activities of the military and security forces in Kosovo. While Western politicians and human-rights officials are making harsh accusations of atrocities, killings and "ethnic cleansing" of the ethnic Albanian population, Serbs are informed that their forces are simply responding to the widespread terrorism of the rebel Kosovo Liberation Army and its sympathizers.
Mr. Djilas, the historian, said he considered that the coming visit by Prime Minister Yevgeny M. Primakov of Russia to be a way for President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia to deal with fierce domestic criticism of NATO and doubted that it would produce anything substantive.
But it would also add to what Zoran Djindjic, the leader of the Democratic Party here -- and an organizer of pro-democracy demonstrations against Mr. Milosevic two years ago -- called the "mental filters of censorship and self-censorship" that protect ordinary people from the reality of atrocity.
"The essence of Milosevic's system is that no one knows anything," Mr. Djindjic said. "We don't know how the economy is doing or what the Zastava factory is producing or how many are unemployed, let alone what is happening in Kosovo."
It is the same process that allowed the Germans under Nazism to discount evidence of the Holocaust, Mr. Djindjic said.
"People first need to hear information," he said. "Then, if they hear it, will they believe it? And if they believe it, how can they explain it?" As they try to explain it to themselves, he said, people will think "Maybe the victims are guilty, somehow, and deserve what they get.' "
Mr. Djindjic, Belgrade's reform Mayor for a few months in 1997, said it was important to understand how much Mr. Milosevic depends on a rural constituency that has always hated Belgrade, the sophisticated metropolis. Yugoslavia's civil wars, over the last decade, have brought more and more angry and impoverished Serbs here from Bosnia and Croatia.
"There's more of a rural fatalism now," he said. "There is more xenophobia, myth and superstition. All of this is only accelerated by the war."

HEADLINE: Oil vs. Wilderness

 BODY:

To the Editor:
In the name of protecting Alaska's landscape, you support the Congressional proposal to designate the last sliver of the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge as wilderness (editorial, March 24).
This designation, you argue, would also help prevent calamities like the Exxon Valdez oil spill. 
 We are 56 percent dependent on foreign oil. If we do not continue to seek domestic sources of oil, this dependence will grow. Moreover, oil arriving by foreign tankers increases our exposure to accidents like the Exxon Valdez.
Of the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge's 18.6 million acres, only 1.5 million acres have been set aside for oil exploration. Alaska is already home to 60 percent of the country's wilderness areas. Designating more of the state's land as wilderness would only cripple the area's economic well-being, including that of the Inupiat Eskimos.
 
FRANK H. MURKOWSKI
Chmn., Senate Energy and Natural Resources Committee
Washington, March 25, 1999

LENGTH: 1661 words

HEADLINE: DOW FINISHES DAY OVER 10,000 MARK FOR THE FIRST TIME

BYLINE: By GRETCHEN MORGENSON 

 BODY:
The Dow achieved a perfect 10 yesterday. Thousand, that is.
After nearly two weeks of thrust and parry, traders pushed the Dow Jones industrial average above 10,000 and kept it there until trading ended. The Dow closed at 10,006.78, up 184.54 points, or 1.9 percent. 
 Although 10,000 is little more than a psychological hurdle for investors, the market's move is significant in what it reflects: the unparalleled strength of the economy and the dominance of the world economic stage by American corporations.
More and more of the world's profits are being claimed by the nation's corporations. "Back in 1990 the U.S. share was 36 percent; last year, it was 44 percent," said Jeffrey M. Applegate, chief investment strategist at Lehman Brothers in New York. "That goes a long way toward explaining why U.S. equities have had such exemplary performance."
The Dow has doubled in less than four years; it reached 5,000 in November 1995. It is up nearly tenfold since 1982.
"The market's ability to sustain itself through all sorts of climates and economic conditions -- inflation/ deflation, growth/lack of growth -- has been impressive," said Laszlo Birinyi, president of Birinyi & Associates in Greenwich, Conn., and a global trading consultant for Deutsche Bank. "The ease and the consistency: that's what's so surprising about the market."
A lurking concern, however, is just how narrow the market's advance has been. A growing portion of Americans' investment money is devoted to the 30 well-known companies in the Dow and the components of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, which rose near its record yesterday. But many portfolios have not matched the performance of the Dow and the S.& P. That is because a relatively small number of stocks have pulled the indexes higher. The winners have been the best-known stocks with household names, stocks like General Electric, American Express and Wal-Mart Stores.
The number of stocks that are down significantly in the broad market far outpaces the number that are up substantially.
Fully 76 percent of all stocks are trailing the S.& P.'s return by 15 percent or more. On the Nasdaq, 93 percent of stocks now fetch prices 10 percent or more below their highs of the last 52 weeks, while 88 percent of the stocks on the New York Stock Exchange are in that category. Among the S.& P. stocks, three out of four are at least 10 percent off their highs.
According to statisticians at Salomon Smith Barney, the stocks in the S.& P. 500 were on average 22.6 percent off their highs as of Friday, while the New York Stock Exchange issues were on average 33.4 percent below their peaks. Showing just how concentrated this rally has been among big stocks, the Nasdaq stocks are on average 41.8 percent below their peaks. And this calculation factors in many of the hot technology and Internet stocks.
Neither has the road to Dow 10,000 been pothole free. The financial crisis that began in Asia with the devaluation of the Thai baht in July 1997 and migrated to Russia last summer and then to Brazil gave stock market investors their first taste of significant losses in eight years. But three cuts in interest rates by the Federal Reserve Board turned stocks around last fall.
Economic problems still simmer overseas: fourth-quarter data from Japan, Germany and much of Latin America show declines in gross domestic products there. But difficulties abroad have only served to make the United States stock market more of a haven to investors worldwide. Because the United States is almost alone in experiencing solid growth now, stocks of big American companies are viewed as enticing. Even the increasingly grim situation in Kosovo did not hold back investors yesterday.
Ralph J. Acampora, director of technical research at Prudential Securities, was an early Wall Street believer in Dow 10,000. On March 16, when the average first topped 10,000 and then retreated, Mr. Acampora exulted: "It's exciting. It's America. We all should get up and sing 'God Bless America.' These are our big stocks that are leading. It's anything but irrational."
The stock market surely is America today. An estimated 45 percent of American households own equities, up from about 14 percent in 1980.
And Americans who have been in the market during the 1990's have been riding a superpowered engine of wealth creation. Over the last decade, according to the Federal Reserve System, the value of stocks held by households is up 381 percent, before inflation, handily beating real estate assets, which were up 55 percent. The value of stocks held by households rose to $10.77 trillion last year and represented 25 percent of total household assets, up from 8 percent 15 years ago.
The increasing enthusiasm for stocks has been well founded. For the last five years, the compounded annual return on the S.& P. 500, including dividends, has averaged 24 percent, more than double the 10.9 percent median annual increase in the index since 1926.
As for the period ahead, Mr. Acampora predicts that the Dow will hit 11,500 this year, a 15 percent gain from yesterday's close.
Behind the long bull market are several factors -- declining interest rates, low inflation, increasing productivity as a result of advancements in technology -- that have helped propel corporations to new levels of efficiency.
"The lower rate structure has clearly been the majority of strength in the market," said Ned Riley Jr., chief investment officer at BankBoston. He contends that lower rates account for 80 percent of the return in the stock market this decade. "Only 20 percent was the result of expanding earnings," he said.
But interest rates would never have fallen had inflation not dwindled. On an annualized basis, consumer prices are rising 1.6 percent, well below the average annual rate of 3.2 percent since 1926.
One reason inflation is virtually nonexistent even after years of economic strength is that labor costs remain in the cellar. So even as the economy expands, usually a precursor to higher inflation, there is little threat of overall price increases.
Other factors behind the push to Dow 10,000 include an increase in the money supply, falling commodity prices and a very recent and large decline in total shares outstanding. In the fourth quarter of last year, for example, the total shares outstanding shrank by $178 billion, meaning investors had fewer stocks from which to choose.
Still, the rising tides of both the Dow and the S.& P. index have not been enough to lift all investors' fortunes. With so many stocks underwater, the market's rally sits on thinner and thinner pilings.
It is in fact a worry to many strategists that the market has become even more bifurcated -- a few haves and many have-nots -- than it was last year.
While investors rhapsodize over big, famous-name stocks like Microsoft and McDonald's, smaller issues are scorned. The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks is down 7.7 percent for the year.
But Timothy J. Morris, chief investment officer of Bessemer Trust in New York, says that this market split makes sense. "You could make a very strong argument that investors have acted rationally, rewarding companies that have very strong earnings gains and penalizing ones that haven't," he said.
Nonetheless, some of the forces that have helped push the market higher have been losing steam in recent weeks. That leads some market analysts to wonder how much more investors can expect from stocks this year.
The growth in the nation's money supply has slowed substantially this year. That growth in money, as measured in the broadest sense to include money market funds, currency in circulation, checking and savings accounts, helped stabilize the stock market during the financial crises last fall. Much of the additional money found its way into equities. A decline in that growth could put a lid on stocks. Early this year, according to Christine Callies, equity strategist at Credit Suisse First Boston, annualized growth in money supply fell to 8.4 percent from 16.4 percent.
Interest rates have also been nudged higher by bond traders, nervous that strong economic growth in the United States could ignite inflation. The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond stands at 5.64 percent, well above the 5.10 percent level at the start of the year. The jump could be temporary, of course, but every increase in rates makes bonds slightly more attractive when compared with stocks.
Another worry is that violent price swings in stocks are increasing. According to the Leuthold Group, in the first two months of the year, the S.& P. closed each day with an up or down move of 1 percent or more 45 percent of the time. Between 1992 and 1998, such moves took place only 8 percent of the time.
Even as the Dow reached the milestone yesterday, Tom Telegades, who said he works for an Internet company, shrugged off its importance on his way through Grand Central Terminal. "I'm in the market, but it has no impact on me whatsoever," he said. "It doesn't make any difference in my investment strategy. I'm not doing anything extra because of it."
Such tepid enthusiasm has not been the norm in recent years. According to James Paulsen, chief investment officer at Wells Capital Management in Minneapolis, during the last five years, declines in the stock market have occurred on about one out of four days. Only during the bull stampede beginning in 1926 and extending to 1929 was there less downward movement.
This outsized performance has also put stocks at valuations never seen before. The stocks in the S.& P. 500 are trading at 34 times their earnings, more than double the median historical valuation of around 15 times. And the value of the stock market now exceeds the gross domestic product by a sizable amount.
Regardless, United States stocks are for now very much the place to be. Eight years of rising share prices are a powerful draw.

"Lately, Stocks Have Been the Road to Riches"
In the years after the Dow Jones industrial average first neared 1,000 in the 1960's, stock prices failed to keep up with the growth of the overall economy, as shown by the gross domestic product; with inflation, as shown by the Consumer Price Index; with house prices or with gold. But in recent years stock prices have risen much more rapidly than the other measures, and have now exceeded 1960's peaks for all except the G.D.P. In the following charts, a rising line indicates the Dow did better than the other measure during the period.
 Graph tracks the Dow Jones industrial average, indexed to the following: the gross domestic product; single-family home prices; gold prices; the consumer price index. Data tracked since 1965. (pg. C12)
 
"By Any Measure The Dow Is on a Roll"
The Dow Jones industrial's performance has been extraordinary whether graphed on a conventional scale, right, or on a logarithmic scale, left, which gives the same visual weight to comparable percentage changes.
The bull market that began in 1990 is not as great in percentage terms as the bull market that began in 1921. And the bear market that began in 1929 was much deeper than the brief crash of 1987.
Graph compared a conventional scale of the Dow and a logarithmic scale of the Dow (which is based on percentage changes), plotted monthly, since 1896. It highlights bear and bull markets.

HEADLINE: Computer Associates to Purchase a Rival

BYLINE: Bloomberg News

DATELINE: ISLANDIA, N.Y., March 29

 BODY:
Computer Associates International Inc., the fourth-largest maker of software in the United States, agreed today to buy a rival, Platinum Technology International Inc., for about $3.5 billion in cash, bolstering its fledgling service business to help counter slower corporate sales.
Computer Associates said it would pay $29.25 for each share of Platinum, which makes software and helps companies run their information systems. That is about triple Friday's closing price of $9.875. Shares of Platinum, based in Oakbrook Terrace, Ill., soared $14.1875 today, to $24.0625 on the Nasdaq. Computer Associates rose $3.0625, to $37 on the New York Stock Exchange. 
 Computer Associates, based in Islandia, has been buying companies that set up and run computer systems as demand ebbs for its software used to manage computer networks at big companies. It is also facing increased competition from rivals such as BMC Software Inc. This is the fifth and largest acquisition since the Computer Sciences Corporation, one of the top computer-services companies, rebuffed Computer Associates' $9.8 billion unsolicited offer a year ago.
"It gives them critical mass" needed to remain competitive with rivals, said Paul Rodriguez, an analyst with CE Unterberg Towbin.
Computer Associates plans to link its products with Platinum Technology's consulting services, especially in Europe. Computer Associates also wants Platinum's software for managing data and technology.
Computer Associates, which initiated talks about a week ago, will pay for the acquisition through a $4.5 billion credit line with Credit Suisse First Boston Group. It expects to pay the loan back in about four years.
The acquisition of Platinum is expected to add 25 cents a share to Computer Associates' earnings in the first 12 months, excluding charges. It should also increase cash from operations by about $450 million, the company said.
"There's a lot of room for profit improvement, and I think C.A. sees that," said Jean Orr, an analyst with Nutmeg Securities in Westport, Conn.
Computer Associates plans to complete the purchase "within a couple of months," officials said on a conference call.
Platinum Technology had $986.2 million in 1998 revenue, with services accounting for about 30 percent of that. Computer Associates had sales of $3.62 billion for the nine months ended Dec. 31.
"The services dimension is one of the reasons they are buying them," said Gary Abbott, an analyst with Punk, Ziegel & Company.
The large premium offered to Platinum's shareholders suggests that Computer Associates was competing with other suitors, possibly I.B.M. and the Compuware Corporation, said Jim Mendelson, an analyst with Soundview Technology Group.
Shares of both companies plunged over the last year amid concern about slowing sales as the economic slowdown in Asia crimped business and competition increased. Since they reached highs in July, Platinum Technology's shares are down about 71 percent and Computer Associates slumped about 50 percent.
Platinum Technology said in February that it would dismiss 1,000 workers, or about 15 percent of its work force. The chief executive, Andrew Filipowski, vowed then to lift profits or sell the company by 2000.
The companies said some workers would be laid off, and Computer Associates instituted a hiring freeze.

HEADLINE: Sales of New Homes Declined in February

BYLINE: Bloomberg News

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
New-home sales unexpectedly fell in February for a third consecutive month, suggesting that higher mortgage rates might prevent the housing market this year from matching the record pace of 1998, the Commerce Department reported today.
Sales of new single-family homes declined 2 percent in February, to a seasonally adjusted annual rate of 881,000, the lowest in five months, the department said. That followed declines of 6.7 percent in January and 2.1 percent in December. 
 Even with the three-month drop, February's sales rate was close to last year's. The glass "is more than half full; it's 93 percent full" for housing, said Paul Taylor, senior economist at America's Community Bankers, an industry group. This year is likely to rank second in the records for new-home sales, he said.
For all of last year, new-home sales rose to a record 886,000 from 804,000 in 1997. Last year's sales beat the record of 819,000 set in 1977.
Higher mortgage rates may be one reason sales have tapered off since November. The average rate on a 30-year fixed mortgage increased to 7.04 percent this month from 6.81 percent in February and 6.79 percent in January, according to figures from Freddie Mac, a big buyer of home mortgages.
Industry indexes that measure volume of prospective buyers at model homes and mortgage applications have also declined. Analysts had expected February new-home sales to rise seven-tenths of a percent, to an annual pace of 924,000 units.
The stock prices of home builders are lower this year because of expectations that sales have peaked. The Standard & Poor's Homebuilding Index -- a barometer for the industry consisting of Kaufman & Broad, the Pulte Corporation and the Centex Corporation -- rose 1.7 percent today. For the year, however, the index is down 23.5 percent. That compares with a 9 percent gain for the Dow Jones industrial average this year.

HEADLINE: I.M.F. Relents on Aid to Russia, but U.S. Talks Tougher

BYLINE: By DAVID E. SANGER

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 29

 BODY:
The International Monetary Fund said today that it had agreed on "a broad framework" to resume financial aid to Russia, but American officials immediately warned that they would permit only enough support to keep Moscow from defaulting on international loans.
The vague accord, the first since Russia's economic meltdown last summer, followed a hastily arranged meeting in Moscow between Michel Camdessus, managing director of the fund, and Prime Minister Yevgeny Primakov of Russia. But only hours after Mr. Primakov's aides suggested that the I.M.F. would lend Russia more than $8 billion this year, the fund denied that Mr. Camdessus had discussed specific amounts. 
 Other officials suggested that Russia would receive only a few billion dollars this year. That would barely cover what Russia owes the I.M.F. this year, meaning that the aid may amount to a paper transfer that allows Moscow a grace period on repaying its loans.
American opposition to new loans to Russia reflects how deeply diplomatic and economic relations between Moscow and Washington have deteriorated in the last nine months.
Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, who helped engineer an I.M.F.-led, $17 billion emergency bailout for Russia last year, is now reluctant to provide more than token aid to the country. The July bailout failed in August, when Russia devalued its currency and then fired the team of economic reformers whom the United States was betting on to remake the Russian economy.
Since then, the I.M.F. and Mr. Primakov have been involved in an elaborate political and economic dance. Russia has refused to undertake many politically painful economic reforms, but still insists on a resumption of aid. An accord was expected last week, when Mr. Primakov was scheduled to arrive in Washington. But he ordered his plane to return home when he learned that NATO was planning to begin bombing Serbia.
Now, Russia's opposition to the NATO bombing and suggestions that Moscow might quietly resume military aid to President Slobodan Milosevic of Serbia have greatly complicated the I.M.F. negotiations with Russia. That became clearer than ever today, when, only hours after Mr. Primakov declared that an I.M.F. deal was imminent, the Russian Defense Minister claimed that a Russian-made air defense system sold to the Yugoslav military was responsible for shooting down a United States F-117A stealth fighter on Saturday.
White House officials said today that they would open themselves up to Congressional attack if they approved a resumption of financial aid to Moscow at a moment when Russia was resuming aid to Mr. Milosevic. While the United States holds only 18 percent of the voting power on the board of the fund, as a practical matter it could veto an aid package. Today, White House officials went out of their way to make sure that Mr. Primakov understood that the two issues were closely linked.
"A U.S. judgment about an I.M.F. program," a senior Administration official said, "would depend on Russian economic policy actions and could also be influenced by broader political developments," a clear reference to tangible Russian support for Mr. Milosevic.
Even before the situation in Kosovo strained relations with Moscow, the United States had been trying to limit the I.M.F.'s re-engagement with Moscow. American officials have told the fund that they would oppose offering what the Treasury refers to as "new resources" to Russia, meaning that any loans could be used only for repaying the fund or the World Bank. Under one possible arrangement under discussion, Russia would repay the fund and receive a check for roughly the same amount the next day -- a move that, in other contexts, is often referred to as "check kiting."
American officials have gone further, saying that by the end of the year they want to make sure that Russia pays more to the I.M.F. in 1999 than the I.M.F. pays Russia. But officials of the World Bank and the fund say they oppose that approach.
Mr. Camdessus was clearly seeking to mend fences in Moscow over the weekend. He made a whirlwind tour of the Russian political elite, meeting with parliamentary leaders and with directors of Gazprom, the giant Russian natural gas company, and even dining with the Patriarch of Moscow and All Russia, Alexei II. He also talked on the telephone with President Boris N. Yeltsin.
Russia agreed to raise 60 billion rubles -- about $2.4 billion at current exchange rates -- in new revenues, partly by postponing a cut in its value-added tax. The fund protested when the Russian Duma approved the tax cut, noting that the value-added tax was one of the few taxes that Russia successfully collects.
The fund said Russia also agreed that its budget would run what is called a "primary surplus" of 2 percent of the country's gross domestic product, or roughly $3 billion. The term refers to a surplus before payments toward foreign debts. But how the Russians will achieve that surplus, one senior I.M.F. official said, "is still a considerable mystery."

HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
BP and ARCO Said to Edge Toward Deal

BYLINE: By AGIS SALPUKAS and LAURA M. HOLSON

 BODY:
BP Amoco P.L.C. is close to an agreement to acquire the Atlantic Richfield Company in a stock swap worth about $28 billion, executives close to the talks said yesterday.
The executives also said that some of the top executive officers at ARCO including its chief executive, Michael R. Bowlin, were not likely to remain in their jobs if an agreement was reached. 
 The boards of both companies are expected to meet on Wednesday to consider the deal.
BP Amoco, which was formed last December in a merger of British Petroleum and the Amoco Corporation, forming the word's third-largest oil company, and ARCO confirmed the negotiations in a joint statement yesterday.
They cautioned, however, that an agreement had not been reached and that the discussions could still be called off.
Nevertheless, shares of ARCO surged yesterday, rising $8.6875, or 13.3 percent, to $74.0625, in heavy trading on the New York Stock Exchange. The American depository shares of BP Amoco, each of which represents six ordinary shares, were also up sharply, climbing $4.5625, or 4.5 percent, to $105. In London, BP Amoco shares rose 40 pence, or 3.9 percent, to 1,077.
ARCO, based in Los Angeles, has most of its operations on the West Coast, where it is a large refiner and seller of gasoline. It has about 26 percent of the gasoline retail market in California.
Analysts said that ARCO would flesh out BP Amoco's national refining and marketing business, which is strong in the East and Midwest but not on the West Coast.
It also has major oil and natural gas fields and production in Alaska, gas fields in Indonesia and is making an effort to get a foothold in the Caspian Sea region.
"Buying ARCO would transform BP Amoco from a very powerful regional company to a truly global oil company," said J. Robinson West, the chairman of the Petroleum Finance Company, an industry consulting group based in Washington. "In the past, they have cut costs very well, but this gives them growth as well."
The main force in building up BP Amoco has been Sir John Browne, its chief executive, who took over a slow-moving and troubled company in 1991 and helped to transform it into an industry pacesetter by sharply cutting costs and forming alliances with other oil companies.
His takeover of Amoco last August helped lead to the purchase of the Mobil Corporation by the Exxon Corporation.
In the current talks, ARCO is being advised by Goldman, Sachs & Company and Salomon Smith Barney, a unit of Citigroup. BP Amoco is being advised by Morgan Stanley Dean Witter & Company.
If Sir John were successful in buying ARCO, analysts said it would put great pressure on other large oil companies such as Texaco Inc. and the Chevron Corporation to also find merger partners. The stocks of most major oil companies rose yesterday, with shares of Texaco up $2.1875, to $57.75, and Chevron up $2.8125, to $89.625.
Executives close to the talks said yesterday that BP Amoco would offer about $28 billion in stock for ARCO.
"That's a good deal for ARCO shareholders," said Adam Sieminski, an analyst with BT Alex. Brown, because they would gain shares in a growing company.
ARCO has been severely hurt by the big fall in crude oil prices last year, which sharply lowered its earnings and led to loss in the fourth quarter. Analysts began to wonder whether it could keep paying its substantial yearly dividend of $2.85 a share.
Analysts said that one of the main reasons BP Amoco was interested in ARCO was that both companies have big holdings and production in Prudhoe Bay, Alaska, where they made major finds in the late 1960's.
ARCO has largely lived off its production there, generating money to build its refining and chemical business.
For a time the company was an industry leader, being one of the first oil companies to add convenience stores to its gas stations. It has 1,700 in seven Western states, with 900 in California. It also built two large refineries, one in Cherry Point, Wash., and the other in Carson, Calif. Both can process more than 200,000 barrels of crude oil a day.
Rather than concentrating on exploration to find another Prudhoe Bay, however, the company went into the petrochemical business and bought tankers to transport crude oil.
As the production began to run down in Alaska, the company began an aggressive program to find more oil but had disappointing results. In the 1990's, it sold its chemical business to finance more exploration and bought an independent oil company, Union Texas Holdings Inc. of Houston, last year to strengthen its overseas exploration operations.
Its refining and gas station business has also been under pressure as new low-cost independent refiners have entered its markets, and other refiners have merged their operations and cut costs.
ARCO has not joined the industry in building stations where customers can use a wide variety of credit cards to fill up at the pumps. It still relies on cash and debit cards.
Some analysts think that employees of the former Amoco, who were considered savvy marketers of gasoline, will be of great help in revamping ARCO's gasoline business.
While ARCO has been more of a no-frills seller of gasoline, it nevertheless has been making good money at it, with profits from its stations surpassing many of its larger rivals.
"We're not talking Bloomingdale's, we're talking Sam's," Mr. Sieminski said, referring to the discount retail chain. "They're price conscious and they're doing very well."

SECTION: Section A; Page 22; Column 1; Editorial Desk

LENGTH: 296 words

HEADLINE: 10,000

 BODY:
It was only last fall that stock markets in the United States and most other countries were reeling in fear of a possible worldwide recession. That it failed to appear is readily apparent from the fact that the Dow Jones industrial average yesterday closed above 10,000 for the first time -- an increase of more than 30 percent in less than seven months.
The Asian crisis that sparked the worries is far from over, and the best thing that can be said about Russia, whose default on its debt precipitated the market plunge, is that the country has not descended into anarchy. Latin American economies remain troubled, with Brazil in a severe recession. 
 Nonetheless, the developed world is optimistic. The British stock market also hit a record high this month, and most European markets have moved up as well, although not to new highs. Even long-suffering Japan seems to be reviving. One reason is that central banks in all major economies lowered interest rates, spurring economic growth and providing liquidity for stock market speculation.
Contrary to the expectations of most economists -- and of this page -- the American economy did not even pause while much of the world was suffering. Instead, consumers benefited from lower prices for commodities and imported goods, and their willingness to spend rather than save more than offset weakness in the manufacturing sector.
There are ample signs of speculative excess in the stock market, most notably in Internet stocks. Corporate profits have not risen nearly as rapidly as stock prices, a fact that is likely to be important when economic growth starts to slow. But for now, America is in love with the market and happy to see rising stock prices as an endorsement of its supremacy in the world economy.

HEADLINE: U.S. Eases Stance on the Eviction of Landowners in Oneidas' Suit

BYLINE: By DAVID W. CHEN

 BODY:
The Federal Government moved yesterday to soften its support of the Oneida Indians' claim to 270,000 acres of land in central New York, saying it did not want to evict any private landowners from their homes.
A Department of Justice official said that the Government still supported the Oneidas' original claim that state and local governments had acquired Oneida lands in the late 1700's and early 1800's through illegal and coercive treaties. The official, speaking on the condition of anonymity, reiterated that the 20,000 or so landowners who lived within the land-claim area were still defendants in the lawsuit, and therefore liable for any future damages. 
 But in recent months, the specter of eviction -- or ejectment, in legal terms -- had so overshadowed the lawsuit, and distracted from the parallel effort to hammer out a compromise, that the Justice Department felt that it was imperative to assure landowners that eviction was not a viable part of the solution, the official said. So yesterday, the Department of Justice formally asked for permission to amend its complaint during opening arguments in Federal District Court in Syracuse.
It was not immediately clear when Judge Neal McCurn would rule on the Government's request. But if the request is granted -- which seems all but certain -- then it could help assuage the anxiety that has been percolating in recent months in Madison and Oneida Counties, where there have been boycotts of Oneida businesses, protest meetings and personal threats and uncivil language.
"They are listening. They are paying attention, and I think that's very significant," said Representative Sherwood Boehlert, a Republican from Utica, summing up his discussions with Government officials. "I think this takes away the cloud of uncertainty that's hovering over all the private property owners in the region that someone is going to knock on their door and say, 'Get out!' "
Others, including Gov. George E. Pataki, want the Government to go even further.
"The Federal Government's decision to back down from its outrageous attempt to frighten and intimidate the people of central New York by seeking to eject them from their homes is a small first step, but not nearly enough," Mr. Pataki said in a statement. He added that the Government "must end its aggressive campaign against its own citizens by completely removing all homeowners from the land claim lawsuit."
Leon R. Koziol, a lawyer for Upstate Citizens for Equality, a landowners group that says it has 3,500 members, was even more blunt. While he said he believed that the Government's request took a little bit of the steam out of the Oneidas' claim, he also blamed the Government for stirring up fear and resentment in the first place.
"They used the people as pawns," Mr. Koziol said. "It was an unconscionable act, to use their own citizens as pawns in a scheme to raise the level of political discussion and legal argument in a lawsuit."
A spokeswoman for the Oneida Indian Nation, Carole Trimble, characterized the Government's request as inconsequential to the merits of the case. She restated the Oneidas' hope that they can reach a resolution that is fair to all sides.
The dispute between the Oneidas and the state officially dates from 1970, when the Oneidas filed a lawsuit in Federal District Court saying that their land had been seized illegally. In 1985, the Supreme Court upheld the Oneidas' claim. But talks between the Oneidas and the state stalled for years.
So in December, to prod the negotiations along, the Federal Government sided with the Oneidas, filing an amended complaint that named 20,000 landowners as defendants. And that, in turn, unleashed a latent sense of resentment over the Oneidas' recent good fortunes.
Like all federally recognized tribes, the Oneida Indian Nation does not pay taxes. And thanks in part to their new Turning Stone Casino, the Oneidas have seemingly risen from abject poverty to unbridled affluence in a very short time, while their neighbors have been grappling with a tough local economy.
To vent their frustration, more than 2,000 motorists participated in a rally in January in front of the casino. An even larger one is scheduled for May 1, when Mr. Koziol said he expected that 5,000 cars would travel from Buffalo to Albany to protest the Oneidas.
The next court hearing on the land-claim issue is scheduled for May 26. The court-appointed mediator, Ronald J. Riccio, who was brought in last month to try and broker a compromise, has arranged two mediation sessions and has visited the land-claim area, and said he planned more visits and more meetings. Mr. Riccio, the mediator who is also dean of Seton Hall University School of Law, described the progress as encouraging, even suggesting that there might be some kind of agreement in mid-May.
"It's possible; I'm not ruling it out," Mr. Riccio said. "At least what's possible is you can come up with the broad framework of a settlement. I think the actual implementation would take some time, but you want everyone to say, 'Yes, these are the components of a settlement,' and I think we're at the stage where I think everybody has a good idea of what the components are."

SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1395 words

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: Market Place -- Does the Dow Measure Up?;
Despite Growth of Rivals the Old Gauge Still Matters

BYLINE: By EDWARD WYATT

 BODY:
It is a staple of the nightly news, of daily discussions at dry cleaners and doughnut shops, overheard in cell-phone conversations and in cocktail party chatter.
When Americans talk about the stock market, they talk about the Dow Jones industrial average. For roughly a century "How'd the market do today?" has inevitably been answered with a reference to the Dow. 
 Yet for all its longevity and accuracy as a barometer of the progress of the stock market, the 30 stocks of the Dow industrial average have, in recent years, failed to keep up with the gains of smaller, more sought after companies that are part of other indexes that track the overall market. Since the beginning of 1995, the Dow has lagged behind by a few percentage points each year the returns of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, the broader index that most professional investors on Wall Street use as a benchmark for their own portfolios.
The Dow has fallen even further behind the Nasdaq composite index, rich with the technology stocks that lately have been the market's biggest gainers. Even the unwieldy Wilshire 5000, the universe of stocks which, despite its name, encompasses more than 7,000 companies based in the United States, has climbed at a rate more than 1 percent a year faster than has the Dow.
All of which raises the question: Does the Dow matter anymore?
The answer is still yes.
"The Dow has come in for a lot of criticism for a long time," said Steven Leuthold, a market analyst whose firm, Leuthold Group, is based in Minneapolis. "People complain that it has a limited number of stocks; they complain about the way the index is calculated," based on the numerical price of stocks rather than the total market value of the companies it tracks.
"But I think the rather amazing thing is how accurately, over time, it has tracked a broader index like the S.& P. 500," Mr. Leuthold added. "There are times when the two get out of sync. But we use it because it is still the first thing people refer to when they talk about how the market did today."
So far this year, the Dow, with a gain of nearly 9 percent -- and closing above 10,000 for the first time yesterday -- is more than a percentage point ahead of the S.& P. 500. But those gains mask a growing disparity in the overall market. While the major stock indexes are trading at or near record highs, three-quarters of the stocks in the S.& P. index, and 88 percent of the stocks traded on the New York Stock Exchange, are more than 10 percent below their all-time highs.
The differences between the gauges can be traced to how they are calculated. The Dow industrial average is price-weighted, meaning that high-priced stocks, like I.B.M. at over $177 a share, have more of an effect on the Dow than do low-priced stocks like Disney, at about $33. That is true even though a company with a low share price but substantially more shares in circulation could be worth more.
In contrast, the S.& P. indexes are weighted by market capitalization, meaning that the indexes are most influenced by companies whose total stock market value -- derived by multiplying their share price by the number of shares in circulation -- is greatest.
A system like the Dow's price-based system was necessary in the late 19th century, when the Dow was first compiled and the calculations had to be done with pencil and paper. The first calculations, in 1896 with a group of 12 stocks, were very simple: add the stocks' prices and divide by 12.
Today, the average is calculated by dividing the sum of the prices by a fractional divisor, currently about 0.225. That means that each $1 change in the price of a Dow stock moves the average by about 4.44 points.
With the advent of superfast computers and instant reporting of changes in stock prices, any index can be calculated in a microsecond, adjusted for stock splits and dividends. More importantly for the companies that devise and market indexes for use with options, futures and other derivatives contracts, an index can be frequently tinkered with through the removal and replacement of companies without difficulty.
That has caused new indexes to sprout like mushrooms, with each seeming to slice the stock market into ever-smaller pieces. Bloomberg lists 417 indexes tracking United States equity markets alone.
From a statistical perspective, however, the Dow is among the most efficient gauges around. It uses only 30 stocks, which together account for close to a quarter of the stock market's total value. The S.& P. 500's components, which together account for about four-fifths of the market's total value, are, by comparison, much closer to a full census than to a sampling.
Recently, critics of the Dow have argued that the older gauge, having missed the upswing caused by the Internet investment mania, lags behind too far. From the beginning of 1995, when the latest leg of the stock market's upward march began, through the end of 1998, the Dow climbed 24.3 percent annually, compared with 27.8 percent for the S.& P. 500.
The difference stems primarily from the absence of the technology giants Intel and Microsoft from the Dow industrials. While the Dow has contained I.B.M. since 1981 and Hewlett-Packard since 1997, those stocks have not performed as well as Intel and Microsoft, the stars of the new economy built around personal computers and the Internet.
Over longer periods, however, the gauges track quite closely. Since the beginning of 1980 through the end of last year, the Dow gained 13.4 percent a year, compared with 13.6 percent for the S.& P. 500. Including reinvested dividends, a measure known as total return, an investment in the Dow and the S.& P. 500 would have come even closer -- an average annual gain of 17.72 percent for the Dow industrials versus 17.65 percent for the S.& P.
Even when measured since the beginning of 1990, the two benchmarks differ by an average of less than a half of 1 percent a year: 17.8 percent for the S.& P. 500 versus 17.4 percent for the Dow.
The proliferation of indexes in part comes from the desire to more closely track certain sectors of the market.
"It depends what you want a benchmark to be," said Leah Modigliani, an equity strategist at Morgan Stanley. "It serves to represent a universe of investments, to act as a benchmark" for investment managers to measure their own performance.
Increasingly, however, stock market gauges have also provided an alternative investment, one that frequently can be had at a lower cost than that charged by traditional portfolio managers. Today, index mutual funds and unit investment trusts track virtually every stock market gauge imaginable, from subsets of Internet stocks to the entire universe of publicly traded companies.
"Without a doubt, the Dow has a lot of problems that are well known," said Theodore R. Aronson, a money manager and market analyst at Aronson + Partners in Philadelphia. "I nonetheless marvel at how experts in the field allude to it. And it's still hands-down the most popular shorthand for how the market's done. I wake up each morning as National Public Radio is reporting the Dow, which they still do to two decimal places. Think about how meaningless that is."
Indeed, said Mr. Leuthold, people seem to be increasingly frightened by the Dow's amplitude. Hundred-point swings in the Dow often result in bigger headlines in the next day's paper, even though 100 points recently represented little more than a move of 1 percent in the average.
With the Dow crossing 10,000, perhaps the excitement that has come with each 1,000-point mark will die down. But the 10,000 level might also represent a level that has greater historic resonance to investors. For once it reaches the Promised Land, will the Dow be able to move on?
Consider that for six periods from 1963 to 1982, the Dow Jones industrial average approached or crossed the 1,000 level, only to retreat back into triple digits. The first of those concluded a fantastic run of three and a half years, during which the Dow gained more than 20 percent a year.
The Dow first finished a full day of trading above 1,000 in November 1972. But it was not until a full decade later, at the beginning of the bull market in late 1982, that the Dow average firmly climbed above the 1,000 mountain. Today, of course, it looks more like a molehill.
