SECTION: Section C; Page 1; Column 2; Business/Financial Desk

LENGTH: 1535 words

HEADLINE: Company's Plan to Privatize Angers Shareholders

BYLINE: By JOSEPH KAHN

 BODY:
Even in a raging bull market, some New York Stock Exchange companies wallow in neglect. A few, like dusty antiques in the attic, turn out to be gems.
The PEC Israel Economic Corporation, a holding company that has stakes in Israeli ventures, is one such company. Management takes a low profile. Analysts ignore the company. The stock price has gone mostly downhill since the early 1990's. So forgotten is PEC that it is slated for extinction, with its largest shareholder preparing to buy up the remaining shares and delist it as of today. 
 The delisting, however, comes as several PEC investments, including a stake in Cellcom, Israel's largest mobile telephone operator, are scheduled to go public and capture a market value that analysts believe will greatly exceed the amount that PEC says they are worth.
The Discount Investments Corporation, a company controlled by the Recanati family of Israel, is the majority PEC shareholder that wants to privatize PEC. By doing so, it would not have to share any future gains in the market value of PEC assets with other stockholders, disgruntled shareholders contend.
The offer to privatize PEC was made several months after Goldman, Sachs & Company, the investment bank, paid $78 million for a 9.5 percent stake in the Israeli holding company that controls Discount Investments. Goldman, jointly with Lehman Brothers, also happens to be a lead underwriter of Cellcom, which plans to list shares in the United States shortly.
Minority PEC shareholders have filed lawsuits to block the deal, contending that the offer substantially underestimates the value of the investments PEC has made over the years. Unless the suits prevail, the delisting is a done deal because Discount Investments already holds an 80 percent stake in PEC. Shareholders will formally vote on the proposal at a shareholder meeting today in New York.
The conflict involves a tangled story of American-Israeli economic connections that turned sour. Some shareholders see the demise of PEC as an ignominious end to a company founded in 1926 by Justice Louis Brandeis of the Supreme Court. He and other Zionists established the company to support Palestine at a time when Jews there had difficulty raising money for even the most basic necessities, like housing and utilities.
In fact, until the Israeli stock market boom of the 1990's made it easier for Israeli companies to raise capital, PEC was run as much as a charity as a corporation. Its stock is still widely held by Jews around the world who once regarded it as much as a donation as an investment.
"This company was never supposed to be about squeezing every last dime out of shareholders," said Murray S. Greenfield, a retired 72-year-old Israeli broker who sold PEC stock to Jewish visitors to Israel in the 1960's. "It was about building Israel. If Brandeis woke up today he would not be the happiest man in the world, I can tell you that."
For more recent shareholders, PEC's privatization is a fresh lesson about the risks of investing in undervalued companies. Some investors said they snapped up PEC stock in recent years because the price fell far below the level at which analysts estimated the value of its underlying investments. These investors were hoping PEC would eventually enjoy a run-up in the value of Israeli companies that went public after the 1993 peace accord with the Palestinians.
Like PEC, some investment holding companies trade at a discount to the book value of their underlying assets, especially when management is indifferent. But PEC's discount -- more than 46 percent, according to an estimate made by an analyst at Lehman Brothers late last year -- was large enough to make PEC a prime target for investors seeking the most undervalued companies on the United States stock market.
"The pity is that this is happening just as some of PEC's investments are just about to take off," said one large shareholder who declined to be identified because his company has legal action pending. Among the largest minority shareholders are mutual funds run by Credit Suisse Asset Management and the Vanguard Group.
PEC's management team, which includes two members of the Recanati family, declined to comment through a spokesman on the lawsuits or the buyout offer, citing Securities and Exchange Commission regulations that they say prohibit them from speaking publicly while the offer is pending. Goldman, which stands to benefit if the Recanatis have their way at PEC, also declined to comment.
But in documents, the company says that the $30-a-share purchase price for the shares of PEC that Discount Investments does not already own is fair, pointing out that the price is 32 percent higher than what the shares were fetching before Discount Investments made a bid.
Late last year, PEC management hired Merrill Lynch & Company to appraise its value. After Merrill's review, Discount Investment raised its bid to $30 a share from $25.50. Merrill called the $30 price fair, a fact that PEC officials said validates the latest offer.
But some shareholders fault Merrill for collecting as much as $625,000 from PEC in fees if the deal flies, but failing to extract from PEC management the sort of information Merrill normally seeks to judge the fairness of a deal. Merrill acknowledged in its fairness opinion, for example, that it did not have access to the management of Cellcom, the mobile phone operator and what many consider the portfolio's hidden jewel.
"We have not been afforded the opportunity to meet with the management of Cellcom Israel, the company's largest single holding, and thus have not been able to discuss with such management the business prospects of Cellcom," the Merrill review states.
Israeli newspapers have reported that Cellcom plans to go public in the United States this year. Both Merrill and Goldman competed for the right to underwrite that company's shares, which are expected to generate considerable investor interest because of Cellcom's rapid growth and its status as the No. 1 provider of mobile phone services in Israel, itself a leader in per-capita use of cellular telephones.
In its fairness opinion, dated Dec. 11, Merrill explored various ways to value Cellcom and settled on a range of $2 billion to $2.5 billion. More recently, Israeli news reports and Wall Street analysts close to the company have placed a value on Cellcom closer to $3.5 billion.
Merrill officials declined to comment in detail on the fairness opinion. But a spokesman said that the opinion explored the reasons that PEC shares traded at such a heavy discount to the value of its individual investments. It also weighs the possibility that Cellcom's plan to go public could be delayed by a downdraft in the American stock market, as it was late last year.
Shareholders opposed to Discount Investments' offer contend that the discrepancy between Merrill's valuation of Cellcom and the price underwriters now hope to achieve in the initial public offering amounts to $11 a share for PEC's current holders. Discount Investments, they say, should pay at least $41 a share instead of the $30 being proposed.
The dissenting shareholders also say that the understatement of PEC holdings goes beyond Cellcom and extends to PEC's stakes in several private companies and real estate holdings in Israel.
The offer to take PEC public, these shareholders say, does not account for a recent spike in the value of its stake in Gilat Satellite Networks, which just went public on the Nasdaq market last month. Another example: PEC has the right to convert a loan it made to United Pan-Europe Communications, a European cable television company, into shares in that prosperous concern. Shareholders say they consider that conversion right a lucrative opportunity that they will miss if PEC is privatized anytime soon.
Evidence that some long-dormant PEC investments are starting to ascend can be seen in the value of Discount Investment stock listed on the Tel Aviv stock exchange, the shareholders said. Discount Investments' assets mirror many of those held by PEC, as the two invest side by side in many ventures. The two, for example, each hold 12.5 percent stakes in Cellcom, and have identical positions in many other Israeli technology and industrial companies.
Discount Investment stock has been on a tear, with shares rising from less than 80 shekels, or about $19, at the time it offered to buy the shares of PEC it did not already control last fall to more than 140, or about $34.68, in recent days, an all-time high. PEC shares on the New York Stock Exchange, which closed yesterday at $29.6875, rose in December when Discount Investment made its $30-a-share offer, but are still well below 1993 highs.
For now, the issue seems destined to end up in court. But for some shareholders, it's an unfortunate paradox that American Jews who once bought PEC shares to support cash-short Jews in Palestine would wind up in court contending that Israeli financiers are profiting at their expense.
"It's a shame," said Mr. Greenfield, the former broker and PEC shareholder. "But if they are hellbent on profits, then at least we should have our share."

DATELINE: CHICAGO, March 29

 BODY:
The Bank One Corporation, the nation's fifth-largest bank, plans to cut as many as 2,200 jobs, or 2.4 percent of its work force, by the middle of next year as a result of its merger with the First Chicago NBD Corporation, a spokesman said.

 Bank One was formed by last year's merger of the Banc One Corporation and First Chicago NBD. The company last month said it would cut 200 people, or 20 percent of its staff outside the United States. Those cuts are included in the 2,200 figure.

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Dow Closes Above 10,000, Reflecting Strong Economy
After nearly two weeks of thrust and parry, stock traders pushed the Dow Jones industrial average above 10,000 and kept it there until the end of the day's trading. The Dow closed at 10,006.78, up 184.54 points, or 1.9 percent.
Although 10,000 on the Dow was little more than a psychological hurdle for investors, the market's move is significant in what it reflects: unparalleled strength of the economy and American corporations' dominance of the world stage. Page A1. 

Almost lost in the shuffle of the Dow's record-setting day was a solid performance by broader gauges. The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index rose 27.37 points, or 2.1 percent, to 1,310.17, while the Nasdaq composite index jumped 73.67 points, or 3 percent, to 2,492.84. C11.
 
Microsoft Reorganizes Into 5 Units
Microsoft's president, Steven Ballmer, announced the reorganization of the company into five divisions that he suggested would act with some independence. Brad Chase, left, and Jon DeVaan will run a newly created consumer and commerce group that includes the Microsoft Network. The other new units are the Business and Enterprise Division, the Consumer Windows Division, the Business Productivity Group for "knowledge workers" like telecommuters, and the Developer Group. C2.
 
A Virus Writer Strikes Again
A software detective who has assembled a dossier of the author of a rapidly spreading computer virus, called Melissa, thinks the virus writer has struck previously and that his activities are already well known to computer security experts. A14.
 
Kodak Issues First-Quarter Alert
In a radical departure for the traditionally secretive company, Eastman Kodak warned that its first-quarter earnings would come in below expectations but gave detailed, and much rosier, forecasts for the rest of the year. C6.
 
Computer Associates to Buy Rival
Computer Associates International, the fourth-largest maker of software in the United States, agreed to buy a rival, Platinum Technology International, for about $3.5 billion. C8.
 
MCI Worldcom Seeks Internet Links
MCI Worldcom, seeking advanced wireless Internet links, is said to be investing about $200 million in what is known as the wireless cable business. It is buying bonds of three small wireless cable operators, CS Wireless, People's Choice and Wireless One, and shares of another, CAI Wireless Systems. C9.
 
Amazon.com Set to Offer Auctions
Furthering its ambition to be involved virtually any time anyone sells anything on the Internet, Amazon.com plans to let individuals and small businesses conduct auctions on its popular Web site, beginning today. Shares of Amazon.com rose nearly 8 percent, closing at $149.625, up $10.5625. C10.
 
KPMG to Consolidate Operations
KPMG, the fifth-largest accounting firm, said that it would consolidate most of its operations into two regional groupings as it seeks to transform a loose association of firms into a unified global business. C9.
 
OPEC Cuts May Hinder U.S. Access
Greater American access to Saudi Arabian petroleum has been put on indefinite hold, senior Saudi officials said after a new OPEC agreement was reached. That agreement, aimed at raising prices by reducing production, was essentially negotiated by Saudi Arabia. C4.
 
Olivetti Raises Telecom Italia Bid
Olivetti raised its hostile bid for a bigger rival, Telecom Italia, by 15 percent, to 60.4 billion euros, or about $64.7 billion. C4.
 
Viag and Algroup Abandon Merger
Viag, Germany's third-largest utility, and Algroup, the world's biggest maker of packaging for the pharmaceutical industry, abandoned their $8.7 billion merger plan because they could not agree on terms. C4.

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: IN BELGRADE;
Belgrade 'Targets' Find Unity 'From Heaven'

BYLINE: By STEVEN ERLANGER

DATELINE: BELGRADE, Serbia, March 29

 BODY:
Along Belgrade's loveliest shopping street, Knez Mihajlova, groups of young men filled with anger and beer today completed the trashing of the American Center, the British Council, the Goethe Institute and the French Cultural Center, as scattered policemen watched and chatted among themselves.
The impact of the stones shattering the windows seemed in rhythm with the sounds of another patriotic concert in the city center, starring Ceca Raznjatovic, wife of the notorious Serbian paramilitary leader Zeljko Raznjatovic, known as Arkan. 
 "Ceca" was famous, a Serbian Cher, before she married Arkan, a proud pioneer of ethnic cleansing in Bosnia. But she has taken to doing music videos with a live tiger cub, a reference to the name of Arkan's brigades, the Tigers.
As ordinary Belgrade residents walked by, or stood to watch the destruction, the young stone throwers also spray-painted obscene graffiti on the walls, comparing NATO to the Nazis and making snide references to President Clinton's sexual life.
Like many of the passers-by, they wore a "target" logo that has swept Belgrade -- a gesture of contempt toward NATO's bombs that is made more palatable by the fact that no NATO bombs have struck the populated center of the Yugoslav capital.
"I'm ashamed to see this," said a medical doctor dressed in a fashionable pale-beige suit who identified herself only as Zaklina. "But you know the bombs have made people crazy. They crack the veneer of civilization, as if war excuses anything."
She laughed then, saying, "Anyway, they are bored with being down in the shelters, with the grannies."
Belgrade is a city where people say "ciao" and drink espresso, judge the latest Hollywood movies with asperity and surf the Net. But the war is dragging it farther from Europe, into isolation, uniting the Serbs behind their soldiers, their Kosovo and even President Slobodan Milosevic. American movies have been withdrawn from the theaters, though the Original Levi's Store across from the American Center was left untrashed by the mobs.
"People used to say that only God could unify the Serbs," said Aleksa Djilas, the historian son of the late Milovan Djilas, the most famous dissident of the Tito period. "But now they've been unified from heaven -- but by the bombs, not by God."
No one really feels they deserve to be bombed, Mr. Djilas said, "even if they know that Milosevic's policies have played a big role."
At the same time, the Government has urged citizens to run to the air raid shelters for 12 or 15 hours a day, understanding that collective panic provides a sense of solidarity with the nation and its military.
"But if you refuse to go down, or say that there is no point in it, people get very angry," said Ilija, a former journalist in his 50's. "People think you're belittling their plight or somehow saying that it's all right that they're sitting in a shelter."
There is much more suspicion around, with rumors of Western spies and local dissidents planting radar guidance devices to insure that NATO's bombs find their targets. Some Serbs insist, conversely, that some devices have been planted in the hotels where journalists are staying, intended not to guide the bombs, but to deflect them.
And the city authorities, while deciding now that it is safer to keep Belgrade's street lights on, still insist that curtains be drawn after 6 P.M., as if NATO's modern weapons still depended on illuminated living rooms to find their targets.
Still, as Yugoslavia faces its sixth day of bombing without major civilian casualties, people are becoming inured to the sharp wail of the sirens. Fewer spend all night in the shelters, but there is little else to do, with nearly all the restaurants, clubs, cafes and movie theaters shutting down at dusk.
"There is a deepening sense of boredom -- the war is really very boring, especially with this monotonous war propaganda on the news," said a cosmopolitan Serb, who spoke on the condition of anonymity. "My son is bored; he can't go out, except to visit friends in their shelters, where it's boring. My little one is bored, because all her favorite cartoons like Power Rangers have been replaced by these interminable news programs."
So people are renting armfuls of videos, especially comedies, like "You've Got Mail."
Yet the Serbs remain resourceful and ironic. A basement cafe downtown has renamed itself "The Shelter Cafe," offering satellite television along with coffee and drinks. A placard put up in a window along Knez Mihajlova read: "F-117A. Crashed. Low mileage. Foreign license plates. Cheap. Call after 4 P.M."
Instead of "Dobra den," or good day, people wish each other "Bombar den."
The target placards worn by so many here were printed various ways, but some come straight off the Internet (http://www.beograd.com/ nato/target/target72.gif).
And with cigarettes so scarce, people joke, the war is a superb opportunity to quit smoking.
Some of the mockery is cruder and angrier.
A funeral notice featured Mr. Clinton and said he would be buried alongside others "in charge of destroying civilization," like "Hitler, Napoleon, Mussolini and Ante Pavelic," the Croatian fascist leader during World War II. Mr. Clinton is survived, the notice said, "by wife Hillary, daughter Chelsea, mom, dad, dog, necktie, friend Monica and lots of Tomahawks and radar locators and a big family of NATO rats."
The Milosevic Government, through its censorship of the news media, has isolated Belgrade and the rest of Serbia proper from the real war going on to the south, in Kosovo.
While state television shows careful film of NATO damage, there is little effort to discuss the activities of the military and security forces in Kosovo. While Western politicians and human-rights officials are making harsh accusations of atrocities, killings and "ethnic cleansing" of the ethnic Albanian population, Serbs are informed that their forces are simply responding to the widespread terrorism of the rebel Kosovo Liberation Army and its sympathizers.
Mr. Djilas, the historian, said he considered that the coming visit by Prime Minister Yevgeny M. Primakov of Russia to be a way for President Boris N. Yeltsin of Russia to deal with fierce domestic criticism of NATO and doubted that it would produce anything substantive.
But it would also add to what Zoran Djindjic, the leader of the Democratic Party here -- and an organizer of pro-democracy demonstrations against Mr. Milosevic two years ago -- called the "mental filters of censorship and self-censorship" that protect ordinary people from the reality of atrocity.
"The essence of Milosevic's system is that no one knows anything," Mr. Djindjic said. "We don't know how the economy is doing or what the Zastava factory is producing or how many are unemployed, let alone what is happening in Kosovo."
It is the same process that allowed the Germans under Nazism to discount evidence of the Holocaust, Mr. Djindjic said.
"People first need to hear information," he said. "Then, if they hear it, will they believe it? And if they believe it, how can they explain it?" As they try to explain it to themselves, he said, people will think "Maybe the victims are guilty, somehow, and deserve what they get.' "
Mr. Djindjic, Belgrade's reform Mayor for a few months in 1997, said it was important to understand how much Mr. Milosevic depends on a rural constituency that has always hated Belgrade, the sophisticated metropolis. Yugoslavia's civil wars, over the last decade, have brought more and more angry and impoverished Serbs here from Bosnia and Croatia.
"There's more of a rural fatalism now," he said. "There is more xenophobia, myth and superstition. All of this is only accelerated by the war."

HEADLINE: Oil vs. Wilderness

 BODY:

To the Editor:
In the name of protecting Alaska's landscape, you support the Congressional proposal to designate the last sliver of the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge as wilderness (editorial, March 24).
This designation, you argue, would also help prevent calamities like the Exxon Valdez oil spill. 
 We are 56 percent dependent on foreign oil. If we do not continue to seek domestic sources of oil, this dependence will grow. Moreover, oil arriving by foreign tankers increases our exposure to accidents like the Exxon Valdez.
Of the Arctic National Wildlife Refuge's 18.6 million acres, only 1.5 million acres have been set aside for oil exploration. Alaska is already home to 60 percent of the country's wilderness areas. Designating more of the state's land as wilderness would only cripple the area's economic well-being, including that of the Inupiat Eskimos.
 
FRANK H. MURKOWSKI
Chmn., Senate Energy and Natural Resources Committee
Washington, March 25, 1999

HEADLINE: COMPANY NEWS;
LOCKHEED MARTIN UNIT IS CUTTING 1,200 JOBS

BYLINE: AP

 BODY:
Lockheed Martin Astronautics has announced plans to cut 1,200 jobs by the end of the year, including a 30 percent reduction in management positions. Astronautics, an operating unit of Lockheed Martin's Space and Strategic Missiles Sector, makes space and military systems, including launching systems and ground systems. The job cuts amount to about 10 percent of the total work force at the Denver-based facility but include 300 that have already been dropped through attrition. Most of the cuts will come gradually throughout the rest of the year, but the management positions will be dropped by the end of June. Lockheed Martin, with headquarters in Bethesda, Md., employs more than 165,000 people worldwide.

LENGTH: 1661 words

HEADLINE: DOW FINISHES DAY OVER 10,000 MARK FOR THE FIRST TIME

BYLINE: By GRETCHEN MORGENSON

 BODY:
The Dow achieved a perfect 10 yesterday. Thousand, that is.
After nearly two weeks of thrust and parry, traders pushed the Dow Jones industrial average above 10,000 and kept it there until trading ended. The Dow closed at 10,006.78, up 184.54 points, or 1.9 percent. 
 Although 10,000 is little more than a psychological hurdle for investors, the market's move is significant in what it reflects: the unparalleled strength of the economy and the dominance of the world economic stage by American corporations.
More and more of the world's profits are being claimed by the nation's corporations. "Back in 1990 the U.S. share was 36 percent; last year, it was 44 percent," said Jeffrey M. Applegate, chief investment strategist at Lehman Brothers in New York. "That goes a long way toward explaining why U.S. equities have had such exemplary performance."
The Dow has doubled in less than four years; it reached 5,000 in November 1995. It is up nearly tenfold since 1982.
"The market's ability to sustain itself through all sorts of climates and economic conditions -- inflation/ deflation, growth/lack of growth -- has been impressive," said Laszlo Birinyi, president of Birinyi & Associates in Greenwich, Conn., and a global trading consultant for Deutsche Bank. "The ease and the consistency: that's what's so surprising about the market."
A lurking concern, however, is just how narrow the market's advance has been. A growing portion of Americans' investment money is devoted to the 30 well-known companies in the Dow and the components of the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index, which rose near its record yesterday. But many portfolios have not matched the performance of the Dow and the S.& P. That is because a relatively small number of stocks have pulled the indexes higher. The winners have been the best-known stocks with household names, stocks like General Electric, American Express and Wal-Mart Stores.
The number of stocks that are down significantly in the broad market far outpaces the number that are up substantially.
Fully 76 percent of all stocks are trailing the S.& P.'s return by 15 percent or more. On the Nasdaq, 93 percent of stocks now fetch prices 10 percent or more below their highs of the last 52 weeks, while 88 percent of the stocks on the New York Stock Exchange are in that category. Among the S.& P. stocks, three out of four are at least 10 percent off their highs.
According to statisticians at Salomon Smith Barney, the stocks in the S.& P. 500 were on average 22.6 percent off their highs as of Friday, while the New York Stock Exchange issues were on average 33.4 percent below their peaks. Showing just how concentrated this rally has been among big stocks, the Nasdaq stocks are on average 41.8 percent below their peaks. And this calculation factors in many of the hot technology and Internet stocks.
Neither has the road to Dow 10,000 been pothole free. The financial crisis that began in Asia with the devaluation of the Thai baht in July 1997 and migrated to Russia last summer and then to Brazil gave stock market investors their first taste of significant losses in eight years. But three cuts in interest rates by the Federal Reserve Board turned stocks around last fall.
Economic problems still simmer overseas: fourth-quarter data from Japan, Germany and much of Latin America show declines in gross domestic products there. But difficulties abroad have only served to make the United States stock market more of a haven to investors worldwide. Because the United States is almost alone in experiencing solid growth now, stocks of big American companies are viewed as enticing. Even the increasingly grim situation in Kosovo did not hold back investors yesterday.
Ralph J. Acampora, director of technical research at Prudential Securities, was an early Wall Street believer in Dow 10,000. On March 16, when the average first topped 10,000 and then retreated, Mr. Acampora exulted: "It's exciting. It's America. We all should get up and sing 'God Bless America.' These are our big stocks that are leading. It's anything but irrational."
The stock market surely is America today. An estimated 45 percent of American households own equities, up from about 14 percent in 1980.
And Americans who have been in the market during the 1990's have been riding a superpowered engine of wealth creation. Over the last decade, according to the Federal Reserve System, the value of stocks held by households is up 381 percent, before inflation, handily beating real estate assets, which were up 55 percent. The value of stocks held by households rose to $10.77 trillion last year and represented 25 percent of total household assets, up from 8 percent 15 years ago.
The increasing enthusiasm for stocks has been well founded. For the last five years, the compounded annual return on the S.& P. 500, including dividends, has averaged 24 percent, more than double the 10.9 percent median annual increase in the index since 1926.
As for the period ahead, Mr. Acampora predicts that the Dow will hit 11,500 this year, a 15 percent gain from yesterday's close.
Behind the long bull market are several factors -- declining interest rates, low inflation, increasing productivity as a result of advancements in technology -- that have helped propel corporations to new levels of efficiency.
"The lower rate structure has clearly been the majority of strength in the market," said Ned Riley Jr., chief investment officer at BankBoston. He contends that lower rates account for 80 percent of the return in the stock market this decade. "Only 20 percent was the result of expanding earnings," he said.
But interest rates would never have fallen had inflation not dwindled. On an annualized basis, consumer prices are rising 1.6 percent, well below the average annual rate of 3.2 percent since 1926.
One reason inflation is virtually nonexistent even after years of economic strength is that labor costs remain in the cellar. So even as the economy expands, usually a precursor to higher inflation, there is little threat of overall price increases.
Other factors behind the push to Dow 10,000 include an increase in the money supply, falling commodity prices and a very recent and large decline in total shares outstanding. In the fourth quarter of last year, for example, the total shares outstanding shrank by $178 billion, meaning investors had fewer stocks from which to choose.
Still, the rising tides of both the Dow and the S.& P. index have not been enough to lift all investors' fortunes. With so many stocks underwater, the market's rally sits on thinner and thinner pilings.
It is in fact a worry to many strategists that the market has become even more bifurcated -- a few haves and many have-nots -- than it was last year.
While investors rhapsodize over big, famous-name stocks like Microsoft and McDonald's, smaller issues are scorned. The Russell 2000 index of small-capitalization stocks is down 7.7 percent for the year.
But Timothy J. Morris, chief investment officer of Bessemer Trust in New York, says that this market split makes sense. "You could make a very strong argument that investors have acted rationally, rewarding companies that have very strong earnings gains and penalizing ones that haven't," he said.
Nonetheless, some of the forces that have helped push the market higher have been losing steam in recent weeks. That leads some market analysts to wonder how much more investors can expect from stocks this year.
The growth in the nation's money supply has slowed substantially this year. That growth in money, as measured in the broadest sense to include money market funds, currency in circulation, checking and savings accounts, helped stabilize the stock market during the financial crises last fall. Much of the additional money found its way into equities. A decline in that growth could put a lid on stocks. Early this year, according to Christine Callies, equity strategist at Credit Suisse First Boston, annualized growth in money supply fell to 8.4 percent from 16.4 percent.
Interest rates have also been nudged higher by bond traders, nervous that strong economic growth in the United States could ignite inflation. The yield on the 30-year Treasury bond stands at 5.64 percent, well above the 5.10 percent level at the start of the year. The jump could be temporary, of course, but every increase in rates makes bonds slightly more attractive when compared with stocks.
Another worry is that violent price swings in stocks are increasing. According to the Leuthold Group, in the first two months of the year, the S.& P. closed each day with an up or down move of 1 percent or more 45 percent of the time. Between 1992 and 1998, such moves took place only 8 percent of the time.
Even as the Dow reached the milestone yesterday, Tom Telegades, who said he works for an Internet company, shrugged off its importance on his way through Grand Central Terminal. "I'm in the market, but it has no impact on me whatsoever," he said. "It doesn't make any difference in my investment strategy. I'm not doing anything extra because of it."
Such tepid enthusiasm has not been the norm in recent years. According to James Paulsen, chief investment officer at Wells Capital Management in Minneapolis, during the last five years, declines in the stock market have occurred on about one out of four days. Only during the bull stampede beginning in 1926 and extending to 1929 was there less downward movement.
This outsized performance has also put stocks at valuations never seen before. The stocks in the S.& P. 500 are trading at 34 times their earnings, more than double the median historical valuation of around 15 times. And the value of the stock market now exceeds the gross domestic product by a sizable amount.
Regardless, United States stocks are for now very much the place to be. Eight years of rising share prices are a powerful draw.

"Lately, Stocks Have Been the Road to Riches"
In the years after the Dow Jones industrial average first neared 1,000 in the 1960's, stock prices failed to keep up with the growth of the overall economy, as shown by the gross domestic product; with inflation, as shown by the Consumer Price Index; with house prices or with gold. But in recent years stock prices have risen much more rapidly than the other measures, and have now exceeded 1960's peaks for all except the G.D.P. In the following charts, a rising line indicates the Dow did better than the other measure during the period.
 Graph tracks the Dow Jones industrial average, indexed to the following: the gross domestic product; single-family home prices; gold prices; the consumer price index. Data tracked since 1965. (pg. C12)
 
"By Any Measure The Dow Is on a Roll"
The Dow Jones industrial's performance has been extraordinary whether graphed on a conventional scale, right, or on a logarithmic scale, left, which gives the same visual weight to comparable percentage changes.
The bull market that began in 1990 is not as great in percentage terms as the bull market that began in 1921. And the bear market that began in 1929 was much deeper than the brief crash of 1987.

HEADLINE: Monterrey Journal;
Beisbol, Si! But Can U.S. Players Drink the Water?

BYLINE: By SAM DILLON 

DATELINE: MONTERREY, Mexico

 BODY:
Major League Baseball's first season opener to be played outside the United States or Canada is scheduled for Monterrey on April 4. The game has aroused so much excitement that tickets sold out in three hours when they went on sale here last month.
Mexican fans hope that the opening game, between the San Diego Padres and the Colorado Rockies, means that the major leagues could one day have an expansion team in Monterrey, Mexico's baseball capital. Major league executives say only that the game is part of their campaign to globalize baseball. 
"We're making an enormous statement by opening the season in Mexico," said a vice president of Major League Baseball, Timothy J. Brosnan. "It's part of our plan to grow the game worldwide. Mexico is our neighbor, and there's a great Latin baseball tradition. We want to bring the game to our fans."
For half a century, Mexico's baseball leagues have served as a farm system for American players and a training ground for Mexican greats like Fernando Valenzuela, even as they have delighted Mexican fans.
When major league executives decided this year to schedule the game in Mexico, it was little surprise that Monterrey was chosen for the game.
Monterrey Stadium is Mexico's largest and most comfortable baseball park, seating 25,000 spectators. The Monterrey Sultanes are recurring champions, and the city has 50 Little League networks, as well as the Mexican Baseball Hall of Fame.
The game will open the 1999 season for the major leagues. But the season for Mexico's summer league, which has 16 teams affiliated with the United States' minor leagues, started on March 17. The Sultanes met the Saraperos of Saltillo, an automobile-manufacturing city an hour's drive west through the desert.
Before the Mexican opener, fans were munching not hot dogs, but platefuls of peanuts smothered in chile sauce. Otherwise the baseball rituals were familiar.
Families wept during a scoreboard video tribute to fabled Mexican baseball stars. An army bugler played Mexico's national anthem as the sun dipped behind the Sierra Madre over the right field fence, and the Governor of the State of Nuevo Leon threw out the first pitch.
"Play ball!" the announcer beckoned in English.
The Sultans-Saraperos game drew 12,000 fans, partly because even in Monterrey, soccer overshadows baseball in Mexico. And many working-class fans poured savings into buying tickets for the major league opener and could not afford to buy tickets for the Mexican opener as well.
Tickets for regular Mexican League games, including the March 17 opener, range from 50 pesos, about $5, for seats behind home plate to 10 pesos, about $1, for the outfield bleachers. Tickets for the Padres-Rockies game ranged from $60 for the best seats to $10 for the bleachers, about twice the daily minimum wage earned by many laborers.
"I got mine," said Sigifredo Cantu, 32, the owner of a trucking company who was seated in a box along the third base line for the Mexican League opener. "I watch fun baseball in this stadium twice a week. But what's lacking here is really great baseball."
Mexico has had outstanding baseball in times past.
In the spring of 1946, Jorge Pasquel, a Mexican businessman, and his four brothers offered lucrative salaries to lure American major leaguers to the Mexican League.
When more than a dozen players -- among them pitchers Sal Maglie of the New York Giants and Max Lanier of the St. Louis Cardinals, and Mickey Owen, a Brooklyn Dodgers catcher -- went to Mexico, the major leagues imposed a five-year ban on those who defected. Most of these players soon became disillusioned as reports circulated of broken contract promises and poor playing conditions in Mexico, and they sought to rejoin their old teams. Happy Chandler, the baseball commissioner, refused to reinstate them, and it wasn't until 1949 that the ban was lifted.
In this decade, exchanges between Mexican and United States baseball have accelerated. In 1990, the Dodgers, with Valenzuela pitching, played an exhibition series here with the Brewers, led by a Mexican pitcher, Teodoro Higuera. Four years later, a consortium of Monterrey executives applied to Major League Baseball for an expansion team, but withdrew the application when a peso devaluation damaged the Mexican economy.
In 1996, the Padres and the Mets played a series of regular season games here, the first time that had happened outside the United States and Canada.
Now, the major league opener has revived talk about a Mexican expansion team.
"Having the major leagues open their season in Monterrey is a point of pride for us," Gov. Fernando Canales Clariond of Nuevo Leon said after throwing out the first pitch on March 17. "We have great fans and we feel very close to baseball. But the cost of sustaining a major league team -- well, it's high. So it's a question of working out the arithmetic."
Pat Courtney, a spokesman, said Major League Baseball, which has two teams in Canada, said the company had "no concrete plans for the foreseeable future" to expand into Mexico.
"But is it an area that's talked about?" he asked. "Yes, it is."
The Sultanes owner, Jose Maiz Garcia, who filed the application in 1994, said it was too early for Monterrey to think about refiling. "Not yet," Mr. Maiz said, rising to cheer a hit by a Sultanes batter from a box seat behind the plate. "Our economy still hasn't gotten back to 1994 levels."
Moments later, Ty Gainey, a former Houston Astros centerfielder from South Carolina who plays for the Sultanes, smacked a homer with two runners on. What did Gainey think of the chances for an expansion club in Monterrey, he was asked later.
"Some major league players probably wouldn't be able to adapt to living in Mexico," Gainey said. "The food. The water. If you can play here, you can play anywhere. But an expansion team is still possible, because Mexican fans really love baseball."

HEADLINE: U.N. Panel Seeks to Ease Suffering of Iraq's People

BYLINE: By JUDITH MILLER 

DATELINE: UNITED NATIONS, March 30

 BODY:
Concluding that Iraq has gone from "relative affluence to massive poverty" since economic sanctions were imposed against it after the 1991 Persian Gulf war, a new report to the Security Council recommends several steps aimed at alleviating its people's suffering.
The report was prepared by one of three special panels convened in January to overcome the political divisions over Iraq on the Security Council and to help devise a common policy. It did not recommend that sanctions be lifted, but it did suggest easing them with specific modifications, some of which the United States said today it might oppose. 
 The four-member panel of senior United Nations officials recommended, for instance, that oil companies be allowed to invest directly in Iraqi oil fields to increase Iraq's production, which is currently at 2.5 million barrels of oil per day. This would enable Iraq to free up funds for food that it now spends buying new oil equipment and repairing and replacing spare parts in its oil fields.
While Iraq is now permitted under an oil-for-food program to sell $5.256 billion worth of oil every six months and to use some of the proceeds to alleviate suffering caused by the sanctions, it has been unable to produce that much, given depressed oil prices and the state of its fields.
In addition, the report suggests that the Security Council authorize foreign companies to invest in Iraqi export industries, like fertilizer, sulfur, and dates, that are unrelated to military production. In both cases, the report states, the added funds would be monitored by the United Nations to insure that they are only used for buying essential goods.
The report also recommends that the Council consider reducing temporarily the amount of money that Iraq pays into a fund that compensates victims of its 1990 invasion of Kuwait, which also comes from the oil proceeds.
"It is the panel's view that under current conditions the humanitarian outlook will remain bleak and become more serious with time," the report states.
"Infant mortality rates in Iraq today are among the highest in the world," it says. "Low infant birth weight affects at least 23 percent of all births, chronic malnutrition affects every fourth child under 5 years of age; only 41 percent of the population have regular access to clean water; 83 percent of all schools need substantial repairs."
The report added that it would take $7 billion just to bring the country's power to generate electricity to where it was before the gulf war.
"The gravity of the humanitarian situation of the Iraqi people is indisputable and cannot be overstated," the report concludes.
But it was disputed, only hours after Celso L. N. Amorim, Brazil's representative, who heads all three panels, gave it to the Council.
Western diplomats noted, for instance, that many of the report's statistics about the situation of the Iraqi people -- like infant mortality rates -- relied heavily on data provided by Iraq, much of which had not been independently confirmed.
While Washington is concerned about alleviating the suffering, said A. Peter Burleigh, the American representative, "the basic responsibility for their plight lies with Saddam Hussein," Iraq's leader.
Sanctions were still in place "because Iraq has not disarmed itself of weapons of mass destruction" or honored other commitments to the United Nations. Several United Nations agencies contend that Iraq has not spent the money it has on food and medicine, he said.
The United States wants to insure that any additional funds for Iraq would help Iraqis, not their Government, he said.
While Mr. Burleigh was not willing to single out the proposals that troubled him, other American officials cited, for example, the report's recommendation that contributions to the Iraqi victims' compensation fund be temporarily reduced, or borrowed to enable Iraq to buy more goods for its people.
Though the proposal was sponsored by Britain, America's closest ally on the Council, one official said that the United States might hesitate to help one group of victims, the Iraqi people, at the expense of another -- people from many different countries who were working in Iraq when it invaded Kuwait.

SECTION: Section B; Page 1; Column 1; Metropolitan Desk 

LENGTH: 759 words

HEADLINE: About New York;
Trying to Save Spring's Soul From Auction

BYLINE: By DAVID GONZALEZ 

 BODY:
HARRY BUBBINS smiled at the season's first daffodils -- a burst of bright yellow blossoms perched atop rich green stems -- thriving in a community garden on East 138th Street and the Bruckner Expressway in Mott Haven. The Bronx garden's location -- a triangular lot that ages ago was a wreck -- was a testament to life's tenacity amid housing projects and a tangle of roads and highway ramps. Mr. Bubbins calls this plot, the southernmost garden in the South Bronx, the Liberated Zone.
"I don't know why T.S. Eliot said April was the cruelest month," he said. "That's when things all come out in the sunshine." 
 Elsewhere in the garden, which has been tended since the mid-1980's by the volunteers of the Cherry Tree Association, skeletal trees awaited the warmth and seedlings broke through the soil. A wooden cross decorated with tin snakeheads was chained to a stone where a computer had been placed before it. It was a sculpture in honor of Geeza Daniels, a founder of the garden.
"The piece is actually called 'Prometheus Bound,' " Mr. Bubbins said. "Today's hottest technology will be tomorrow's refuse. Or art. And the land will always be here."
Considering that he was standing on soil owned by the City of New York, he may have spoken too soon, at least when it comes to what will be on that land. The Cherry Tree Association's garden and more than 100 other community plots are scheduled to be auctioned in May by the city to spur economic development and housing in blighted communities. Local officials and garden advocates have tried to prevent the auction. They contend that good urban planning, especially in poor neighborhoods like Mott Haven, must provide open spaces that temper man-made progress with natural tradition.
Mr. Bubbins's reference to Eliot came from "The Waste Land," which is what Mott Haven had become after years of abandonment, crime and an exodus of people. Some said it got that way through poor planning that left it with too many housing projects, homeless shelters and other facilities for the poor. Nor did it help being hemmed in by an elevated expressway.
The gardens, made possible through the city's Green Thumb program, turned ratty lots into oases that were leased to community groups for pennies a year. It was a temporary arrangement, made with the understanding that the parcels could one day be sold. But a lot has changed since the gardeners took up their rakes. Advocates said the city land-use review that allowed the sale of the lots is outdated and does not reflect the current needs of a community.
Lisa Westberg came to the Cherry Tree garden five years ago to work the soil and share in artistic and cultural events organized by a group of artists who work next door. It sparked in her a sense of freedom.
"There was a kind of uncompromising readiness to take control of certain things in life," she said. "Most of the city is a symbol of flair and fun, where so many people come to it but never look here to commit themselves to something. Coming up here for me is very much on the other end of that spectrum."
It is, to her, planning for a future that is not cookie-cutter, conformist or commercial. "Other places in the city are about creating a culture of conformity, to set standards everyone is supposed to adhere to and uphold as a common vision," she said. "Instead of standardizing and searching for uniformity, we should have a more profound search for freedom that means we have to be more sure about what our desires are. It's kind of abstract."
TO make it real, a group of Bronx gardeners dressed like sunflowers and tomatoes for a bit of street theater outside the midtown office of Gov. George E. Pataki. They went there Monday afternoon to preserve their endangered gardens by seeking financing from the state's Environmental Bond Act. The gardeners hope they could use the money to buy the plot at auction, or to convince city officials that the plot shouldn't be sold because they have the resources to make better use of it. Policy analysts familiar with the act said it was not a cure-all, since groups that receive the state money had to match the amount received.
A woman with patchwork silk butterfly wings floated through the crowd of pedestrians. Another woman whose face peeked through a sunflower headdress tiptoed along the sidewalk. Sandy Walsh, a systems analyst, watched in approval before heading to an appointment.
"Part of living in the city consists of dirt, crime and corruption," she said. "Where is life? We need sanctuaries."

HEADLINE: THE MARKETS: STOCKS & BONDS;
Rising Concern Over Profits Sends Dow Below 10,000

BYLINE: By KENNETH N. GILPIN 

 BODY:
The Dow Jones industrial average retreated from its one-day perch above 10,000 yesterday on renewed concerns about corporate earnings, as other major market measures also slipped.
The worries about first-quarter earnings were set off when Coca-Cola told securities analysts after the closing bell on Monday that it expected worldwide sales to show a slight decline in the first quarter. It cited weakness in each of its international divisions. 
 Later, an analyst at Goldman, Sachs lowered her first-quarter sales estimates for Gillette, citing weak demand in foreign markets. And investors were not pleased to read in The Wall Street Journal yesterday that at a meeting with securities analysts General Motors' president, G. Richard Wagoner, said the auto maker was not likely to meet its most optimistic target for net income for its European operations.
Additional weakness developed in the afternoon, when an Oregon jury ordered Philip Morris to pay a record $81 million to the family of a man who died of lung cancer after smoking Marlboros for four decades.
Those developments outweighed the Federal Reserve Board's announcement that its policy-making committee had voted to leave short-term interest rates unchanged. The decision had been widely expected.
"Stocks are coming under pressure," said Anthony Conroy, head of stock trading at Bankers Trust. "Traders and portfolio managers are looking for earnings to support these prices."
"The fundamentals are fairly strong," he added. "Inflation is low, and so are interest rates. But I would not be surprised to see a 2 percent to 3 percent correction before the market moves higher."
At the close, the Dow had fallen 93.52, or 0.93 percent, to 9,913.26. Meanwhile, the Standard & Poor's 500 index dipped 9.42, or 0.72 percent, to 1,300.75, and the Nasdaq composite index lost 12.55, or 0.50 percent, to 2,480.29. The Russell 2000 index of small-company stocks declined 0.98, or 0.2 percent, to 398.79.
Trading volume was quite light, with about 729.03 million shares changing hands on the New York Stock Exchange. With equity markets scheduled to be closed on Good Friday, the pace of activity is likely to slow further as the week, which includes Passover, progresses.
On Monday, when the Dow closed above 10,000 for the first time, "it was almost as if investors decided for one day to ignore all the problems we face," said Hugh Johnson, chief investment officer at the First Albany Corporation. "Coke's announcement means the global economic crisis was still around in the first quarter, and was a reminder we are entering the earnings season, which can be a very treacherous time."
Coke, down 1 9/16 at 63 1/4, and Philip Morris, off 3 7/16 , to 37 3/4, were the biggest contributors to the Dow's drop yesterday.
Meanwhile, Gillette, a component of the S.& P. 500 index, shed 3 3/8, to 60 1/4.
Shares of America Online, the most active stock on the New York Stock Exchange, soared 12 points to close at 144 1/2, after the company announced an Internet commerce alliance with Sun Microsystems. Sun Microsystems gained 1 7/16 , to 125 15/16 .
Other Internet stocks also gained.
Amazon.com jumped 15 1/16 , to 164 11/16 , on optimism about its fledgling on-line auction business.
And Priceline.com, which runs an Internet site where consumers bid for airline tickets and hotel rooms, catapulted 53, to 69 a share, in its first day of trading.
"There is tremendous activity and strong demand in the Internet stocks," said Jonathan Olesky, head of block trading at Morgan Stanley Dean Witter, which was the lead manager on the Priceline offering. "But with prices quite high in certain sectors of the market, it is quite reasonable to seem some consolidation," he added.

 	 	 		--------------------	 	
 
Treasury Prices Advance
 (By The Associated Press) -- Bond prices rose yesterday, after the latest strategy meeting by Federal Reserve officials produced no change in lending rates.
Recent reports have shown surprising economic strength, fueling concerns about inflationary pressures. However, Fed officials did not raise interest rates to slow the economy.
The price of the benchmark 30-year Treasury bond rose 25/32 point, or $7.81 per $1,000 in face value, to 95 6/32 . Its yield, which moves in the opposite direction, fell to 5.58 percent, from 5.64 percent late Monday.
The price increase was less for shorter-term Treasuries. The actively traded two-year note rose 3/32 , to 99 28/32 , for a yield of 4.94 percent.

HEADLINE: Fed Maintains Steady Stance In Rate Policy

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
The Federal Reserve's top officials left interest rates unchanged today, and passed up an opportunity to signal any concern about inflation.
The meeting of the Fed's governors and regional bank presidents, all members of the policy-setting Open Market Committee, came at a time when inflation has been remarkably tame despite the robust economy. And there is unlikely to be much upward pressure on prices as long as labor costs do not accelerate significantly and Europe and Asia remain in the economic doldrums. 
 "I am sure most members of the committee think everything is going rather beautifully," said Alan Blinder, a Princeton University economist and a former Fed vice chairman. "They have not allowed themselves to be provoked into some sort of pre-emptive action against inflation and, in hindsight, their patience has proven to be correct."
In the past, the Fed sometimes raised interest rates before any rise in the inflation rate, contending that an increase was necessary to discourage borrowing and thus slow a strong economy before companies raised prices in response to shortages and rising wage pressures.
But the economy has boomed for more than three years and forecasts of higher inflation have proved wrong time after time. So Fed officials switched tactics.
"Before they take any action, they are going to wait for the inflation rate to actually pick up," said David M. Jones, chief economist at Aubrey G. Lanston & Company, a Wall Street firm.
The Fed has another tool at its disposal that it chose not to use today. At times, even at sessions when it did not change rates, the central bank has signaled its future intentions through a directive stating that it was shifting its "bias" in one direction or another. Traditionally, such preferences were kept secret until the minutes of each meeting were published six weeks later. But recently the Fed said it would usually announce such directives when they were adopted, rather than make the financial markets guess. There was no announcement today.
The Fed affects a whole range of interest rates by changing its target for the Federal funds rate, the overnight borrowing cost that banks and other financial institutions charge each other. Rates on car loans, mortgages and home equity loans, for example, tend to move up and down with the Federal funds rate. The central bank left its target at 4.75 percent, the level it has been since the Fed last cut rates by a quarter of a percentage point in November.
The stock market may also have been a factor in the Fed's decision not to take any action today. While a small rise in the Federal funds rate might slow the rise in stock prices, it might also provoke a sharp, damaging decline, said Albert M. Wojnilower, a Wall Street economist.
"It worries Fed officials when the stock market runs away," he said. Still, "they don't see it going up in a runaway fashion, but even if it were," Mr. Wojnilower added, "they prefer the market running away than taking a dive, which they would have to do something about."
The stock market, in the view of many economists, has been a major driver behind the economic boom, encouraging people to borrow and spend and fostering a high level of consumer confidence. The Conference Board reported today that its index of consumer confidence rose in March by eight-tenths of a point, to 133.9 from a revised 133.1 in February.
For all the strong growth, low unemployment and labor shortages, the inflation rate has fallen over the last two years, defying traditional relationships. Many economists explain the conundrum by pointing to a weak global economy and a relatively strong American currency, which has resulted in a flood of inexpensive imports that helps to hold down the inflation rate.
"Things may be beginning to turn around overseas," said Kenneth Matheny, a senior economist at Macroeconomic Advisers Inc., a forecasting service in St. Louis. "But the Fed has time before there is any real improvement and Fed officials will wait until they get much stronger signals before they consider tightening."
A competing explanation for the unusual combination of strong growth and shrinking inflation has been offered by Alan Greenspan, the Fed's chairman. He has suggested that the nation's productivity growth rate -- which measures the ability of workers to turn out a greater quantity of goods and services in a given period of time -- has finally started to improve after a quarter-century of stagnation.
Productivity statistics issued by the Government do show recent improvements, thus allowing workers, in effect, to pay for their own wage increases rather than force employers to raise prices or squeeze their profit margins.
But while many economists say the improvement is probably temporary, Mr. Greenspan has argued that it reflects the growing effectiveness of computers and other new technologies in the workplace. And lately some other Fed governors have expressed the same view.

HEADLINE: Requiem for the Middleweights;
Ho-Hum Taurus Marks End of Midsize Sedan as Style's Pace Car

BYLINE: By KEITH BRADSHER 

 BODY:
Remember the first Ford Taurus? When the car was introduced in late 1985, its smooth, aerodynamic curves shook up the design of midsize sedans. And despite fears that its radical look would turn off car buyers, sales took off almost immediately. Moreover, its success was one of the early signs of the resurgence of the Ford Motor Company, which today is at the brink of surpassing the General Motors Corporation as the largest auto maker in the world.
Well, Ford just unveiled the new Taurus yesterday at the New York International Auto Show. But do not look for any surprises. While it may sell well if it is priced cheaply enough, it is unlikely to win any contests for style. Indeed, the most distinctive thing about the new Taurus is probably how indistinguishable it is from all the other lookalike midsize sedans. 
 Ford executives, while acknowledging that the design of the Taurus is cautious and conservative, say it will offer the comfort, competitive price and numerous safety features that consumers want in midsize cars these days. "More than the styling, it's the total package: quality, value, safety," said James G. O'Connor, Ford's president for Ford brand cars and light trucks. "I think the consumer in general doesn't make styling the No. 1 preference in that segment, as opposed to, say, the Mustang segment."
Automotive design experts say that the 2000 model year Taurus represents something of a milestone. It is the last of the once-stylish, high-volume midsize sedans to take on a new, more generic look as Ford and other auto makers put most of their automotive styling efforts elsewhere. After reigning for decades as the main focus of automotive design in the United States, midsize sedans are losing their primacy to sport utility vehicles and to an ever-growing array of so-called crossover vehicles that combine features of sport utility vehicles, pickup trucks, station wagons and cars.
Gone from the 2000 Taurus are many of the quirky external features that made earlier models, including a 1996 redesign, so distinctive. The oval rear window, oval dashboard and many of the other features that distinguished the Taurus sedan for the last three years have been replaced with plain, rectangular designs for windows and dashboard alike.
Underneath the sheet metal, remarkably little has been changed, allowing Ford to avoid hundreds of millions of dollars in expenses for engineering work and new factory tooling. The whole underbody of the vehicle has been left virtually untouched, while the engine is the same other than a few tweaks to improve its horsepower. Ford did not even bother with the time and cost of designing a new rear window for the wagon version of the new Taurus, leaving an oval rear window on it even while the rest of the vehicle has assumed a more rectangular look.
There are several reasons, experts say, why midsize cars have become so indistinguishable.
For one, Americans who value distinctive automotive styling are increasingly choosing sport utility vehicles and crossover vehicles, like the Toyota Motor Corporation's Lexus RX-300, rather than buying midsize sedans. A growing proportion of new cars are also being purchased by households with three or more vehicles, and these households tend to choose plainly designed sedans to go with more fashionable vehicles in their fleets.
Finally, midsize car prices are slowly falling because of an excess of factory capacity and a glut of late-model sedans coming off leases or being sold by rental companies. This encourages auto makers to cut engineering costs and limits their ability to add fancy styling touches.
"There aren't as many people in sedans who are willing to pay for styling," said Susan G. Jacobs, the president of Jacobs & Associates, an auto consulting firm in Rutherford, N.J. "It's wise for the styling dollars to go into the fun vehicles."
In marketing focus groups, buyers of midsize cars also tend to disagree with each other about what they want, unlike buyers of sport utility vehicles, so car designers must make many compromises between conflicting demands, said Jerry Hirshberg, president for North American design at the Nissan Motor Company. "We've gotten a bit risk averse," he said.
Many Americans, of course, say they are not especially interested in automotive styling and would be perfectly happy with vehicles that simply provide comfortable, reliable transportation. But auto executives and market analysts say that Americans' buying decisions do not back up such statements.
Sport utility vehicles sell briskly even though most buyers do not use them for off-road driving, while vehicles that evoke the image of sport utilities, like the Pontiac Montana minivan, are increasingly popular. A few imaginatively styled midsize sedans, like the Volkswagen Passat and Pontiac Grand Prix, have been successful, garnering praise from the automotive media and consumer evaluators alike. But they occupy relatively low-sales niches in the overall market for midsize sedans.
John M. Waraniak, a managing director of Benchmarking Partners, an automotive consulting firm in Bloomfield Hills, Mich., said he had spread out photographs in his kitchen of four new, high-sales sedans going on sale this year and early next year: Ford Taurus, Oldsmobile Aurora, Chevrolet Impala and Nissan Maxima. His wife, Terri, was unimpressed.
"She said, 'They all look the same to me,' and she's the kind of person, about 40 years old, who might switch from a sport utility if there were something exciting to switch to," Mr. Waraniak said.
A fifth sedan, Saturn's first midsize car, will also go on sale soon, but G.M. has not released photographs of it. The midsize Saturn LS, which is being introduced today at the New York auto show, has styling that does not depart significantly from other similarly sized sedans on the market, according to John Middlebrook, G.M..'s vice president for vehicle brand marketing.
Midsize cars are important. Close to $100 billion worth are sold each year in the United States, making it the biggest single segment of the American auto market. It is almost as big a business as the world's passenger airline industry.
Yet the midsize sedan market has been eroded by the success of sport utility vehicles and minivans, falling to 27 percent of the family vehicle market last year from 33 percent in 1985.
Auto engineers object to suggestions that their midsize cars are boring, but they seldom suggest that their offerings are on the cutting edge, either. David T. Marinaro, the chief program engineer for the 2000 Taurus, said that the car "was designed around functionality; the whole car was designed around that."
The new Taurus is also designed so that Ford can build it and sell it for no more money than a current Taurus and possibly for less. Although Ford has not yet decided on the actual price, everything is geared to keeping down costs. By keeping the underbody and the door openings the same as for the previous Taurus, for instance, Ford can continue to use a lot of the same factory equipment to build it.
"My objective is to be actually equal to or lower in cost" for manufacturing the new model, Mr. Marinaro said.
The more modest design of the new Taurus partly reflects the difficulties Ford had in selling the model that is about to be phased out. Ford spared no expense in redesigning the Taurus for the 1996 model year, hoping to repeat the success of the original 1986 Taurus. The 1986-style Taurus produced enormous profits for Ford, becoming the nation's best-selling car from 1992 through 1996.
But the 1996 redesign bombed. Many people complained that the new vehicle looked too unusual. Perhaps more seriously, Ford also had to raise the price by $1,000 to cover the extensive engineering expenses and the cost of features like waves in the sheet metal on the side. Ford was not prepared to compete on price a year later when the Honda Motor Company and Toyota, respectively, introduced less expensive models of the Accord and the Camry.
The combination of radical styling and an increased sticker price soon cost Taurus its leadership of the American car market. The last two years. it has trailed the Accord and the Camry -- two sedans renowned for reliability and functionality over distinctive design -- by a large and widening margin.
While the new midsize cars may be dull, that does not mean that auto makers are producing bland vehicles over all. Ford, G.M., Nissan and other auto makers are all making separate plans, for example, to start selling sport utility vehicles with open pickup-truck beds at the back. Indeed, soaring sales of sport utility vehicles, minivans, pickups and crossover vehicles have produced a wave of innovation in these market segments.
All of these vehicles, including the crossovers, are regulated as light trucks, which means they are partly or entirely exempt from the regulatory straitjacket -- rules on fuel economy, air pollution, bumper heights and the like -- that tends to produce car designs that fit the same mold.
Some auto designers think that midsize cars can be made exciting again. DaimlerChrysler A.G. already makes the striking Dodge Intrepid and Chrysler 300M, which are actually full-size cars, and is putting a lot of effort into redesigning midsize cars, like the Plymouth Breeze, to make them more interesting. Subtle changes such as changing the curve of the windshield or the width between a car's wheels could make a styling difference without necessarily hurting affordability, said Thomas C. Gale, the executive vice president for North American design at DaimlerChrysler. "You have to find ways which make things exciting," Mr. Gale said, "and still do it in ways which are perceived as adding value."
Mr. Hirshberg of Nissan said that it would take a completely new yet practical midsize sedan design to revive one of the oldest segments in the auto industry.
"I don't think it's dead," he said. "It's just going to take someone who comes in and nails something fresh, as Ford did with the first Taurus."

HEADLINE: Longer Solvency for Medicare and Social Security

BYLINE: By DAVID E. ROSENBAUM 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
Relieving some of the political pressure for reform, the Government released new actuarial studies today extending the date at which Medicare can be expected to run out of money by seven years and Social Security insolvency by two years.
The improved financial position of the Medicare and Social Security trust funds is largely the result of the strong economy over the last year, the trustees of the funds said in their annual report. 
 Based on the latest long-range estimates of factors like national economic growth, worker productivity, inflation, unemployment, immigration and birth rates and retirement patterns, the actuaries still expect the Government's two largest programs to require more tax money or a reduction in benefits as the number of baby boomers in retirement mushrooms.
But without any changes in law, the trustees found, the Medicare system should have enough money to pay full benefits until 2015 and the Social Security system should be in the black until 2034. Last year at this time, they projected the insolvency dates to be 2008 for Medicare and 2032 for Social Security.
At a ceremony in the Rose Garden, President Clinton insisted that despite the good news, legislation strengthening the programs should still be enacted this year.
"Now is the time to make those changes," the President said. "Now when our economy is strong, now when our people have renewed confidence and now when we have time on our side so that modest changes today can have major impacts in the years ahead."
Leading Republicans in Congress issued similar statements. "We cannot let this good news make us complacent," said Senator William V. Roth Jr., the Delaware Republican who is chairman of the Finance Committee, which has jurisdiction over Social Security and Medicare.
Furthermore, economists of all political persuasions generally agree that the sooner the necessary changes are made to shore up Social Security and Medicare, the less abrupt and painful those changes will seem to taxpayers and retirees.
But the President and the Republicans who control Congress are so far apart on how the two programs should be fixed and the politics of the matter are so delicate that the odds are already against enactment of comprehensive legislation this year. The effect of today's announcement postponing a crisis was to make a search for compromise less urgent.
"These are actuaries from heaven," said James A. Thurber, director of the Center for Congressional and Presidential Studies at American University here. "It takes pressure off all the players on the Hill and the President," Mr. Thurber said, and makes it safe for them to leave the problem for the next President and the next Congress.
John Rother, the politically savvy lobbyist for the American Association of Retired Persons, which favors enactment of Social Security legislation this year, said, "I'm not going to throw in the towel." After a pause, Mr. Rother added, "Publicly."
The six trustees of the trust funds include three Cabinet members: Treasury Secretary Robert E. Rubin, Labor Secretary Alexis M. Herman and Donna E. Shalala, Secretary of Health and Human Services. The others are Kenneth S. Apfel, the Social Security Commissioner, and two experts who were appointed by President Clinton: Stephen G. Kellison, an actuary, and Marilyn Moon, an economist who specializes in health policy.
 Although they are political appointees, their annual report is, by and large, the work of career Government actuaries and economists. The reports were also reviewed by outside actuaries who signed statements saying the methodology and assumptions were sound.
The projections that the Medicare fund would be insolvent in 2015 and the Social Security fund in 2034 were based on what the trustees called an intermediate set of estimates about the economic and demographic factors.
The economic assumptions were quite conservative. The trustees estimated, for instance, that economic growth between 2010 and 2050 would average less than 1.5 percent a year. That is approximately half the average growth rate of the last 75 years, but the trustees were taking into account the fact that the country's labor force is not expected to grow as fast in the 21st century as it has in the 20th.
The report offered for comparison's sake two other projections. One based on less favorable assumptions showed Medicare running out of money in 2007 and Social Security in 2028. Another based on more favorable conditions showed that neither program would become insolvent in the next 75 years.
As recently as two years ago, projections indicated that the Medicare fund would run out of money in 2001. Ms. Shalala said today that the improved situation was the result of a stronger economy and of changes the President and Congress made in the Balanced Budget Act of 1997. That law shifted some of the cost of home health care out of the hospital insurance trust fund and reduced Medicare payments to doctors, hospitals and other health-care providers.
The 1997 report forecast that Social Security would become insolvent in 2029. Last year's report said the reserves would be exhausted in 2032. The brighter picture is almost entirely the result of the flourishing economy over the last two years.
Even with reduced pressure to act, the President and Congress are sure to continue to debate what to do with Social Security and Medicare -- right through the 2000 elections.
In the case of Social Security, the President and the Republicans favor setting aside much of the budget surplus over the next 15 years to strengthen the retirement system.
But Republicans want to use the money to allow workers to establish private retirement accounts that they would invest in stocks and bonds. The President wants to allow some Social Security money to be invested in the markets, but he would have the Government control the investments.
On Medicare, Republicans are generally supporting a marketplace system under which retirees would be given a fixed amount of money that they could use to buy private health insurance or the Government's program.
The President and most Democrats oppose this idea and favor setting aside money from the budget surplus to strengthen Medicare and provide new funds to cover prescription drugs.
