HEADLINE: INTERNATIONAL BUSINESS;
No Quick Chinese Entry Seen Into Trade Group

BYLINE: By ERIK ECKHOLM 

DATELINE: BEIJING, March 30

 BODY:
Dampening hopes for a quick agreement on China's entry into the World Trade Organization, American officials said today that "difficult and complex issues remain across all areas of trade" after a hurried visit here by the White House trade representative.
Chinese and American officials have negotiated intensively for the last week here over the scale of market-opening concessions that China must make to qualify for membership in the global trade forum, which sets rules and arbitrates disputes. 
 China has long sought entry, and after years of impasse, officials from both countries now seem eager for the Chinese to join. They are trying to put the outlines of a deal together by the time Prime Minister Zhu Rongji arrives in Washington for an official visit next week.
More than just a trade deal is at stake. The American and Chinese Governments both see a potential accord as a rare political breakthrough in a relationship increasingly strained by disputes over such issues as human rights, nuclear espionage and the NATO bombing in Yugoslavia, which China condemns.
Hopes that agreement might be near on entry into the trade group were raised over the weekend when President Clinton's trade representative, Charlene Barshefsky, left Washington for a quick trip to Beijing, in hopes of pushing Mr. Zhu to make last-minute concessions on market access for foreign companies in agriculture, financial services and other areas that the Chinese have long been protected.
While Ms. Barshefsky said she did not plan to go home with a deal, she hoped for breakthroughs.
It is unclear whether she got any, American officials said.
Today, she spent five and a half hours talking with a senior trade official, Wu Yi, and with Prime Minister Zhu. But tonight, as Ms. Barshefsky headed home, she issued a written statement saying that although there had been "important progress today in narrowing the issues," there remained "substantial gaps in critical areas."
"The United States will set no artificial deadlines in these negotiations," the statement said.
Secretary of Commerce William M. Daley, who is also here on a trade mission, said he believed that a deal would be reached sometime before the fall. But by then, many in Washington believe, it will be too close to next year's election campaigning for the Administration to submit China's offerings to Congress.
According to American officials, China has offered some major concessions. Mr. Daley said Mr. Zhu told him that his Government would permit the sale of cellular telephones that operate on technology used by Motorola and other American phone makers. Until now, the Chinese system favored European producers. China has also indicated that American companies could buy minority stakes in telecommunications businesses, but so far it has refused to allow them to take majority stakes.
China has also not agreed on a resolution of disputes that have blocked the importing of most types of American wheat. "Based on what we have right now," an American official said, "we couldn't send this thing to the Hill."
The chief China negotiator on Ms. Barshefsky's staff, Robert Cassidy, will stay on for "a couple of days," an official said, and then talks are expected to move to Washington.
The Americans seem uncertain whether China plans last-minute offers -- something not uncommon in trade negotiations -- or whether its leaders doubt that President Clinton has the will or the political strength now to get a deal through Congress.
Along with membership in the trade organization, the Chinese want the protection for their own exports that it would bring. But with rising unemployment and farmers' protests at home, and inefficient domestic industries already reeling, they have been reluctant to cut tariffs and other protective measures as quickly as Western nations demand.
While China's accession to the trade organization requires the consent of many countries, satisfying the United States is the most crucial step. Washington is concerned about a growing deficit with the Chinese, estimated at $57 billion last year, and officials believe that lower tariffs here would increase American exports. At the same time, many American companies have been frustrated by rules that severely limit their ability to invest or operate.
As anti-China sentiment rises in Congress, officials say, no agreement can survive without enthusiastic corporate support and lobbying. And while Congressional approval of a China accord is not required, under the trade body's rules, Congress would have to rescind a law that requires annual renewal of China's most-favored-nation trading status.

HEADLINE: Owner of Bronx Tenements Misused Funds, Audit Finds

BYLINE: By CHARLES V. BAGLI 

 BODY:
A real estate developer who has raised hundreds of thousands of dollars for President Clinton and the Democratic Party has been accused by Federal officials of misappropriating $1.4 million from a troubled housing complex in the South Bronx even as the tenements fell apart.
At the same time, the United States Department of Housing and Urban Development, which holds the mortgages for the Jose DeDiego Beekman Houses, is moving to seize the complex of 38 buildings and 1,238 apartments in the Mott Haven section of the Bronx. 
 For the last 25 years, the complex has been owned by Gerald Schuster, general partner of the Continental Wingate Company, a real estate company in Needham, Mass., that has rehabilitated dilapidated buildings in New York and Massachusetts with Federal subsidies, tax breaks and low-interest loans.
According to an audit by the Inspector General's Office at HUD, Mr. Schuster and his companies siphoned $1.4 million in funds from the complex to pay company employees and to repay loans to his own companies. Some of the funds were also diverted to other investments.
The findings of the audit were referred to the United States Attorney's Office, which notified Mr. Schuster in a March 25 letter that it was considering civil action in connection with the allegations. The United States Attorney has not filed a lawsuit.
Last night, HUD officials, who had taken control of the Beekman Houses more than two years ago, also told the tenants that the agency "would formalize ownership of the properties."
"The department has taken these actions in the best interests of the tenants and taxpayers," said Peter Ragone, a spokesman for HUD. "HUD is committed to insuring that all New Yorkers in federally supported housing have access to a decent, clean and safe home."
Alan Eisner, a spokesman for Mr. Schuster and Continental Wingate, said that the allegations of misappropriation of funds were "without merit." He said the company had not even seen the audit of Beekman Houses.
"To the best of our knowledge and ability," Mr. Eisner said, "we are in full and complete compliance."
He said the company was working with HUD to "return the housing complex to the South Bronx community."
Mr. Schuster and his wife, Elaine, have contributed to, and raised enormous sums for, the Democrats in Massachusetts and nationally. Last year, President Clinton attended a $5,000-a-plate fund-raising dinner at the home of the Schusters in Boston.
Starting in the early 1970's, Continental Wingate and its limited partners renovated the 38 buildings that Beekman Houses comprises with federally subsidized mortgages and later rent subsidies.
"I think you'll find that we have a stellar reputation with HUD throughout the country," said Robert G. Najarian, president of Wingate Management, a subsidiary of Continental Wingate. "We pride ourselves on working in the inner cities and on our reputation."
But the company has also been dogged by complaints and investigations. In the late 1970's, then-Assemblyman Charles E. Schumer led a legislative panel that investigated the financing of Beekman Houses and conditions in the buildings.
"How was it possible for the Federal Government to spend $20 million on these houses and still have them remain such horrible slums?" Mr. Schumer asked at the time.
No action was taken against the company, however.
In December 1996, Continental defaulted on six of the eight mortgages for Beekman Houses. HUD then paid off $27.4 million in loans and took control of the complex. Since then, HUD has spent more than $15 million replacing windows and stoves, rehabilitating 500 kitchens and 450 bathrooms and making the buildings more secure.
"It's a testament to their faith in the system that they persevered," Mr. Schumer, now a United States Senator, said last night of the tenants. "I salute HUD's current leadership, but justice shouldn't take this long."
Norma Allen, president of Tenants United for Better Living, said that conditions had improved at the buildings recently. "HUD has been a treat," she said. "We didn't know we were entitled to all the services we're now getting."
But Ms. Allen and another resident of the complex said the problems at Beekman Houses were more complex than simply a bad management company.
Their neighborhood was plagued by drug gangs in the 1980's and suffered from the Federal Government's declining support for housing for poor people. One woman blamed HUD's negligence for allowing conditions to deteriorate.

LENGTH: 585 words

HEADLINE: BUSINESS DIGEST

 BODY:

Some On-Line Donations Don't Make It to Charities
In a new twist on Internet commerce, at least a half-dozen Web sites are offering to sell goods to on-line shoppers and send a portion of the purchase price to charity. Although the sums have been modest thus far, many charities see it as an important fund-raising development.
But it is impossible to know in every case what happens to the few dollars that customers thought were going to a good cause. Though many charities are getting checks directly from the sites, money may not end up in the charity's hands as fast as shoppers might imagine -- if at all. Page A1.
 
No Plans for Renault Name in U.S.
Having agreed last week to buy a little more than a third of Nissan Motor, Renault may start selling Renault cars under the Nissan name in the United States someday. But it has no plans to revive the Renault name in the American market because its reputation is so weak, the company's chairman and chief executive, Louis Schweitzer, said at the New York International Auto Show. C4.
 
Largest Award Ever in Smoking Suit
A state jury in Portland, Ore., ordered the largest award in a smoking-related lawsuit, ruling that Philip Morris must pay $81 million to the family of a man who smoked Marlboro cigarettes for four decades before he died. A14.
 
Microsoft Settlement Talks Begin
Settlement talks began between Microsoft and the Federal and state authorities who filed antitrust charges against the company. Both sides emphasized before the discussions started that they would talk in complete secrecy, but it was clear from the opening positions of both parties that a vast gulf divides them, making a settlement difficult at best. C25.
 
Dow Tumbles From 10,000 Perch
Just a day after the Dow Jones industrial average surpassed 10,000, it and other market gauges retreated on concerns about sales and earnings at big companies. The Dow fell 93.52 points, to 9,913.26, while the Standard & Poor's 500-stock index dipped 9.42 points, to 1,300.75. The Nasdaq composite index lost 12.55 points, to 2,480.29. Bond traders bid up prices on the Federal Reserve's announcement that it would leave interest rates unchanged. C14.
 
Hedge Fund to Pay Back Rescuers
Long-Term Capital Management, the giant hedge fund that Wall Street firms saved from collapse during the turmoil in stock and bond markets in September, has recovered robustly and plans to start paying back its rescuers as early as June. C25.
 
Pepsi Bottling Plans to Go Public
Pepsi Bottling Group is going public in one of the biggest public offerings ever, but not necessarily the hottest. Market Place. C13.
 
U S Airways to Buy Back Stock
U S Airways Group, the nation's sixth-largest airline, said that it would buy back $500 million in stock to lift its lagging shares. The company also said that its first-quarter earnings would be in line with analysts' recently reduced estimates. Shares of U S Airways rose $3.3125, or 7 percent, to $49.8125. C12.
 
Equant Expands Its Data Services
Helping to expand the digital world to some of the globe's least-wired corners, Equant plans to announce that it will offer advanced data services in 25 additional territories. C4.
 
Stock of Express Scripts Surges
Express Scripts, a pharmacy benefits management company that has a customer base of more than 36.5 million people, announced plans for an on-line drugstore, and its shares surged $17.8125, or 24.1 percent, to $91.875. C2.

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE WHITE HOUSE;
Clinton Says Milosevic Hurts Claim To Kosovo

BYLINE: By JOHN M. BRODER 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
President Clinton warned President Slobodan Milosevic of Yugoslavia today that he was increasingly jeopardizing his claim to keep Kosovo as a province of Yugoslavia with his assault on the ethnic Albanian population there.
Mr. Clinton vowed to pursue air attacks against Serbian targets in Yugoslavia until Mr. Milosevic ceases the killing. 
 "If there was ever any doubt about what is at stake in Kosovo, Mr. Milosevic is certainly erasing it by his actions," Mr. Clinton said at a State Department ceremony to unveil a portrait of Warren M. Christopher, the Secretary of State in Mr. Clinton's first term in office.
"They are the culmination of more than a decade of using ethnic and religious hatred as a justification for uprooting and murdering completely innocent peaceful civilians to pave Mr. Milosevic's path to absolute power," Mr. Clinton said.
In a later statement, the President firmly rejected a proposal from Mr. Milosevic to withdraw some of his forces from Kosovo in exchange for a halt in allied bombing. Mr. Clinton called the offer "unacceptable" and said peace would come only when Mr. Milosevic ended what the United States says is a campaign of terror in Kosovo.
In his remarks at the State Department, Mr. Clinton said, "We will see that his military will be seriously diminished, key military infrastructure destroyed, the prospect for international support for Serbia's claim to Kosovo increasingly jeopardized."
Under terms of a peace agreement signed this month at Rambouillet, France, by Kosovar Albanians but not by Mr. Milosevic, Kosovo would gain autonomy for three years from the central Yugoslav Government in Belgrade. NATO forces would guarantee the agreement. After that, the two sides would negotiate Kosovo's long-term status.
The United States and European nations who oversaw the peace talks have consistently opposed immediate for Kosovo, whose population is 90 percent ethnic Albanian. Western leaders have said they do not want to see the further dismemberment of the remnants of Yugoslavia into a collection of small ethnic states with limited economic prospects and no history of self-government.
Administration officials said previously that the only way to guarantee that Kosovo remained part of Yugoslavia was for Mr. Milosevic to accept the autonomy arrangement that was being negotiated at Rambouillet. The alternative, officials have said, is continuing insurgency by Albanian separatists and intervention by NATO forces.
But Mr. Clinton suggested publicly for the first time since the allied bombing began last week that the Western position was shifting toward independence for Kosovo under some sort of international protection.
Joe Lockhart, the White House spokesman, said that Mr. Clinton did not intend to announce a new policy today. Rather, Mr. Lockhart said, the President was describing a change in world opinion as Serbian fighting spreads through Kosovo.
"It is not our policy that Kosovo should be independent," Mr. Lockhart said. "But Milosevic's continuing on a campaign of atrocities had radicalized the people to such an extent that he forfeits any claim to it and loses international support for Kosovo remaining part of Yugoslavia."
Ivo Daalder, a Balkan specialist at the Brookings Institution who formerly served on the National Security Council, said that Mr. Clinton was making an implicit promise to protect the ethnic Albanian populationof Kosovo from Serbian atrocities. The only realistic way to accomplish that, Mr. Daalder said, is by creating an enclave in Kosovo protected by NATO and American forces.
"It's not a widening of the war aims, but bringing the war aims to their logical conclusion," Mr. Daalder said. "The only way we can fulfill our political objectives is by giving the Kosovars a territory of their own. My only regret is that we should have made this an explicit claim from the beginning."
The President also said today that the air attacks would continue until Mr. Milosevic's ability to carry out his campaign of repression was "seriously diminished" and critical parts of his military machinery were destroyed.
"The allies are united in our outrage over President Milosevic's atrocities against innocent people," Mr. Clinton said. "We are determined to stay with our policy."
Mr. Clinton said that the Serbian offensive was planned before the NATO air strikes began a week ago. He accused Mr. Milosevic of pretending to negotiate with Kosovar Albanians and European leaders this winter even as he moved "from village to village and atrocity to atrocity."
"Now it is clear that as the Kosovar leaders were saying yes to peace," Mr. Clinton said, "Mr. Milosevic was planning a new campaign of expulsions and executions in Kosovo. He started carrying out that plan as the talks ended, increasing our sense of urgency that the air strikes NATO had threatened for some time must begin."

INTERNATIONAL A3-12
 
NATO Agrees to Bomb Sites in Belgrade
The United States and NATO agreed to an escalated air campaign that would include government buildings in downtown Belgrade as targets, two senior American officials said. Western officials admitted that air strikes had failed to halt the Serbs' assault in Kosovo or to damage their military sufficiently. A1
 
NATO warplanes bombarded Serbian ground forces, airfields and supply depots around the clock, but did not knock out the Serbs' air defenses or blunt their offensive. A11 

The growing exodus of refugees from Kosovo has left Clinton Administration officials and NATO officials wondering whether they will ever be able to achieve the goal of a province in which Serbs and ethnic Albanians can live in peace. A1
 
The refugees are overwhelming neighboring countries, leading to long delays at the borders and, at worst, orders to turn back. A8
 
A day of mediation by the Russian Prime Minister, Yevgeny M. Primakov, produced a flurry of ideas but no evidence of a breakthrough. A9
 
President Boris N. Yeltsin told Russian political leaders that while he condemned the NATO strikes, he would not let Russia be drawn into a military conflict in Yugoslavia. A12
 
Ruling on Chinese Immigrants
A Hong Kong court ruled against 17 mainland residents who had claimed the right to live in Hong Kong. The decision reflects fears about a flood of immigrants from the mainland. A6
 
Advice on Iraq Sanctions
A report commissioned by the Security Council did not recommend that sanctions against Iraq be lifted, but it did suggest specific ways to ease them to help relieve the suffering of the Iraqi people. A6
 
Struggle for Ulster Compromise
Prime Ministers Tony Blair of Britain and Bertie Ahern of Ireland were again unable to persuade Protestant and Roman Catholic leaders to compromise on the bitter dispute over the disarmament of the Irish Republican Army. A third round of talks is to be held today. A5

People who buy bottled water cannot be sure that it is purer or safer than tap water, an environmental advocacy group said in a report of a survey that sampled 1,000 bottles of water sold under 103 brand names. The group said that some samples of bottled water contained unsafe levels of bacteria or chemicals. A14

Airlines Faulted on Disabled
Disabled air travelers are often mistreated by airline personnel and denied special accommodations, including access to wheelchairs and escorts, in violation of a 1986 law, according to a report by the National Council on Disability, a Federal advisory agency. A16

Gun Prosecutions Clarified

HEADLINE: Video Lottery Game Dies In Stalemate Over Budget

BYLINE: By RICHARD PEREZ-PENA 

DATELINE: ALBANY, March 30

 BODY:
As bickering over the state's budget stalemate grew more heated, state lawmakers today insured that Quick Draw, the video lottery game introduced less than four years ago, would come to an abrupt end Wednesday night.
The Senate and Assembly approved a set of emergency bills to keep the state running for two weeks after the April 1 start of the fiscal year, but not before Gov. George E. Pataki and legislative leaders had flirted with a partial government shutdown over the issue of building a luge run and two ski lifts in the Adirondacks for winter Goodwill Games next year. The dispute -- and the brinkmanship involved -- may have said less about the condition of the state's ski resorts than about the relationship between the Republican Governor and the Democrat Assembly Speaker, Sheldon Silver. 
 In the end, Mr. Pataki blinked, taking the money for the resorts out of the emergency bills, but he tore into Mr. Silver in unusually harsh terms, accusing him of jeopardizing the Goodwill Games. "You are telling all of upstate New York you don't care about their economic status," he said. "You are essentially telling them to drop dead."
Mr. Silver and other Democrats countered that the Governor had been manufacturing a crisis to make them look bad, and to win legislation that otherwise would not pass through the threat of a government shutdown.
The first victim of the confrontation was Quick Draw. In 1995, at Mr. Pataki's insistence, the keno-style video game was introduced in New York over the vehement protests of anti-gambling groups and the reservations of the Assembly Democrats. But the law that created it expires Wednesday at midnight.
This year, Mr. Pataki proposed not only extending the game, but making it available in more places, which would increase the $120 million a year in profit it generates for the state. As recently as last week, he said that the emergency spending bills he would send to the Legislature would include a provision keeping the game alive.
But today, the Governor, seeing that neither house of the Legislature had any enthusiasm for the game, sent emergency bills that did not include it, guaranteeing that it would expire Wednesday. It could be resurrected in an eventual budget deal, but lawmakers in both parties called that unlikely. Mr. Pataki insisted today that he did not care whether the game died, despite his earlier support for it.
Scott Wexler, executive director of the Empire State Restaurant and Tavern Owners Association, said the demise of Quick Draw could hurt bars, restaurants, bowling alleys and other establishments where the game is played.
"We're obviously disappointed," Mr. Wexler told The Associated Press. "It will be a loss of revenue to small-business owners, not just in terms of the commission they lose, but also the 10 to 15 percent increase in food and beverage business they have when the game is being run."
In past years, when the budget has been late, Mr. Pataki has routinely attempted to attach items he wanted to the emergency bills, a move that the Legislature has seen as a power grab and has usually beaten back. Mr. Silver accused the Governor today of trying to win his budget battle that way, piece by piece, by calling things like Quick Draw and the ski resort money emergencies.
"The Governor said this is an emergency," the Speaker said, "and I said, 'So is money for the pre-K classes, so is money for reducing class size,' " referring to education programs Mr. Pataki has proposed cutting.
In January, Lake Placid was awarded the first-ever winter Goodwill Games, to be held next February. Mr. Pataki said today that contracts for a new luge run at Lake Placid, and for new gondola lifts at Whiteface Mountain and Gore Mountain had to be awarded by the first week of April to insure that they would be completed in time for the games.
The games, Mr. Pataki said, would be an economic boon to the region, and he said Mr. Silver was putting it at risk. The Governor had planned to add the $10 million cost of the projects to the emergency spending bills he sent to the Legislature today, but at Mr. Silver's insistence, he sent it as a separate bill, rather than risking a government shutdown over the issue.
Mr. Silver and other Democrats emphasized that they have no objection to the projects. But they assert that if the emergency is real, the Governor has ample power to pay for the work from funds he controls, and some members of the Senate's Republican majority agree. And, along with environmental groups, the Democrats also object to Mr. Pataki's proposal to pay for the construction out of the state's Environmental Protection Fund.

HEADLINE: PUBLIC LIVES;
Honoring a Museum's Very Loyal Member

BYLINE: By ELISABETH BUMILLER 

 BODY:
IN 1924, back in the days when the Metropolitan Museum of Art was a sleepy behemoth with grandees like Payne Whitney and Elihu Root on the board, a young woman studying for a masters' degree at Columbia University eagerly gave $10 to join the museum as a member. Today, 75 years later, Luise K. Addiss is still a member -- the only person, the Met says, who has been on the roster that long.
"Unusual, to say the least," said Mrs. Addiss, who will be 100 years old on June 21. 
 Philippe de Montebello, the Met's director, has sent Mrs. Addiss flowers, a handwritten note and an open invitation to tour the museum in her wheelchair and have lunch when the Met is closed on Mondays.
"That sounds very exciting," Mrs. Addiss said.
Her opinion on being old?
"I think it's dull," she said.
The Met, of course, thought that record-breaking loyalty was worth making a fuss over, and it seemed that Mrs. Addiss, nearing 100, might have good stories to tell, or at least amusing opinions on whether museums are good for your health.
Still, while loyalty might endure, one's energy is bound to flag a bit over 99 years. Mrs. Addiss was sound asleep on a recent Monday for a scheduled 9 A.M. interview at her Gramercy Park apartment. "She was so excited about the interview that she didn't sleep last night," said Gail Addiss, Mrs. Addiss's daughter-in-law.
So while Mrs. Addiss napped in the living room in her wheelchair, Gail and her husband, James, the second of Mrs. Addiss's four children, sat by her side and told stories about her. Penelope Yungblut, her youngest child, was there for a time, too.
In her younger days, she wore clothes designed by Hattie Carnegie, gave lots of parties and ran a well-ordered household.
Over the living room fireplace was an oil painting of Mrs. Addiss from another time, in a yellow evening dress, with her dark hair pulled off her face, as if she were on her way to the opera. "She always felt that was glamorized," Ms. Yungblut said.
The portrait was painted when Mrs. Addiss was in her early 30's -- a time when she was working full-time as a home economist at what is now the Community Service Society of New York. Back then The Brooklyn Eagle called Mrs. Addiss a pioneer for going into the Depression-era homes of the poor and teaching them about nutrition.
She had arrived in New York in 1922 from Akron, Ohio, where her father, an Austrian immigrant, ran a plumbing business. Luise Addiss was the first girl in her family to go to college.
IN New York, Mrs. Addiss married (at 29 to Edward F. Addiss, an executive with Burlington Industries), stayed at the Community Service Society for 40 years, retired at the age of 65, studied painting, then worked for another 10 years as a consultant to the Head Start program, which was just beginning. In 1963, she wrote an article for the professional journal Children, saying there was an urgent need for day care for America's working women.
"I wonder if she thought she was ahead of her time?" Mr. Addiss said.
At the age of 70, Mrs. Addiss started making modern silver jewelry -- "It wasn't this quaint, little old lady stuff," her daughter-in-law said. At 75 she went to the United Nations and became chairwoman of a committee that worked to improve the lives of African women. She finally retired at 85. At 89, a leg was amputated because of arteriosclerosis, but she lived on her own until four years ago.
Her secrets? Exercise and mental stimulation. "She walked, and she did exercises, even in the elevator at the old Lord & Taylor," Ms. Yungblut said. "There would be bars around, and she would lift herself up with her arms."
On Monday, a reporter returned for another interview with Mrs. Addiss, this time over lunch.
"We've been having long conversations the last couple of days," said Stephen Addiss, who is child No. 3. "They meander here and there, but they're interesting."
Mrs. Addiss sometimes speaks now in a short, clipped kind of code. But asked what her strongest memories were -- her work, her late husband or her children, Mrs. Addiss was very clear. "Memories of the children," she said. "Never forgetting."
Mrs. Addiss's children say she faced her first big crisis when she lost her leg. "So much of what makes us happy is self-defining," Stephen Addiss said. "She thought of herself as an active person, and when she couldn't do that anymore, it destroyed a lot of the person she thought she was."
Mrs. Addiss had another setback in 1992, when her eldest son, Edward, 62, died of cancer. But three or four years ago, her children say she came to a quiet acceptance of her life.
"Because she's tough," James said. "She didn't give it up."
"It was anger almost," Stephen said. "But she fought. She's ornery."
So now her children, who of course Mrs. Addiss always took to the Met and other museums, do much of her talking, in more ways than one. James is an architectural historian, Stephen is a professor of Japanese art and music at the University of Richmond and Penelope is a psychotherapist and Jungian analyst.
"So maybe being carted away to museums as little tykes had some effect on us," Stephen said. "What do you think, Jim?"

HEADLINE: CRISIS IN THE BALKANS: THE FUTURE;
After Kosovo's 'Cleansing': Can Two Sides Ever Coexist?

BYLINE: By JANE PERLEZ 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
The growing refugee crisis in Kosovo has left Clinton Administration and NATO officials wondering whether they will be able to achieve their goal of a peaceful settlement between Serbs and ethnic Albanians.
Nearly a third of the 1.8 million ethnic Albanians in Kosovo have been forced from their homes by Serbian troops, and many of those refugees have left Kosovo for neighboring Macedonia, Albania or Montenegro. 
 James P. Rubin, State Department spokesman, said today that the strategy of routing ethnic Albanians, under the direction of the Yugoslav President, Slobodan Milosevic, has crystallized the animosity between the two groups. This "radicalization," he said, was making it "all the more difficult to imagine a circumstance where the peoples can begin to live together again."
Mr. Rubin insisted today that the Administration stood by the framework of the Rambouillet accord, which called for Kosovo to stay in Serbia as an autonomous entity.
But NATO officials have already started talking about other outcomes, including talk today that Kosovo could wind up as a separate entity under international protection -- if the allies actually succeed in subduing the Serbian military.
Sketching the outlines of a protectorate, a NATO planner said that NATO troops would oversee the return of ethnic Albanian refugees to Kosovo and guarantee their safety under an arrangement in which the Serbs would have virtually no power.
In London, British officials said the refugees should be allowed to return home. The Defense Minister, George Robertson, said Mr. Milosevic, should not be rewarded for ejecting ethnic Albanians from Kosovo.
"The refugees must be allowed to go home, their homes must be rebuilt," Mr. Robertson said. He and others were not specific about who would pay for rebuilding.
The outflow of refugees, many of them traumatized by the executions they said they had witnessed, could easily destabilize the fragile countries of Macedonia and Albania, as well as the Yugoslav republic of Montenegro.
Given the speed at which the Serbian forces have pushed ethnic Albanians out of their homes, a Balkans specialist in the Administration predicted today that by the end of the week, half the Albanian population could be outside the province and the remainder crouching in the woods.
In his televised speech last Wednesday, outlining the reasons for NATO's intervention in Kosovo, President Clinton said the conflict between the Serbs and the ethnic Albanians had "no natural national boundaries." It was necessary, he said, to intervene to contain its spread.
Displaying a map with bold arrows showing the movement of refugees, Mr. Clinton said, "Already this movement is threatening the young democracy in Macedonia, which has its own Albanian minority and a Turkish minority. Already Serbian forces have made forays into Albania, from which Kosovars have drawn support. Albania has a Greek minority. Let a fire burn here in this area, and the flames will spread."
But since the bombing started, Mr. Milosevic had purposely pushed refugees to Macedonia and Albania in an effort to throw into turmoil the very countries that the bombing was supposed to help protect, a Clinton Administration official said.
About a third of the two million inhabitants of Macedonia are Albanian and a large inflow of ethnic Albanians from Kosovo could tip the balance into a majority Albanian population, the official said. Macedonia has about 40 percent unemployment and a new democracy. Government officials there were appealing today to the European Union to take some of the ethnic Albanian refugees, and foreign ministers from Germany, Austria and Finland said they would meet Thursday near Bonn to discuss the issue.
Albania is the poorest country in Europe, barely able to look after its own people, and certainly not able to care for the tens of thousands of new refugees, the Administration official said.
It was possible, the official said, that NATO troops would have to be sent to Albania as well as Macedonia -- where a 10,000-strong NATO force has been poised as peacekeepers for Kosovo -- to help the two countries control increasing tensions.
"I think we're going to end up with troops in Albania and Macedonia," the official said. "For Macedonia these refugees make big trouble."
Since the bombing began last week, NATO officials said they believed almost 150,000 ethnic Albanian refugees had left the province and gone to Albania. Another 22,500 had fled to Macedonia and 42,500 were now in Montenegro, they said.
Combined with the more than 400,000 that had fled their homes beginning in January of last year when the conflict began, the number of refugees forced either into neighboring lands or left to seek shelter in the countryside had reached about 570,000, Jamie Shea, the NATO spokesman said today. Kosovo has about 2 million people, of which 90 percent are ethnic Albanian, and the remainder Serbian.
Reports from the border at Macedonia today, said that a line of cars and people on foot stretched for six miles inside Kosovo, as people tried to leave.
From inside Kosovo, there were reports of a large gathering of refugees huddled for safety in a valley being shelled by Serbian artillery and of a forced mass exodus along the eastern road out of Pristina, the capital.
The city of Pec, a graceful town of 100,000 was virtually empty and the houses, mosques and stores destroyed, NATO said. With Pristina now a target of ethnic cleansing, there was only one town of any size -- Gnjilane, south east of the capital -- that had not been attacked by the Serbs, officials said.
The stories of the continuing purge of the population were eked out by satellite phone and in some cases landline phone. But most were impossible to verify.
An ethnic Albanian living in Washington, who spoke on condition of anonymity because of fear of reprisals against his family, said he spoke to his parents, and two brothers in Pristina today by telephone.
"They said there's lots of panic because people have fled to Macedonia and those who are left don't know what to do," he said. The villages of Sosali and Matican to the east of the city had been shelled, leaving a line several miles long of people in cars and on foot heading for the border of Macedonia, he said. "My mother is very concerned about my brothers and their kids. How are they going to leave and how are they going to leave together?"

SECTION: Section F; Page 1; Column 2; Dining In, Dining Out/Style Desk 

LENGTH: 1710 words

HEADLINE: Would You Like the Wine List? No, Just a Corkscrew

BYLINE: By ERIC ASIMOV 

 BODY:
I WAS nervous as I approached Patroon, the clubby restaurant known for its fine American food and excellent but expensive wine list. In my bag I had a special bottle of my own, a cabernet sauvignon from Dunn Vineyards that had been aging for a decade. On this night, I hoped, I would drink it with dinner.
That's what made me nervous. Would the sommelier roll his eyes at the rube with his own bottle? Would the hostess sneer at my audacity? Would they tell me to get lost? 
 Nothing of the sort. As the hostess showed us to our table, I showed her my bottle and whispered my request. "Of course," she murmured. A minute later a waiter appeared. "May I take your bottle?" he asked. A sommelier returned with the bottle and decanted it, as was appropriate for this wine. "Where did you ever find this?" he asked appreciatively. Success -- and I didn't have to be embarrassed, or even whisper.
The right, even the necessity, to tote your own wine to dinner is a time-honored practice among many smaller restaurants that are not licensed to sell wine and beer. But the practice also occurs at fancier, more ambitious restaurants that have invested considerable amounts of money in building their wine lists. Restaurants do not encourage the practice, but most allow it, especially, as I found out by experimenting at several restaurants, if you adhere to a certain protocol. This essentially consists of asking permission to bring wine when you make the reservation. And if you encounter resistance, it helps if you can offer a good reason.
The restaurants go along with it mostly to accommodate regular customers and special occasions. They don't advertise the fact because they aren't particularly happy about it under any circumstance. Alcohol, after all, provides them with some of their best profits -- the price is generally twice what you would expect to pay at a retail store, sometimes more. And in many instances, a restaurant's wine service allows it to show off an expertise beyond the expected culinary wizardry.
"It's not thrilling when somebody brings in a bottle of wine because it's one less bottle you're going to sell," said Danny Meyer, whose restaurants include Union Square Cafe, Gramercy Tavern, Eleven Madison Park and Tabla. "We've got a huge amount of money tied up in our wine cellar."
To compensate, most restaurants charge a fee to uncork a bottle of wine brought in by a customer. Depending on the wine you've chosen to bring, the corkage fee may still permit a considerable savings or obliterate any advantage at all. These fees can vary tremendously. At Patroon, the fee was $30. Orinoco, a Venezuelan restaurant on the Upper East Side, charges $10. At Mr. Meyer's restaurants, the charge is $15. At La Caravelle and Windows on the World, it's $20. At Montrachet, it's $35. At the Post House, it's free.
Bringing your own wine is desirable, especially in New York, where so many people do all their entertaining in restaurants. Some people want to drink a special wine on a birthday or anniversary. Others, who have amassed a stock of old and rare bottles, want to bring in wines from their collections that are hard to find in restaurants. And some restaurants have small or limited wine lists to begin with -- Thai and Indian restaurants, for example, rarely offer rieslings or cabernet francs, the kinds of wine that go so well with the food. The bottom line, though, is economic.
Even when a wine list is not inflated beyond reason, the costs can be staggering. Those who think nothing of paying a restaurant $30 for a wine they can buy on the corner for $15 may pause at paying $200 for a wine they can buy for $100 less.
"If you have collected wine over the years, to pay the prices in restaurants is amazing," said Bill Plotch, a bond trader and wine enthusiast, who has brought his own bottles to restaurants for special occasions.
Erika Moore, a communications consultant, and her husband, Terence, belong to a wine-tasting society that occasionally scouts around for restaurants with modest wine lists that charge minimal corkage fees. "It's always been very pleasant," she said. "A lot of our members have incredible cellars. They've bought stuff at auction or 10 or 15 years ago where they spent $80 a case and now it costs much, much more."
Restaurant attitudes toward those bringing in wine vary, ranging from a polite acceptance to outright hostility. As a practical matter, restaurants are free to set their own corkage policies. "No laws regulate or prohibit corkage fees," said Alan Gardner, whose law firm, Verini & Gardner, represents a number of restaurants that do business with the New York State Liquor Authority. "It's strictly up to the individual restaurateur whether to permit you to bring alcohol or what to charge."
Yet even restaurants that strongly discourage the practice find themselves accommodating it occasionally.
"The policy is fairly firm against people bringing their own wine," Daniel Johnnes, the wine director for Montrachet, said. "However, like all things in life, there's room for maneuver. If somebody's celebrating a 50th anniversary, and they bring in a bottle they've been saving, you can be sure we're not going to charge corkage, and we'll serve it with pleasure. However, that's very different from somebody bringing in a case of wine for a tasting and trying to get away from paying for wine."
Mr. Johnnes recalled six businessmen who walked in once with a case of expensive wine, expecting to be accommodated. "They handed us the box, told us which order to serve them in and said, 'We'd like Riedel glasses,' " he said. "Of course we refused. You have to have some courtesy."
Etiquette is crucial when bringing your own wine. It begins with a phone call, simply to smooth the way.
"If you walk into a restaurant cold with a bottle of wine in your hand and say, 'Pour this,' somebody's going to get a little uptight," said Alan Stillman, the chief executive of the New York Restaurant Group, which includes the Post House, Smith & Wollensky, Cite and Park Avenue Cafe. He does not charge a corkage fee at his restaurants, he says, because the only people who bring their own wines are regular customers who do so only on special occasions. "When it happens, we say there must be a reason for it," he said. "I wouldn't be happy if somebody came in with wine once every three weeks."
Most consumers understand that politeness counts. Mr. Plotch, the bond trader, says that he rarely brings wines to restaurants, and only to those where he's developed a relationship with the owner. "I wouldn't feel comfortable unless over the years I'd spent a lot of money in a restaurant," he said. "I think it's rude otherwise." He also recognizes the value of sharing the wealth. "If I do bring in a really great bottle, I'll always offer the owner or the one who's taking care of me a glass," he said.
A quick phone call might have saved embarrassment one recent evening when I tried to bring in a bottle of Jean-Louis Chave Hermitage 1994 to Peter Luger Steak House, where the hamburger-class wine list does not do justice to the restaurant's legendary steaks. I was politely but firmly rebuffed by the maitre d'hotel, and no amount of diplomacy could change his mind.
"We don't do that," the maitre d'hotel said. "I've even turned down Henry Kissinger."
Peter Luger's treasurer, Amy Rubenstein, said the policy makes things simpler for the restaurant. "It's just easier for us," she said. "We don't charge excessively, we don't have large markups, but we're in business to sell wine, and that's what we do."
Even if you've received permission from a restaurant to bring that special wine, the hand that pours the bottle for you will still be outstretched. "The waiter's salary has to do with what we make on wine," Mr. Meyer said, "so generally people are more generous with the gratuity." As a rule, Mr. Johnnes of Montrachet recommends tipping as if you had bought the wine at the restaurant.
While restaurateurs dread the B.Y.O. Bordeaux crowd, the actual number of people bringing in their wines is small. "Our restaurant has been open about six months, and I can count on my fingers the number of people who have brought bottles," Steven Eckler, the general manager at Eleven Madison Park, said.
Mr. Eckler, the former general manager of Postrio in San Francisco, says this small number is in direct contrast to his experience there. "On the West Coast, I would see two to three times as many people," he said. "It's a different restaurant culture. People don't feel the same hesitancy. They weren't embarrassed about bringing it in. As a restaurant, you got used to it."
Larry Stone, the wine director and a co-owner of Rubicon in San Francisco, says the different attitude stems from the area's proximity to wine country. "Winery owners entertain a lot and have to bring their own wines, and that became the basis for it," he said. "Seattle, too, is fairly tolerant, but Southern California is different."
Mr. Stone says that the practice was so common that many area restaurants have not only abandoned hope of resistance but have completely capitulated, instead accepting the profits they make on corkage fees, which Mr. Stone said range from $8 to $20.
"A lot of restaurants don't want to stock wines because people bring their own anyway," Mr. Stone said. "They've just given up on creating a wine list. You just don't see anything older. You don't find the kind of collections that you might on the East Coast."
He estimated that when Rubicon opened in 1994, 20 to 25 percent of the customers on some nights paid corkage fees. The restaurant managed to reduce the figure to 3 percent by emphasizing rare wines at reasonable prices, he said. But that victory comes at a cost of smaller profits on wine, a greater dedication to training the staff about wine and an effort at ferreting out unusual bottles. And still, the restaurant lives in fear of customers bearing bottles.
"Four people bringing in 10 different bottles of wine," Mr. Stone envisioned, practically shuddering. "That's the nightmare part of this. That's 40 glasses. It takes up a lot of time for the server, they have to be washed and scrubbed, and they don't tip!"

SECTION: Section E; Page 1; Column 3; The Arts/Cultural Desk

HEADLINE: ARTS ABROAD;
Swapping Cha-Chas and Rap in Cuba

BYLINE: By PETER WATROUS 

DATELINE: HAVANA, March 29

 BODY:
It was in the air: the thrill of the illicit and the new, the excitement of discovery and the charge of an encounter with idols. And it didn't take place on a baseball field.
Just after the Baltimore Orioles broke ground by playing a Cuban team here, musicians by the score and fans by the thousands poured into the Karl Marx Theater on Sunday night in this city's elegant Playa district for the Music Bridges concert, the biggest convergence of foreign and Cuban musicians since the revolution. Before that came a week of impromptu collaborations outdoors and in makeshift studios. 
 From the United States came singers like Bonnie Raitt, Jimmy Buffett, Beth Nielsen Chapman, Joan Osborne, Lisa Loeb, Burt Bacharach and more. The Cubans brought together musicians from the folk, rock and popular dance music scenes. In the end the all-star concert at the Karl Marx flickered and droned, the lead-up concerts had people dancing and cheering, and the future of this enterprise, musical or otherwise, seemed as hazy and unpredictable as anything else involving Cuban-American relations.
Music Bridges, an organization that specializes in concerts in politically sensitive countries, had spent a year and a half pulling together the legal and practical permission from the United States and Cuba.
Music Bridges, with its 40-some participating American musicians, had come to Havana and taken more than 120 rooms in the Hotel Nacional, overlooking the ocean. The organization filled the sixth-floor executive offices and the presidential suite, along with three rooms that had been converted into recording studios. For a week Cuban and American musicians were to collaborate on songs and to present the works on the final night in a long concert at the Karl Marx Theater.
At events around the city American musicians were getting a feel for the power of Cuban music and its bacchanalian spirit. In the wee hours of Thursday night at La Cecilia nightclub, Michael Franti, a rapper and band leader from San Francisco, mounted the stage with Cuba's version of the Rolling Stones, Los Van Van, a 30-year-old institution playing hard-rocking dance music. Mr. Franti started rapping and repeating a phrase, and Pedrito, one of the group's singers, entered the fray; the two swapped nonsense syllables, but with rhythm. It would have brought down the roof, but La Cecilia is outdoors. Members of the audience -- made up of faranduleros, the habitues of Havana's night life scene, Music Bridges staff members and performers, and journalists -- were impressed and they danced.
On Saturday at the Charlie Chaplin Theater in the Vedado district, Americans and what remains of members of Cuba's intellectual culture had the chance to hear the Buena Vista Social Club play cha-cha-chas, mambos and boleros that have mostly fallen into disregard. The performance, which was not part of Music Bridges, offered an opportunity to hear music with extreme grace and humor, one of the great musical syntheses of the New World, where classical, jazz and African music merged into something special. Played by old, weary and worn musicians, the music reflected the prerevolution grandeur of Cuban society, as does the best architecture of the city, also worn, weary and prerevolutionary.
The American producer of the Buena Vista band, Ry Cooder, who was in Cuba for a screening of a new documentary about the group directed by Wim Wenders, said of the music: "I've never seen any scene explode as quickly as this. That's good, and that's bad."
Regardless, it was one of the greatest windfalls of positive publicity the Cuban Government has had in its post-Batista history. Though the economic embargo of Cuba, the Helms-Burton Act, leaves a loophole for cultural exchange, nothing of this size had been attempted before. The Cuban side, which has everything to gain from such exchanges, regularly proclaimed its happiness with events like this one; the American side was not so sure.
Though many of the American musicians had barely a clue about either Cuban music or Cuban-American relations, the predominant view seemed to be that antagonistic relationships between the two countries did little for Cubans or Americans. Ms. Raitt, who came fully informed and loaded for bear, brought 37 guitars to give away, and a pile of medicine.
"Everybody knows that the embargo the American Government has placed on Cuba just hurts the Cuban people," Ms. Raitt said. "So I went to my guitar company and bought a bunch of guitars at cost, and I'm going to give lots away to the music school here, and the rest to people I know."
In the last year Cuba has cracked down on the casual prostitution prevalent on the streets and in the dance clubs. Where clubs with live music were once almost too numerous to count, now nearly all have been closed by the Government. One result is that the best bands no longer play regularly, and one of the world's great music scenes is moribund.
Instead Havana seems to be undergoing renovation for tourists, and Old Havana is flooded with new hotels and stores that suggest that the economy is doing well. Unfortunately the average Cuban is not allowed to enter the hotels and has little or no access to the dollar economy that has sprung up alongside the peso. In a seeming effort to insure that the city is safe for tourism and to reduce crime, the police, on nearly every corner, were stopping Cubans and checking identification. Some residents associated with the music exchange said that many Cubans from outside Havana were being shipped to jail or back home.
While generally unaware of the politics, American visitors were stunned by the skills of their Cuban counterparts. Some musicians stayed at the Nacional and worked with their Cuban partners. Others, like N'Dea Davenport, the former lead singer from the British group the Brand New Heavies, stayed and worked in Old Havana with her writing partners.
"I love it here regardless of the hardships," said Ms. Davenport, who now lives in New Orleans. "The reason I love New Orleans is that there's music everywhere, and it's the same here. Everybody sings or dances or plays an instrument. It's changing my life."
The average Cuban musician, taught from age 7 in Government-run conservatories, is among the best trained and musically literate in the world, schooled in classical music, pop and jazz. While the American musicians were discovering the riches of Cuban music, the Cubans were learning plenty from the Americans.
Like most of their countrymen, Cuban musicians receive only bits and pieces of information from the outside world. They look to the United States for musical cues but get music from the most powerful stations in Miami or New Orleans, which guarantees a view of a music world heavy on Kenny G and the worst pop. Other information comes from furtive conversations or secondhand accounts of people who have seen bands in Europe. And maybe they will get the occasional recording.
"The most amazing thing about this encounter is the ability to hang out with our idols, to learn what's behind the music that we've heard all our lives," said Luis de la Cruz, guitarist, composer and leader of the band Bolsa Negra (Black Market). "It's one thing to hear a bit of music on the radio, but to actually sit down with somebody we admire and ask questions and collaborate on something intimate like the writing process is really incredible."
Carlos Varela, one of Cuba's most important singer-songwriters, collaborated with the Indigo Girls. He has played in the United States a few times and has flirted with major record labels. But Cuba's isolation, apart from the infatuation with the island internationally, almost guarantees the failure of an international career. For him the weeklong stay was important for another reason.
"When I go to the United States the next time, I have a hundred telephone numbers of musicians to call, new friends I've made," he said. "People want me to come to collaborate, to write and record with them. For that reason alone this is invaluable."
Chucho Valdes, the dean of Cuban musicians, pianist and leader of the group Irakere, thought the encounter was going to leave a permanent impression on the Cuban music scene.
"I've never seen anything like this," said Mr. Valdes, who is also the director of the nearly annual Havana Jazz Festival, which usually attracts a handful of American musicians. "The things that we lack, a formal sophistication in pop music and harmonic sense, we're learning right now. And the way to produce sound, to get a good sound."
Still, the final Music Bridges concert was pretty much an artistic disaster, unlike the weeklong exchange. Generally American pop musicianship is, to be kind, basic. The Cubans were often reduced to being the professional hired hands backing the illiterate boss. Most songs were in English, with Americans leading the bands. The Americans were mostly white, mostly folk-oriented and mostly second rate. It is an old history. The recordings of the concert, and the music made at the Hotel National may be released, depending on legal restrictions imposed by the United States.
There were a few good moments. Ms. Davenport tore the place apart with a song she and the Cuban singer Rene Banos sang called "Que Importa," in which they listed the differences between them, including their beer-brand preferences. Ms. Osborne, backed by Manolito y Su Trabuco, sang a deep and funny blues that had the audience on its feet. There were some mildly political songs -- Ms. Raitt sang "Cuba Is Way Too Cool" -- but most performers stuck to "We Are the Worldisms." (One tune was called "One World.")
But most people, though the musicians had worked hard on the songs, weren't really looking for an end. The means, in this case, was sufficient.

LENGTH: 790 words

HEADLINE: U.S. Data May Understate Productivity Gains

BYLINE: By LOUIS UCHITELLE 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, March 30

 BODY:
After years of maintaining that its statistics on the labor productivity growth rate were generally reliable, the Government agency that produces the numbers now says they may be somewhat understated.
In research to be published on Wednesday, the Labor Department's Bureau of Labor Statistics says that the current economic prosperity, and rising profits "do not seem fully consistent with slow productivity growth." The new studies focused on four industries -- banking, insurance, construction and health services -- that seem robust but have registered no improvement in official productivity statistics in recent years. 
 Edwin R. Dean, chief of the bureau's productivity division, wrote in one of three articles that will appear in the Labor Department's Monthly Labor Review that the published data do not appear to understate productivity gains "by a large order of magnitude." But he said there appeared to have been at least some underestimate, mostly because of measurement problems in the vast service sector.
The unexpected expression of doubt from the Bureau of Labor Statistics gives a fresh twist to perhaps the most important economic debate in the United States today: whether corporate America has become sufficiently efficient to keep the current economic boom going without having to raise prices or sacrifice profits.
National well-being depends on productivity, which measures how much a worker produces in a given hour. Corporate revenue rises when workers produce more in that hour than they did before. A factory worker makes more bottle caps, for example, or a telephone operator handles more calls, or an air traffic controller more flights. The additional revenue can then be divided up as extra profit or higher wages or both. That feeds into spending and investment, and more jobs, and the economy flourishes.
The productivity growth rate rose smartly until 1973 and then the statistics registered a sharp decline that has persisted since, except for temporary upticks during boom periods, including the current boom, which started in late 1995. While some economists contend that the official productivity numbers are accurate, others insist that they have failed to capture the improvements made possible by computers and other technologies.
Alan Greenspan, chairman of the Federal Reserve, has repeatedly argued that the national economy once again enjoys a productivity growth rate reminiscent of the quarter-century after World War II. And now the Bureau of Labor Statistics, for the first time, has publicly expressed doubt about its own figures.
"We have had careful discussions with prominent economists in recent years," Mr. Dean said in a telephone interview, "and we have felt under an obligation to conduct probing studies of this issue and these studies have now been completed."
The bureau's studies fail to address an issue considered crucial by some economists -- whether the findings in three or four industries can be generalized to the economy as a whole. Underestimating productivity in some industries may be offset by overestimating productivity in others, said Barry Bosworth, a senior fellow at the Brookings Institution.
"We don't know if we are dealing with an allocation problem," he said, "or a change in total national productivity."
The bureau's new studies examined not only labor productivity but also a broader measure, known as multifactor productivity, which measures the output or dollar value of the production that results not only from a worker's efforts but also from other inputs, such as the computers and machinery that workers use. In both cases, the bureau found that the biggest problem lay in accurately measuring output. Often it has to be roughly estimated.
The bureau's studies also cited difficulties in adjusting output for inflation, and in defining in the service sector what constitutes input and output in the first place.
"In the production of services," Mr. Dean wrote in an article that summarized the other findings, "the consumer often supplies an essential input. For example, it may not be possible to define medical output adequately without considering whether the patient follows the doctor's advice, or ignores it. Similar issues arise in the fields of education and entertainment. For example, the output of a jazz band may not be well defined without considering whether the audience was 1,000 people, 10 people, or no one at all."
Finally, the bureau's studies covered the last 20 years, too short a time to determine whether the productivity growth rate was similarly underestimated in its heyday before 1973. If so, then the sharp decline in productivity growth after that year would be roughly unchanged.

 
http://www.nytimes.com

PUB-DATE: March 31, 1999

SECTION: Section B; Page 7; Column 1; National Desk; Education Page

LENGTH: 951 words

HEADLINE: Graduate Teaching Assistants, Increasingly Burdened, Are Unionizing

BYLINE: By ANDREA ADELSON 

 BODY:
When Elizabeth K. Shermer started the five-year process of earning a doctoral degree in medieval English from the University of California at Berkeley, her teaching responsibilities began almost immediately. Without any tutoring on how to teach, she was asked to lead a discussion group on Chaucer the next day.
"It's like an entry-level position, but you're thrown in up to your neck," said Ms. Sherman, 28, who makes $1,300 a month for 20 hours a week teaching freshman composition; at the same time, she is finishing her thesis. "It was scary. At least I knew the material, but I felt like I needed a lot more help." 
 Students like Ms. Shermer take on more than half the teaching load for undergraduates at the University of California and at the nation's other major public research institutions, a shift that in the last 20 years has doubled the percentage of teachers not on the full-time faculty. A study by the graduate student teachers' union at Yale University, released on Monday, concluded that part-time instructors and graduate students taught 70 percent of Yale's classes.
Graduate teaching assistants give lectures, hold discussion groups, conduct labs and grade papers. Such reliance on part-time instructors is a much-debated topic in academe and is fostering a climate of labor militancy on campuses nationwide.
Dissatisfaction among teaching assistants has led to union organizing at 18 public-school campuses, including the City University of New York and the University of Michigan. In a decision expected to help organizing efforts elsewhere, last week the Public Employment Relations Board in California ordered the University of California to allow union elections to take place at seven campuses by the school year's end. Earlier in the week, teaching assistants at the University of California at Los Angeles had overwhelmingly voted to join a United Auto Workers affiliate. University officials had previously refused to recognize a union.
"This is the prize they're after," said Judith S. Craig, an associate dean at the University of Wisconsin, where the first union of teaching assistants was formed in 1969.
For 16 years, California's premier public university has fought unionization by its 9,000 graduate students, two-thirds of whom have joined, union officials say. Support is strongest among students in humanities and social science. During the paper and exam crunch at the end of the semester in December, graduate students walked off the job in the first systemwide strike. The action brought pressure on university officials from state legislative leaders, who urged a settlement and union recognition. The new Governor, Gray Davis, was already on record supporting the union.
In late December, the employment relations board determined that teaching assistants should be considered employees and not students, and had the right to unionize. The board rejected an appeal last month.
Members of the newly created U.C.L.A. union, the Student Association of Graduate Employees, are most concerned about declining health benefits, the lack of an effective grievance procedure, workload distribution and enrollment fees that are not fully forgiven, said Constance M. Razza, a union spokeswoman.
Albert Carnesale, U.C.L.A.'s chancellor, who helped the United States negotiate a missile treaty with the Soviet Union, said negotiations over graduate students' concerns were likely to be delicate.
"The difficulty will come in being able to separate academic from workplace issues," Mr. Carnesale said. "It's not a trivial undertaking because the line is not a bright one."
Ms. Craig, a member of the Wisconsin bargaining team, who wrote a dissertation on graduate student unions, said a union benefited the university by forcing faculty members to improve their supervision and evaluation of teaching assistants. It also meant that fair treatment did not rest on the good will of the faculty.
Among the drawbacks, she said, is an adversarial environment. Another is that a student-run collective-bargaining group is transient. A result is a constant redefinition of the union's aims over economic and academic issues. A collective-bargaining contract also takes away a department's ability to make hiring or workload exceptions when necessary, Ms. Craig said.
Unions have failed to raise most graduate-student salaries, said Cary Nelson, an English professor at the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign. Pay that amounts to less than the minimum wage is not unusual. "I find those salaries all the time," Professor Nelson said, citing the $1,125 the University of Nebraska at Kearney pays a graduate student to teach a three-month class meeting three times a week. He figures that amounts to $3.75 an hour.
Professor Nelson is drafting a bill of rights for graduate students. Its provisions include pay equal to 30 percent of median family income for a full year. He will urge its adoption in a June meeting of the American Association of University Professors, which has 44,000 members.
The Modern Language Association, the first academic group to help graduate students and part-time instructors establish their claim statistically, plans to survey working conditions in English and language departments at all of the country's 3,500 colleges this fall.
"We hope historians and economists will take similar actions to reform their own workplaces," Professor Nelson said.
